Essential Accidents: Three Modes of Dependence on the Essence
Al-Dasuqi defines essential accidents and classifies their dependence on the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three ways an accident can depend on the essence: directly, via a part, or via an equal cause.

Core Idea
Essential accidents derive from the essence itself, whether directly, through a part, or through a coextensive cause.

In this passage, al-Dasuqi explains why certain accidents are called 'essential'—they depend on the essence (dhāt) of the subject. He then reveals that this dependence is not uniform; it occurs in three distinct degrees. Understanding these types is crucial for recognizing which predicates truly belong to a subject in demonstrative sciences like kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of essential accident
Find the sentence that says 'these accidents are called essential only because they depend on the essence.' This establishes that the defining property is istinād li-l-dhāt.
2
Locate the three types of dependence
The passage lists three divisions: the first is direct (ẓāhir), the second is via a part (al-juzʾ), and the third is via an equal cause (amr musāwin). Underline them in the Arabic.
3
Analyze each type
Direct: the accident attaches without any intermediary (e.g., wonder for man? but the example is unclear). Via a part: the accident attaches to a part of the essence, so indirectly to the essence. Via an equal cause: the accident attaches via something coextensive with the essence, which itself depends on the essence.
The third type is the most subtle; it involves a middle term that is equal to the essence.
4
Map examples to types
Check which examples in the passage correspond to each type. For instance, 'wonder is coextensive with man' may illustrate the third type. 'Occupying space' for body could be direct. The passage does not give a clear example for the second type, but it can be illustrated with a fiqh example: 'being legally capable' for a person depends on the part 'intellect'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتَّعجُبُ مُساوِ لِلإنسان، وإنَّما سُمِّيَت هذهِ الأعراضُ ذاتيَّةٌ؛ لاستنادِهَا لِلذَّات، وإنْ تفاوت الاستنادُ لِلذَّاتِ في القوَّةِ، أمَّا الاستنادُ لِلذَّاتِ في القسم الأوَّلِ؛ فظاهرُ، وأمَّا في الثَّاني؛ فَلِأنَّ العارض مُستند للجزء، والجزء داخل في الذَّاتِ، فيكونُ مستنداً إلى ما في الذَّاتِ والمستند لِمَا فى الذَّاتِ مُستندٌ لِلذَّات . العطار محمولات المسائلِ الأُخر، شَامِلٌ لِجميع أفرادِ موضوعِ العِلْمِ، فيكونُ عَرَضاً ذاتيا له، مثال شمولِ العَرَضِ الذَّاتي على سبيل الانفراد : كُلُّ متحير، فإنَّ التَّحيز وَحدَهُ شَامِلٌ لِجَميعِ أفرادِ الجِسْمِ، بدونِ أنْ يعتبر معهُ مقابلُهُ أو شمولهُ معَ مُقابِلِهِ، بمعنى أنَّهُ إذا لُوحِةً وحدَهُ لا يَكونُ شَامِلاً ، فإنْ لُوحِظَ مع مُقابِلِهِ تحقَّقَ الشُّمولُ؛ سواء كانَ التَّقابل بينَهُ وبينَ ذلك المقابل تقابلَ الضَّدَّيْنِ، أَو تَقَابُلَ العَدَمِ والمَلَكَةِ، مثالُ العَرَضِ الذَّاتيِّ الشَّامِلِ على سبيلِ التَّقابُلِ قولُنَا : كُلُّ خَطِّ إِمَّا مُنحَنِ وَإِمَّا مُستقيم، فالتَّقابُلُ بينَ الاستقامة والانحناء تقابلُ التَّضاد، ولا شَكٌّ أَنَّ مَجموع الأمرَيْنِ عَرَضٌ شامل لجميع أفرادِ الخطوط، وأما الاستقامة وحدها أو الانحناء وحدَهُ ؛ فَلَا، وَمِثَالُ العَرَضِ الذَاتيّ الشَّامل على سبيل تقابل العدم والملكة : العدد إمَّا زَوج أو فرد، فالتقابل بين الفرديَّةِ والزَّوجيَّةِ تَقابل العدم والمَلَكَةِ، ولا شَكٍّ في شمولِ العَرَضِ الذَّاتيّ في هَذَينِ المثالينِ لِلمَوضوع؛ مع اعتبارِ التَّقابل لا أحدهما فقط، وأمَّا التَّقابل على طريقِ السَّلْبِ والإيجابِ؛ فَغَيرُ مُعتبرٍ لِمَا قالَ؛ إذ المتقابلان تقابل الإيجاب والسَّلْبِ لا اختصاص لهما بمفهوم دون مفهوم؛ لُنَا : الجوهرُ إمَّا مُمكن أوْ لا ؛ ليس فيه شمول ؛ لأنَّ كُلًّا مِنَ الإمكانِ وسَلبِهِ لا يختصَّان بالجوهر، إذ يجريان في العَرَضِ أيضاً، والحاصل أنَّنا نعتبر في المتقابلين على الوجه المذكورِ أنْ يكونَ كُلَّ واحدٍ مِنهُما مَحمُولاً مع ما يقابلهُ إذا أخذ على وجهِ التَّرديدِ كالأمثلة المذكورة، وأَمَّا فِي الثَّالِثِ؛ فَلانَّ العارض اللاحق بواسطة أمرٍ مساو؛ مستند لذلك الأمر المساوي، والمساوي مُستند لِلذَّاتِ، والمستند إلى المستند إلى شيء؛ مستند لذلك الشَّيء.
Wonder is coextensive with man, and these accidents are called essential only because they depend on the essence, although the dependence on the essence varies in strength. As for the dependence on the essence in the first division, it is apparent. As for the second, it is because the accident depends on the part, and the part is within the essence, so it depends on what is in the essence, and what depends on what is in the essence depends on the essence. Al-Attar: The predicates of the other sciences are comprehensive of all individuals of the subject of the science, so it is an essential accident for it. An example of the comprehensiveness of the essential accident individually: Every body is occupying space, for occupying space alone is comprehensive of all individuals of body, without considering its opposite or its comprehensiveness with its opposite, meaning that if it is considered alone it is not comprehensive, but if considered with its opposite, comprehensiveness is realized. Whether the opposition between it and that opposite is opposition of contraries or opposition of privation and possession. An example of the essential accident comprehensive through opposition is our statement: Every line is either curved or straight. The opposition between straightness and curvature is opposition of contraries, and there is no doubt that the sum of the two is an accident comprehensive of all individuals of lines. As for straightness alone or curvature alone, it is not. And an example of the essential accident comprehensive through opposition of privation and possession is: Every number is either even or odd. The opposition between oddness and evenness is opposition of privation and possession, and there is no doubt about the comprehensiveness of the essential accident in these two examples for the subject, with consideration of the opposition, not just one of them. As for opposition in the way of negation and affirmation, it is not considered for what he said; for the two opposites in negation and affirmation have no specificity for one concept without another. For us: Every substance is either possible or not possible; there is no comprehensiveness in it, because each of possibility and its negation is not specific to substance, since they occur in accident as well. The conclusion is that we consider in the two opposites in the mentioned manner that each one of them is predicated with its opposite when taken in the manner of disjunction, like the mentioned examples. And as for the third, it is because the accident that attaches by means of an equal cause depends on that equal cause, and the equal depends on the essence, and what depends on what depends on something depends on that thing.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage from al-Dasuqi and al-Attar defines essential accidents (al-a‘rāḍ al-dhātiyya) and explains their types. The discussion begins with the reason these accidents are called essential: they depend on the essence (dhāt) itself, though the strength of dependence varies. The author then divides this dependence into three types. The first type is direct dependence: the accident attaches to the essence without intermediary, like wonder attaching to man? (the example is unclear). The second type is dependence through a part: the accident attaches to a part of the essence, and because the part is within the essence, it attaches indirectly to the essence. The third type is dependence through an equal cause: the accident attaches via something coextensive with the essence, and that coextensive depends on the essence, so the accident ultimately depends on the essence. Following this, al-Attar explains how essential accidents can be comprehensive of all individuals of a subject. He describes two ways: individually (an individual accident like occupying space applying to every body) and through opposition (a pair of opposite accidents that together cover all individuals, like straightness and curvature for lines, or evenness and oddness for numbers). He contrasts these with opposition of negation and affirmation, which is not specific to a given subject and thus not considered comprehensive. This is important for understanding how essential accidents serve as predicates in demonstrative sciences. The third type of dependence is then reiterated, completing the classification.

Key Terms
العَرَض الذاتي
الاستناد للذات
القسم الأوّل (direct dependence)
القسم الثاني (dependence via a part)
القسم الثالث (dependence via an equal cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following describes the dependence of an essential accident through an equal cause?
A) The accident attaches directly to the essence without any intermediary.
B) The accident attaches to a part of the essence, and the part is in the essence.
C) The accident attaches via something that is coextensive with the essence, and that thing depends on the essence.
D) The accident attaches through negation and affirmation of the essence.

Key Takeaway
An essential accident is defined by its dependence on the essence, which occurs in three distinct ways: directly, via a part, or via an equal cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of 'valid contract' in fiqh: 'An expression connecting two offer and acceptance, legally binding.' Identify which of its essential accidents (e.g., legally binding effect) depend directly on the essence, which depend via a part (e.g., offer depends on the part 'declaration'), and which depend via an equal cause (e.g., consideration is coextensive with contract?). Write a brief justification for each.

How Essential Accidents Cover Their Subject: Individual vs. Oppositional Comprehensiveness
Al-Dasuqi defines essential accidents and classifies their dependence on the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two ways an essential accident can be comprehensive of all individuals of its subject: individually or through a pair of opposites, and explain why negation/affirmation is excluded.

Core Idea
Essential accidents cover their subject either alone or as a pair of opposites, but not by simple negation/affirmation.

Al-Attar extends the discussion of essential accidents to explain how they can be 'comprehensive' (shāmil) of all individuals of their subject. He presents two modes: some essential accidents cover all individuals by themselves (like occupying space for body), while others do so only as a pair of opposites (like curved/straight for lines). He also warns against confusing this with the opposition of negation and affirmation, which is not specific to a subject. This precision is vital for defining the scope of a science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modes of comprehensiveness
The passage describes comprehensiveness 'on the way of individuality' (ʿalā sabīl al-infirād) and 'on the way of opposition' (ʿalā sabīl al-taqābul). Mark where these are introduced.
2
Analyze the example of individual comprehensiveness
The example 'Every body is occupying space' (kullu mutaḥayyiz) shows that one predicate alone covers all bodies. The opposite (e.g., non-occupying) is not needed.
Note the text says the opposite is not considered here.
3
Analyze the examples of oppositional comprehensiveness
Two sub-types: opposition of contraries (taqābul al-taḍād) – 'Every line is either curved or straight' – and opposition of privation and possession (taqābul al-ʿadam wa al-malaka) – 'Every number is either even or odd.' Notice that neither predicate alone covers all; only the pair does.
4
Understand why negation/affirmation is excluded
The passage gives the example 'Every substance is either possible or not possible' and explains that possibility and its negation are not specific to substance – they apply to accidents too. Thus, this type of opposition does not yield an essential accident that is coextensive with the subject.
The key is specificity (ikhtiṣāṣ) to the subject's concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتَّعجُبُ مُساوِ لِلإنسان، وإنَّما سُمِّيَت هذهِ الأعراضُ ذاتيَّةٌ؛ لاستنادِهَا لِلذَّات، وإنْ تفاوت الاستنادُ لِلذَّاتِ في القوَّةِ، أمَّا الاستنادُ لِلذَّاتِ في القسم الأوَّلِ؛ فظاهرُ، وأمَّا في الثَّاني؛ فَلِأنَّ العارض مُستند للجزء، والجزء داخل في الذَّاتِ، فيكونُ مستنداً إلى ما في الذَّاتِ والمستند لِمَا فى الذَّاتِ مُستندٌ لِلذَّات . العطار محمولات المسائلِ الأُخر، شَامِلٌ لِجميع أفرادِ موضوعِ العِلْمِ، فيكونُ عَرَضاً ذاتيا له، مثال شمولِ العَرَضِ الذَّاتي على سبيل الانفراد : كُلُّ متحير، فإنَّ التَّحيز وَحدَهُ شَامِلٌ لِجَميعِ أفرادِ الجِسْمِ، بدونِ أنْ يعتبر معهُ مقابلُهُ أو شمولهُ معَ مُقابِلِهِ، بمعنى أنَّهُ إذا لُوحِةً وحدَهُ لا يَكونُ شَامِلاً ، فإنْ لُوحِظَ مع مُقابِلِهِ تحقَّقَ الشُّمولُ؛ سواء كانَ التَّقابل بينَهُ وبينَ ذلك المقابل تقابلَ الضَّدَّيْنِ، أَو تَقَابُلَ العَدَمِ والمَلَكَةِ، مثالُ العَرَضِ الذَّاتيِّ الشَّامِلِ على سبيلِ التَّقابُلِ قولُنَا : كُلُّ خَطِّ إِمَّا مُنحَنِ وَإِمَّا مُستقيم، فالتَّقابُلُ بينَ الاستقامة والانحناء تقابلُ التَّضاد، ولا شَكٌّ أَنَّ مَجموع الأمرَيْنِ عَرَضٌ شامل لجميع أفرادِ الخطوط، وأما الاستقامة وحدها أو الانحناء وحدَهُ ؛ فَلَا، وَمِثَالُ العَرَضِ الذَاتيّ الشَّامل على سبيل تقابل العدم والملكة : العدد إمَّا زَوج أو فرد، فالتقابل بين الفرديَّةِ والزَّوجيَّةِ تَقابل العدم والمَلَكَةِ، ولا شَكٍّ في شمولِ العَرَضِ الذَّاتيّ في هَذَينِ المثالينِ لِلمَوضوع؛ مع اعتبارِ التَّقابل لا أحدهما فقط، وأمَّا التَّقابل على طريقِ السَّلْبِ والإيجابِ؛ فَغَيرُ مُعتبرٍ لِمَا قالَ؛ إذ المتقابلان تقابل الإيجاب والسَّلْبِ لا اختصاص لهما بمفهوم دون مفهوم؛ لُنَا : الجوهرُ إمَّا مُمكن أوْ لا ؛ ليس فيه شمول ؛ لأنَّ كُلًّا مِنَ الإمكانِ وسَلبِهِ لا يختصَّان بالجوهر، إذ يجريان في العَرَضِ أيضاً، والحاصل أنَّنا نعتبر في المتقابلين على الوجه المذكورِ أنْ يكونَ كُلَّ واحدٍ مِنهُما مَحمُولاً مع ما يقابلهُ إذا أخذ على وجهِ التَّرديدِ كالأمثلة المذكورة، وأَمَّا فِي الثَّالِثِ؛ فَلانَّ العارض اللاحق بواسطة أمرٍ مساو؛ مستند لذلك الأمر المساوي، والمساوي مُستند لِلذَّاتِ، والمستند إلى المستند إلى شيء؛ مستند لذلك الشَّيء.

As for the second, it is because the accident depends on the part, and the part is within the essence, so it depends on what is in the essence, and what depends on what is in the essence depends on the essence. Commentary
This passage from al-Dasuqi and al-Attar defines essential accidents (al-a‘rāḍ al-dhātiyya) and explains their types. The discussion begins with the reason these accidents are called essential: they depend on the essence (dhāt) itself, though the strength of dependence varies. The author then divides this dependence into three types. The first type is direct dependence: the accident attaches to the essence without intermediary, like wonder attaching to man? (the example is unclear). Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of oppositional comprehensiveness through privation and possession?
A) Every line is either curved or straight.
B) Every number is either even or odd.
C) Every body occupies space.
D) Every substance is either possible or not possible.

Key Takeaway
An essential accident can be comprehensive of its subject either individually or as a complementary pair of opposites, but the pair must be specific to the subject; negation/affirmation pairs are too general.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following as an example of individual comprehensiveness or oppositional comprehensiveness (and if oppositional, specify the type: contraries or privation/possession): a) Every number is either even or odd. b) Every line is either curved or straight. c) Every body occupies space. d) Every substance is either possible or not possible. For (d), explain why it does not count as a proper essential accident.

Implicative Indication: Understanding Parts Within the Whole
Defining implicative indication and debating its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish implicative indication from conformity and implicative indication, and explain why tadamun does not require a separate mental transition.

Core Idea
Implicative indication is grasping a part within the whole in one mental act, not a separate inference.

In Islamic logic, words can indicate their meanings in different ways. One key distinction is between indicating the whole meaning (mutabaqa), indicating a part within the whole (tadamun), and indicating a necessary concomitant outside the whole (iltizam). This passage from al-Dasuqi's commentary clarifies the nature of implicative indication, addressing a debate about whether it involves a separate mental step. Understanding this helps you analyze how definitions and concepts are expressed in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of indication
Locate the definitions of mutabaqa, tadamun, and iltizam in the passage. Note how tadamun is specifically defined as a word's indication of a part of its meaning.
The passage gives the example of 'human' indicating 'rational animal' by mutabaqa and 'rational' or 'animal' by tadamun.
2
Grasp the single-act nature of tadamun
The text emphasizes that tadamun involves no transition from word to meaning to part. Instead, when the whole meaning is understood, its parts are understood with it in the same act.
This is contrasted with iltizam, which requires inference to something outside the original meaning.
3
Analyze the debate on transition
Some scholars claimed tadamun requires a transition from word to general meaning to detailed parts. Examine the objections: this would imply the whole is mentally prior to the part (contradicting known priority) and would require understanding the part twice. Note the preferred view: tadamun is understanding the part within the whole, not after.
The text provides a rejoinder that the priority of part over whole applies only when the whole is understood independently of language.
4
Apply the distinction to a new example
Take the Islamic term 'iman' (faith). Its full meaning includes belief in the heart and speech. Determine: does the word 'iman' indicate 'belief in the heart' by tadamun or iltizam? Use the criteria from the passage.
Tadamun: the part is part of the meaning; iltizam: the concomitant is outside the meaning. 'Belief in the heart' is part of the meaning of iman.

Source Text

۲۰۵
أصلاً،
(قَوْلُهُ : وَدَلَالَتُهُ عَلَى جُزْئِهِ ) أي : في حالِ دلالتِهِ على الكلِّ ; لا في حالةٍ أُخرى، فالتَّضمُّنُ فَهُمُ الجزء في ضمن الكلِّ ، وَلَا شكٍّ أنَّهُ إذا فهم المعنى ; فهمت ه معه، فليس في دلالةِ التَّضمن انتقال من اللَّفظِ إلى المعنى، ومن المعنى إلى الجزء، بل هو فَهم واحد يُسمَّى بالقياس إلى تمام المعنى مطابقة، وبالقياس إلى جزئه تضمناً، بخلافِ دلالةِ الالتزام، فإنَّه لا بُدَّ فيها من الانتقالِ مِنَ اللّفظ للمعنى، وَمِنَ المعنى إلى اللازم ; ضرورة أنَّ اللَّازِمَ لا دخل لهُ في الوضعِ وهذا وجهُ مَن يقولُ : إِنَّ التَّضمُنيَّةَ،وضعيّة، والالتزاميَّةَ عقليَّةٌ، وذهبَ بعضُهم إلى أنَّ في دلالةِ التَّضمُّنِ انتقالاً من اللفظ إلى المعنى إجمالاً، ثم إلى أجزائه تفصيلاً، وبحث فيه بأنَّهُ يستلزم تقدُّم وجودِ الكلِّ على وجودِ الجزء في الذهنِ، مَعَ اتَّفَاقِهِم على تقدم الجزء على الكلِّ في الوجودَيْنِ ; الذهني والخارجي، وبأنَّهُ يستلزمُ فَهُم الجزء مرَّتين ; مرَّةً في ضمن المركَّب، وأخرَى مُنفرداً. والوجدان يكذِّبُه، فَالأقيس : ما ذهب إليه بعضُهم من أنَّ التَّضِمُّنَ; فَهُمُ الجزء في ضمن الكلِّ ; لا بعد فهمه، وقد يُجاب عن البحث الأوَّلِ بأَنَّ تقدُّمَ الجزء على الكلِّ في الوجودين; محله إذا اعتبر فَهم الكلِّ بوجه لا من اللفظ، وأمَّا إذا اعتبر فهمه من اللّفظ الذي وضعَ لِلكلِّ، ولم يوضع للجزء ; فلا نسلّم تقدُّم الجزء، كيف وهو مخالف لوضع اللفظ للكلِّ؟!، وأيضاً فهم الكلِّ من اللفظ إِنَّما هو فَهم إجمالي . والجزء لا يتقدَّمُ إلا على الفَهْمِ التّفصيلي، ولذا قالُوا : إِنَّ النَّوعَ قد يحضر في الذهن، وَلَا يحضر الجنس ، يعنون بحضورِ النَّوع ; الحضور الإجماليَّ لَا التَّفصيلي. (قَوْلُهُ : وَدَلَالَتُهُ عَلَى جُزْئِهِ تَضَمُّنُ ... إلخ) حاصلُهُ أَنَّكَ إِذا قُلْتَ: إنسان; فدلالته على الحيوانِ النَّاطِقِ مُطابقة، ودلالته على أحدهما تَضَمُّن . (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ: جُزْءِ الْمَعْنَى الْمَوْضُوع لَهُ أي: حيث كان المعنى الموضوع له
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى جُزْئِهِ قِيلَ : وإِنْ لمْ يُعْلَمُ ذلكَ الجزء بعينه، كما إذا عُلِمَ أَنَّ اللَّفْظَ الكُل
موضوع لِشَيءٍ مُعيَّن وغيرِهِ لم يعيَّن ذلك الغيرُ بعينه، وهو مفهوم في ضمن
Chapter: On the definition of the three types of indication and their rulings. Indication of implication: Its indication of its part; i.e., part of the meaning posited for. Al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'And its indication of its part' means: in the state of its indication of the whole, not in another state. So implicative indication is understanding the part within the whole, and there is no doubt that when the meaning is understood, its part is understood with it. So in implicative indication there is no transition from the word to the meaning and from the meaning to the part; rather it is a single understanding, called with respect to the complete meaning 'conformity' and with respect to its part 'implication'. Unlike implicative indication, for in it it is necessary to transition from the word to the meaning and from the meaning to the necessary implication, because the necessary implication has no role in the positing. This is the view of those who say that implicative indication is conventional and implicative indication is rational. Some held that in implicative indication there is a transition from the word to the meaning generally, then to its parts in detail. It is objected that this requires the prior existence of the whole over the part in the mind, despite their agreement that the part precedes the whole in both mental and external existence, and that it requires understanding the part twice: once within the compound and once alone. And intuition denies it. So the more correct is what some held: that implicative indication is understanding the part within the whole, not after understanding it. The first objection may be answered: that the priority of the part over the whole in the two existences applies when the understanding of the whole is considered in a way that is not from the word; but if it is considered from the word that is posited for the whole and not posited for the part, then we do not concede the priority of the part – how could it be, since it contradicts the positing of the word for the whole? Also, understanding the whole from the word is only a general understanding, and the part does not precede except detailed understanding. Hence they said: the species may be present in the mind while the genus is not present, meaning by presence of the species the general presence, not the detailed. (His saying: 'And its indication of its part is implicative... etc.) The summary: when you say 'human', its indication of 'rational animal' is conformity, and its indication of one of them is implication. (His saying: i.e., part of the meaning posited for) i.e., when the meaning posited for is composite. Al-Attar: (His saying: 'of its part') it is said: even if that particular part is not known, such as when it is known that the whole word is posited for a specific thing and something else, but that other is not specified, and it is understood within.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage defines one of the three types of indication: implicative indication (dalalat al-tadamun). It begins by stating that implicative indication is a word's indication of a part of its meaning. Al-Dasuqi immediately adds that this indication occurs in the same act as the word's indication of the whole — not as a separate step. Thus implicative indication is simply understanding the part as included within the whole. He distinguishes it from implicative indication (dalalat al-iltizam), which requires a transition from word to meaning to a necessary concomitant, because the concomitant is not part of the original positing. This is why some scholars say implicative indication is conventional (wad'iyya) while implicative indication is rational ('aqliyya). Then a debate is presented: some claim that implicative indication involves a transition from the word to a general understanding of the whole, then to detailed understanding of the parts. This is criticized because it would imply that the whole is mentally prior to the part, whereas it is universally agreed that the part is prior in both mental and external existence. Moreover, it would require understanding the part twice — once within the whole and once separately — which is contrary to intuition. The preferred view, therefore, is that implicative indication is simply grasping the part within the whole without a separate transition. A response to the first objection is given: the priority of the part over the whole applies when the whole is understood independently of language; but when the whole is understood through a word specifically posited for it, the priority does not hold, because the word directs the mind to the whole first. Additionally, the understanding from the word is general (ijmali), and the part's priority is only with respect to detailed (tafsili) understanding. The passage concludes with an example: the word 'human' indicates 'rational animal' by conformity (mutabaqa) and indicates either 'rational' or 'animal' by implication (tadamun). Finally, al-Attar adds a note that the part need not be specifically known; it suffices that it is understood in general within the whole.

Key Terms
دلالة التضمن
دلالة المطابقة
دلالة الالتزام

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between implicative indication (tadamun) and implicative indication (iltizam)?
A) Tadamun requires a transition from word to meaning to part, while iltizam does not.
B) Tadamun is understanding the part within the whole without separate inference, while iltizam requires inference to a necessary concomitant outside the meaning.
C) Both are the same.
D) Tadamun is conventional while iltizam is rational.

Key Takeaway
Implicative indication (tadamun) is a concurrent understanding of a part with the whole, not a separate logical step.

intermediate
7 min
Entailment Between Signification Types: Inclusion and Implication Necessitate Correspondence
Commentary explains that tadammun and iltizam necessarily entail mutabaqah, even if only notionally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why inclusive and implicative signification necessarily entail correspondence, and identify the nuance of 'notional' (taqdiri) correspondence.

Core Idea
Inclusive and implicative signification always imply correspondence, at least hypothetically.

In this passage, the author explains a foundational relationship among the three types of signification (mutabaqah, tadammun, iltizam). The key claim is that whenever a word signifies by inclusion or implication, it also signifies by correspondence—either actually or potentially. The commentator and glossators expand on this, clarifying that the entailment is one-way and that the phrase 'even if notionally' accounts for cases where correspondence is not actualized in usage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
From the passage, recall the three types: mutabaqah (correspondence) where the word signifies the whole meaning; tadammun (inclusion) where it signifies a part; and iltizam (implication) where it signifies a necessary concomitant. Note that the passage focuses on the relationship between the latter two and the first.
These are standard in Arabic logic, often illustrated with the example of 'house'.
2
Locate the claim about entailment
The matn states: 'And they entail correspondence, even if notionally.' The 'they' refers to inclusive and implicative signification. The claim is that both types necessarily bring about correspondence signification.
The dual pronoun is confirmed by the commentary, though some manuscripts use plural.
3
Analyze 'wa law taqdira' (even if notionally)
Al-Dasuqi explains that this qualification covers cases where the word is not actually used for its correspondence meaning, but only for a part or concomitant. In such usage, correspondence is potential: the word could indicate the full meaning if intended. This preserves the logical entailment without requiring actual usage.
Al-Attar connects this to the logician's focus on communication and cautions against overcomplicating definitions.
4
Understand the criticism of 'talazum' (mutual entailment)
The commentary notes that 'talazum' implies two-way entailment, but the author immediately says 'and no reverse' (la 'aks), meaning correspondence does not entail inclusion or implication. Therefore, 'luzum' (one-way implication) is more accurate. Abd al-Hakim defends 'talazum' as part of completing the definitions for deeper disclosure.
This nuance is important for precise reasoning about signification relationships.

Source Text

الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: وَإِذْ قَدْ فَرَغَ أي: حين قد فرغ عن تحديد؛ أي: تعريف الدلالات. (قَوْلُهُ: وَتَلْزَمُهُمَا الْمُطَابَقَةُ أي تحقيقاً؛ إذ متى تحقَّقَتِ النَّصْمُنيَّةُ أو الالتزاميَّةُ؛ تحقَّقَتِ المطابقيَّةُ، فيكونانِ مُستلزمين لها، وهذا ما قبل المبالغة. العطار
•
 في أنَّ نظر المنطقي في الألفاظ ليس إلا باعتبار الإفادة والاستفادة، فَلَا وجه لتجديدِ اصطلاحٍ بِلا ضرورةٍ؛ مَعَ إفضائهِ إلى ضيق في أمرِ الدَّلالةِ لإخراج تِلْكَ الدَّلالاتِ السَّابِقَةِ في الاعتبارِ عن الاعتبارِ لا يُقَالُ: الدَّالَّ عندَهُم مَجموعُ اللَّفْظِ والقرينة، فَاللُّزومُ عَقلي مُطْلَقاً، لأنَّا نقولُ: ليس للمجموع معنًى مَلزومٌ لِذَلكَ اللَّازِمِ، بَلْ ليسَ لَهُ وَضْع حقيقي أصْلاً؛ تَأَمَّلْ .
(قَوْلُهُ: شَرَعَ فِي بَيَانِ التَّلَازُمِ بَيْنَهُمَا كَذَا فِي نُسخةٍ بِضَمِيرِ التَّثنيةِ، فيرجِعُ الضَّميرُ لِلاثْنَيْن اللَّذَيْنِ تَضمَّنتَهما الدَّلالاتُ الثَّلاثُ؛ أي: استلزامُ التَّضمن المطابقة، واستلزام الالتزام المطابقة، وفي أُخْرَى بينَها بِضميرِ الجمع؛ فَيُراد المجموعُ، ثمَّ إِنَّ التَّعبير بالتَّلازم ليس على ما ينبغي؛ لاقتضائِهِ التَّلَازِمَ مِنَ الجانبَيْنِ، فَيُنَافِيْهِ قَوْلُ المصنِّفِ: وَلَا عَكْسَ، فَالأولَى التَّعبير باللزوم، قال عبد الحكيم: وَبيانُ التَّلازم مِنْ تَيَمَّةِ التَّعريفات؛ لأنَّه مُوجِبُ لِمَزِيدِ انكشافِ الدَّلالاتِ، فَلَا يردُ أنَّ بيانَ الاستلزامِ لَا دَخْلَ لَهُ في الإفادة والاستفادة. (قَوْلُهُ: وَلَوْ تَقْدِيراً) لم يتعرَّضِ الشَّارِحُ لِشرح هذهِ الغاية، وفي حاشية مير أبي الفتْحِ أَنَّهُ يحتَملُ أنْ يكونَ مُتعلّقاً بالمطابقة؛ أي: لو كانَتِ المطابقة اللازمة تحقيقيَّةٌ وَلَو كَانَتْ تقديرية، ويحتمل أَنْ يَكونَ مُتعلّقاً بِاللُّزوم؛ أي: لَو كَانَ اللُّزوم تحقيقيًّا
And when he had finished defining the three types of signification, he proceeded to explain the entailment between them and its absence, and said: 'And they [dual] entail' i.e., inclusive and implicative; 'correspondence, even if notionally.' Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'And when he had finished') i.e., when he had finished defining; i.e., defining the significations. (His saying: 'And they entail correspondence') i.e., actually; for whenever inclusiveness or implicativeness is realized, correspondence is realized, so they are mutually entailing of it, and this is before exaggeration. (His saying: 'even if notionally') i.e., they entail it even if notionally; i.e., where the word is not used for its correspondence meaning actually, but is used for its part or necessary concomitant, then it is indicative of it by correspondence by virtue of hypothetical intention of it. Al-Attar: • In that the logician's consideration of words is only from the perspective of imparting and seeking understanding, so there is no basis for inventing a new technical term without necessity, especially when it leads to narrowness in the matter of signification by excluding those previously considered significations from consideration. It cannot be said: The signifier for them is the combination of word and contextual indication (qarina), so implication is absolutely rational, because we say: The combination does not have a meaning that is the necessary concomitant of that necessary concomitant, rather it has no real conventional signification at all; consider. (His saying: 'He proceeded to explain the entailment between them' thus in one manuscript with dual pronoun, so the pronoun refers to the two that are contained in the three significations; i.e., entailment of inclusion to correspondence, and entailment of implication to correspondence. In another manuscript, with plural pronoun, so the totality is meant. Then, the expression 'entailment' (talazum) is not appropriate, because it implies mutual entailment from both sides, which contradicts the author's saying: 'and no reverse,' so the preferable expression is 'implication' (luzum). Abd al-Hakim said: The explanation of entailment is from the completion of the definitions, because it necessitates deeper disclosure of the significations, so it is not objected that the explanation of entailment has no role in imparting and seeking understanding. (His saying: 'even if notionally') The commentator did not explain this purpose. In the hashya of Mir Abu al-Fath, it is possible that it is attached to 'correspondence'; i.e., even if the necessary correspondence is actual or notional, or it may be attached to 'implication'; i.e., even if the implication is actual.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage explains the entailment relationship between the three types of signification—mutabaqah (correspondence), tadammun (inclusion), and iltizam (implication). The author states that inclusive and implicative signification entail correspondence, even if only notionally (wa law taqdira). Al-Dasuqi comments that whenever a word signifies by inclusion or implication, it also signifies by correspondence in reality, because if you understand the part or the concomitant, you necessarily understand the whole. However, the phrase 'even if notionally' accounts for cases where the word is not actually used for its correspondence meaning, but only for a part or concomitant; in such cases, correspondence exists hypothetically, as if the speaker intended the full meaning. Al-Attar adds a remark that the logician is only concerned with communication, so there is no need for a new term like 'notional correspondence' that narrows the scope of signification. He also rejects the idea that the signifier is the word-plus-context, which would make all implication rational, because the combination does not have a fixed conventional meaning. The commentary then notes a textual variant: some manuscripts use a dual pronoun referring to inclusion and implication, while others use a plural pronoun referring to all three. The term 'talazum' (mutual entailment) is criticized because it suggests a two-way relationship, but the author explicitly denies the reverse ('la 'aks'), so 'luzum' (one-way implication) is more appropriate. Abd al-Hakim defends the use of 'talazum' as part of a complete definition that clarifies the types. Finally, the phrase 'wa law taqdira' is analyzed: it could modify 'mutabaqah' (correspondence is present even if hypothetical) or 'luzum' (the entailment holds even if only notionally).

A) The entailment is not real but purely hypothetical.
B) Correspondence is sometimes only potential, when the word is not used for its full meaning.
C) The entailment is mutual, so correspondence also entails inclusion and implication.
D) The phrase is meaningless and the commentator ignores it.

Key Takeaway
Inclusive and implicative signification necessarily entail correspondence, even if only notionally; this relationship is one-way (no reverse), so 'luzum' is more precise than 'talazum'.

The Three Significations and Their Non-Entailment
Author defines three significations and their mutual non-entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of signification (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) and explain why tadammun does not entail iltizam.

Core Idea
Inclusion and implication are distinct; a word may include a part without implying something else.

The passage introduces the three foundational types of signification that every term can have: correspondence (mutabaqa), inclusion (tadammun), and implication (iltizam). It then gives a crucial rule: inclusion does not imply implication, and vice versa. This rule prevents a common error in definition and is essential for precise conceptual analysis in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
In the Arabic, locate the terms for the three significations: muṭābaqa (مُطَابَقَة), taḍammun (تَضَمُّن), and iltizām (الْتِزَام). Note that the passage explicitly defines the necessary concomitant (lāzim) for iltizām.
2
Understand each signification
Muṭābaqa is the whole meaning assigned to the word; taḍammun is a part of that meaning; iltizām is something external that necessarily accompanies the meaning, without being part of the original signification.
Example: 'house' mutabaqatan means the entire building; tadammunan means 'room'; iltizaman implies 'door' (if we accept that a house necessarily has a door).
3
Analyze the definition of lāzim
The author defines lāzim as something that follows from conceptualizing the meaning without additional commitment (bi-dūn iltizām). This means iltizām does not require an explicit mental connection beyond the concept itself.
For instance, from 'sun' you spontaneously think of 'light' – that is a lāzim, but it is not part of the meaning of 'sun'.
4
Apply the non-entailment rule
The passage explicitly states that taḍammun does not entail iltizām, and vice versa. Test this: 'house' includes 'room' (taḍammun), but does it imply something like 'door'? Not necessarily – a house could be doorless in some contexts. Conversely, 'sun' implies 'light', but 'light' is not a part of the meaning of 'sun' (no taḍammun).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في تعريف الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها لَازِمٌ بِحَيْثُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْمَعْنَى تَصَوُّرُهُ بِدُونِ الْتِزَامٍ. وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ التَّضَمُّنَ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْالْتِزَامَ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ.
A chapter: On defining the three significations and their rulings. A necessary concomitant such that from the conceptualization of the meaning, its conceptualization follows without commitment. And know that inclusion does not entail implication, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage from the commentary defines the three significations (mutābaqa, taḍammun, iltizām) and clarifies their interrelations. The author begins by stating that a necessary concomitant (lāzim) is something that follows from the conceptualization of the meaning without any additional commitment. For example, from the concept of 'whiteness' one necessarily conceives 'color', but this does not require an explicit connection. The key ruling is that taḍammun (inclusion) does not necessitate iltizām (implication), and iltizām does not necessitate taḍammun. This means a term can include a part of its meaning without implying something external, and a term can imply something without its meaning being part of the original signification. For instance, the word 'house' includes 'room' (taḍammun) but does not necessarily imply 'door' (iltizām) unless specified, while 'sun' implies 'light' (iltizām) but 'light' is not an included part of the meaning of 'sun'. The passage guards against mistakenly thinking these relations are reciprocal.

Key Terms
دَلالَة (dalāla): Signification; the relation between a word and its meaning. Here the three types are the focus.
مُطَابَقَة (muṭābaqa): Equivalence; the word signifies the whole meaning it is assigned to.
تَضَمُّن (taḍammun): Inclusion; the word signifies a part of its meaning.
الْتِزَام (iltizām): Implication; the word signifies something external that necessarily accompanies the meaning.
لَازِم (lāzim): Necessary concomitant; something that must be conceptualized when the meaning is conceived. [Note: In later logic, al-lāzim may be subdivided into bayyin (evident) and ghayr bayyin (non-evident).]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
From the passage, which statement is correct regarding the relationship between taḍammun and iltizām?
A) Taḍammun always entails iltizām.
B) Iltizām always entails taḍammun.
C) Taḍammun does not entail iltizām, and vice versa.
D) Taḍammun and iltizām are identical.

Key Takeaway
The three significations—correspondence, inclusion, and implication—are distinct; inclusion does not imply implication nor vice versa.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Understanding Istiqlal and the Independent Simple Term
Commentators explain the division of mufrad into mustaqill and ghayr mustaqill based on independence in meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a term is mustaqill or ghayr mustaqill based on conceptual self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
A simple term's independence is defined by meaning-self-sufficiency, not by its ability to predicate alone.

This lesson unpacks the key division of the simple term (mufrad) into independent (mustaqill) and non-independent (ghayr mustaqill). We focus on al-Attar's definition of istiqlal as conceptual self-sufficiency, and contrast it with the particle (harf) which requires a related noun (muta'allaq) to complete its meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its division
Find the definiendum: the simple term (mufrad) is divided based on whether it is independent (mustaqill) or not.
2
Extract the definition of istiqlal
Al-Attar says independence means 'by conceptual independence' — the meaning is self-sufficient without needing another term.
This is the essential definition (hadd), not the necessary consequence (lazim).
3
Contrast with the particle (harf)
A particle's meaning depends on its related noun (muta'allaq); thus it is not independent and cannot be used alone in predication.
Example: 'فِي' (in) requires a location noun to be understood.
4
Recognize the commentator's method
The commentator defined istiqlal by its sign (ability to predicate alone), but al-Attar clarifies this is a necessary consequence, not the essence.
Understanding this distinction helps avoid confusing a definition with its effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام المفرد (وَهُوَ) أي: المفردُ : (إِنِ اسْتَقَلَّ) بالإخبار به وحدَه. قَالَ العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : إِنِ اسْتَقَلَّ) أي : بالمفهوميَّة؛ أي بإفادة المفهومِ بِنَفْسِهِ مِن غير احتياج لشيء آخر، وحينئذ؛ فيلزمُ الإخبارُ بِهِ وحدَه، فما فسَّر بِهِ الشَّارحُ الاستقلال؛ تفسير باللازم، وهذا بخلافِ الحرف، فإِنَّ فَهُم مَعناهُ يتوقَّفُ على ذكرِ المتعلِّقِ. وقَالَ الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : تَأَمَّلْ) أي: في الفرق المذكور، وتأمَّلْنَاهُ فَوجدناه غير صحيح؛ إذ الحقِّ أَنَّهُ لَا فرقَ بينَهما؛ لأنَّ الجزء من كلِّ منهما حال كونهما عَلَمَيْن بمنزلةِ زاي زيد في الدَّلالة على شيء، ودلالة الجزء من كلّ منهما إنَّما هي قبل جعلهما عَلَمَيْن، وَلَا كلامَ فيه، فالحقُّ أنَّ المفردَ قِسمان فقط كما تقدَّم.
Divisions of the simple term: (And it) i.e. the simple term: (if it is independent) in reporting by it alone. Al-Attar said: (His statement: 'if it is independent') means by conceptual independence; i.e., by its meaning being self-sufficient without needing anything else. Then, it follows that reporting by it alone is necessary. So what the commentator interpreted the independence with is an interpretation by implication. This is unlike the particle, for the understanding of its meaning depends on mentioning its related noun. And al-Dasuqi said: (His statement: 'reflect') meaning on the aforementioned difference, and we reflected and found it incorrect. Indeed, the correct view is that there is no difference between them, because the part of each of them, while they are proper nouns, is like the letter 'zay' of 'Zayd' in indicating something. And the indication of the part of each of them is only before making them proper nouns, and there is no dispute about that. So the correct view is that the simple term is only two divisions as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage defines and expands upon the division of the simple term (mufrad) in logic. The text under discussion states that the simple term is divided based on whether it can stand alone for predication (istiqlal). Al-Attar explains that 'independence' here means conceptual self-sufficiency: the meaning is understood by itself without requiring another term. If the meaning is independent, then the word can be used alone to report/predicate. However, he notes that the commentator's interpretation of istiqlal as 'being able to be used alone for predication' is actually a description of the necessary consequence (lazim) of independence, not the definition itself. The particle (harf) is different: its meaning is not fully understood without mentioning its related noun (muta'allaq). Al-Dasuqi then addresses a previous distinction between two kinds of proper nouns (al-alam al-shakhsi and al-alam al-jinsi) which was questioned. He argues that upon reflection, there is no real difference between them: in both cases, the component parts (like 'Abd' in 'Abdullah') lose their independent indication once they become part of the proper noun, similar to the letter 'zay' in 'Zayd' which does not independently indicate anything. Therefore, the correct division of the simple term is only two: independent (mustaqill) and non-independent (ghayr mustaqill). The non-independent category includes both particles and verbs (as argued elsewhere). This division is fundamental in logic for understanding how terms function in propositions.

Key Terms
مفرد
استقلال
حرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is the particle (harf) considered non-independent?
A) Because it cannot be used in a sentence.
B) Because its meaning is not understood without mentioning its related noun.
C) Because it indicates time.
D) Because it is always compound.

Key Takeaway
A simple term is independent if its meaning is understood by itself; otherwise, it is non-independent like a particle.

Refining the Division: Al-Dasuqi's Objection to a False Distinction
Al-Dasuqi corrects a previous distinction about proper nouns, reaffirming the simple bipartite division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why al-Dasuqi rejects the distinction between two types of proper nouns and state the correct bipartite division of the simple term.

Core Idea
The part of a proper noun is like the letter 'zay' in 'Zayd' — it has no independent indication once it becomes part of the proper noun.

This lesson examines al-Dasuqi's critique of a proposed distinction between personal proper nouns (alam shakhsi) and generic proper nouns (alam jinsi). He argues that the supposed difference is unfounded and that the simple term remains only two divisions: independent and non-independent. Through his analogy, we learn how parts of proper nouns lose their independent meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed distinction
Some claimed a difference between al-sham al-shakhsi (e.g., 'Abdullah') and al-sham al-jinsi (e.g., 'Usamah') regarding the independence of their parts.
The text says 'تأمَّلْ' (reflect) on the difference.
2
Analyze al-Dasuqi's analogy
Al-Dasuqi says: The part of each proper noun (like 'Abd' in 'Abdullah') is like the letter 'zay' in 'Zayd' — it only indicates something before becoming part of the proper noun, not after.
Once the term becomes a proper noun, its parts no longer independently indicate their original meanings.
3
Understand the conclusion
There is no difference between the two types; both are proper nouns whose parts function the same way.
The correct view is that mufrad is only two divisions: mustaqill and ghayr mustaqill.
4
Apply the correction to the division
Restate the correct division: simple terms are either independent (like 'rajul') or non-independent (like 'fi'). The earlier suggested extra category is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Which Seven Divisions Apply to Nouns, Verbs, and Particles
Author explains which of the seven divisions apply to verbs and particles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify each of the seven divisions (ishtirak, naql, haqiqa, majaz, tawato', tashkik, 'alam) as applicable to nouns, verbs, or particles based on Dasuqi's commentary.

Core Idea
Not all seven logical divisions apply equally to nouns, verbs, and particles.

The seven divisions of mufrad are a key tool in Islamic logic, but their application is not uniform. In this passage, Dasuqi clarifies which divisions are specific to nouns and which extend to verbs and particles, correcting a common misunderstanding. Understanding this scope prevents misapplication in later logical and jurisprudential reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of mufrad
The passage divides single words into noun (ism), verb (fi'l), and particle (harf). Recognize these categories as the types over which the seven divisions are distributed.
2
List the seven divisions
The divisions are: mushtarak (common), manqul (transferred), haqiqa (literal), majaz (metaphorical), mutawati' (univocal), mushakkik (graded), and 'alam (proper noun). Write them down for reference.
3
Match each division to applicable word types
From the passage: ishtirak, naql, haqiqa, majaz apply to nouns, verbs, and particles. But tawato', tashkik, and 'alam apply only to nouns. Check Dasuqi's statements: 'فَلَا يجريان إلا في الاسم' for tawato' and tashkik, and 'وكذلكَ العَلَم'.
Note that naql (transfer) is said to apply to verbs and particles, though the commentator elsewhere notes it is rare for particles.
4
Understand the reason for the restriction
The exclusive applicability to nouns arises because univocality and gradation require a universal concept that applies equally or with priority/posteriority to individuals, which verbs and particles do not exhibit in the same way. Proper nouns designate specific entities, which verbs and particles cannot do.
5
Apply the classification to an example
Take the particle 'min'. According to the passage, 'min' can be mushtarak (between meanings of commencement and partition), haqiqa (when used for partition), and majaz (when used for other relations). But it cannot be mutawati', mushakkik, or 'alam. Verify this reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مباحثِ الألفاظ
الدسوقي
وكذلكَ الحرفُ؛ يكونُ مُشتركاً كَـ من بين الابتداء والتَّبعيض، ويكون حقيقةً «في» إذا استعمل في الظرفية، وقد يكون مجازاً كـ«في» إذا استعمل بمعنى، وأمَّا التَّواطُؤ والتَشكُّكُ؛ فَلَا يجريان إلا في الاسم، وكذلكَ العَلَم. وظاهرُ المصنف: أنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ مِن أقسام المفرد؛ سواء كان اسماً أو فعلاً أو أداة؛ ينقسم إلى هذهِ الأقسام السَّبعة، وليس كذلك، فكانَ الأَوْلَى لِلمصنِّفِ جعلُ المقسَّمِ الاسم خاصَّة كَمَا فعل الكاتبيُّ، وإن كان يمكنُ الجواب عن المصنِّف بأنَّ المراد بالمفردِ الَّذي جعلهُ مُقسَّماً لهذه الأقسام السبعة المفرد من حيثُ تحققه (۱) الاسم لا المفرد من حيثُ هو، فَتَأمَّل.
العطّار
في
عَبذب
جعل المقسم المفرد صدق تعريفِ العَلَمِ على الحرفِ بالنَّظر إلى ظَاهِرِ قَولِهِ: فَمَعَ تَشخُصِهِ وضَعاً علم؛ لأنَّ معنى الحرفِ جزئي مُشَخَصٌ فَمندفع بِمَا صَرَّحٍ بِهِ الحكيمِ مِنْ أنَّ هذا التَّقسيم مبني على رأي القائلين بأنَّ المضمرات وأسماء الإشارة والحروفَ مَوضوعةٌ للمعاني الكُلِّيَّةِ، إِلَّا أَنَّهُ شرط استعمالها في الجزئيات؛ فهي داخلةٌ في الكُلّيّ، وأمَّا على رأي مَنْ قالَ إنَّها مَوضُوعةٌ بِالوضع العام للمعاني الجزئية؛ فَخارجة عن أقسام القسمةِ الأولى؛ لِعدمِ كَونِ معناها واحداً، وَعَن أقسام القِسمةِ الثَّانية وهو ظاهرُ، ومَن قالَ إِنَّها موضوعةٌ لِمعانٍ مُشخّصةٍ؛ فَقَدْ سَهَا؛ لأنَّها موضوعةٌ لِمعانٍ جزئيّةٍ داخلةٍ تحتَ المفهوم الكُلّيّ الَّذي هُوَ آلَةٌ لِوَضْعِهَا؛ سواءٌ كَانَتْ مُشخّصةً أَوْ لَا .ا.هـ. على أنَّ البحث الَّذي في الحاشية أصلُهُ للدَّوَّاني، واقتصر
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ: المفرد من حيثُ تَحقّقُهُ ... إلخ) أي: فيراد به خصوص الاسم، وفي كلام المصنف استخدام حيث جعل المقسم أولاً المفرد من حيث هو ثم أعاد عليه الضمير في التقسيم الثاني باعتبار بعض أفراده وهو الاسم لأنه هو الذي يكون علماً ومتواطئاً ومشككاً دون قسيميه الكلمة والأداة، وإن اشترك الجميع في الباقي والذي دعا إلى هذا قول المصنف أيضاً وهذا الفهم غير متعين لجواز أن يكون المقسم الثاني هو الاسم بقرينة قوله فمع تشخص معناه والتعبير بأيضاً لا ينافيه فإن أقسام الجزئي وهو الاسم أقسام للكلّي وهو المفرد ضرورة تحقق الكلِّي في ضمن جزئياته كما لا يخفى، ولعلَّ هذا هو السر في أمر المحشّي . فتأمل .
Chapter: On the Investigations of Words. Dasuqi: And likewise the particle; it can be common (mushtarak) like 'min' between the meanings of commencement and partition, and it can be literal (haqiqa) like 'fi' when used for containment, and it can be metaphorical (majaz) like 'fi' when used to mean 'upon'. As for univocality (tawato') and gradation (tashkik), they do not apply except to the noun, and likewise the proper noun (alam). The apparent meaning of the author is that every one of the divisions of the single word (mufrad)—whether noun, verb, or particle—is divided into these seven divisions, but it is not so. Thus it would have been better for the author to make the divided term specifically the noun, as al-Katibi did, although a response for the author is possible: that what is meant by mufrad that he made subject to these seven divisions is the mufrad in terms of its realization (1) as a noun, not mufrad as such. So reflect. al-Attar: ... (the text regarding the definition of alam applying to particles is rebutted by what al-Hakim explicitly stated: that this division is based on the view that pronouns, demonstratives, and particles are placed for universal meanings, but their use is conditioned to particulars; thus they fall under the universal. As for the view that they are placed by a general putting for particular meanings, they fall outside the first division because their meaning is not one, and outside the second division, and that is apparent. Whoever says they are placed for particularized meanings has erred, because they are placed for particular meanings falling under the universal concept which is the instrument for their placement, whether they are particularized or not. Therefore, the discussion in the gloss originally belongs to al-Dawwani, and he limited it. Footnote (1): (His saying: 'mufrad in terms of its realization' etc.) i.e., it intends specifically the noun. In the author's statement, there is a usage of putting the divided term first as mufrad as such, then returning the pronoun in the second division with consideration of some of its individuals, which is the noun, because it is what is proper noun, univocal, and graded, not its two counterparts: the verb and the particle, even though all share in the rest. What prompted this is also the author's statement. And this understanding is not certain, because it is possible that the second divided term is the noun, indicated by his saying 'with the particularization of its meaning', and the expression 'also' does not negate it, for the divisions of the particular—which is the noun—are divisions of the universal—which is the mufrad—necessarily due to the realization of the universal within its particulars, as is not hidden. Perhaps this is the secret in the matter of the commentator. So reflect.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the division of the single word (mufrad) into seven categories: common (mushtarak), transferred (manqul), literal (haqiqa), metaphorical (majaz), univocal (mutawati'), graded (mushakkik), and proper noun (alam). The commentator, Dasuqi, clarifies that these divisions do not apply equally to nouns, verbs, and particles. He states that verbs and particles can be common, literal, and metaphorical, and can also be transferred (naql is rare for particles). However, univocality and gradation only apply to nouns, not to verbs or particles. Similarly, being a proper noun (alam) is exclusive to nouns. The commentator then critiques the author (the writer of the original text being commented upon) for appearing to claim that every mufrad—whether noun, verb, or particle—is divided into all seven. He says this is not the case, and it would have been better to make the divided term specifically the noun, as al-Katibi did. However, he offers a possible interpretation: that the author meant by 'mufrad' only the noun in the context of these seven divisions. The commentary then moves to al-Attar's remarks, which address a technical objection: the definition of 'alam (proper noun) might seem to apply to particles because their meanings are particularized. Al-Attar rebuts this by citing al-Hakim (likely Ibn Sina) that this division is built on the view that particles and demonstratives are placed for universal meanings, even if their usage is for particulars. If one holds that they are placed for particular meanings, they fall outside the initial division. The footnote further clarifies that the term 'mufrad from the perspective of its realization' refers specifically to the noun in the second division, explaining the apparent inconsistency. The core lesson is that logical classifications of words are sensitive to grammatical categories, and a one-size-fits-all approach fails.

Key Takeaway
The seven logical divisions of mufrad are not universally applicable across nouns, verbs, and particles; univocality, gradation, and proper names are exclusive to nouns.

The Five Universals
intermediate
5 min
Critique of Universal Application of the Seven Divisions
Author explains which of the seven divisions apply to verbs and particles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Dasuqi's critique of the claim that all seven divisions apply to every mufrad, and evaluate his suggested correction to restrict the divided term to nouns.

Core Idea
The author's apparent claim that all seven divisions apply to every mufrad is false; it is better to restrict them to nouns.

Dasuqi identifies a flaw in the original author's presentation: it seems to claim that every single word—noun, verb, particle—falls under all seven divisions. But this is false because three divisions are exclusive to nouns. Dasuqi argues it would have been better to restrict the divided term to nouns, following al-Katibi, though he offers a possible reconciliation. This lesson examines the critique and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original author's apparent claim
From Dasuqi's words: 'وظاهرُ المصنف: أنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ مِن أقسام المفرد؛ سواء كان اسماً أو فعلاً أو أداة؛ ينقسم إلى هذهِ الأقسام السَّبعة' – The author appears to claim that every mufrad, regardless of being noun, verb, or particle, is divided into all seven divisions.
2
Extract Dasuqi's objection
Dasuqi says 'وليس كذلك' – it is not so. He then provides the reason: tawato', tashkik, and 'alam do not apply to verbs or particles, as previously stated.
3
Understand the suggested correction
Dasuqi says: 'فكانَ الأَوْلَى لِلمصنِّفِ جعلُ المقسَّمِ الاسم خاصَّة كَمَا فعل الكاتبيُّ' – It would have been better to make the divided term specifically the noun, as al-Katibi did. This restricts the scope to nouns, which can have all seven divisions.
4
Analyze the possible reconciliation
Dasuqi offers a possible defense: perhaps the author meant 'mufrad in terms of its realization as a noun' (المفرد من حيثُ تحققه الاسم). This reconciles by interpreting the divided term as implicitly restricted to nouns. Evaluate whether this is a plausible interpretation.
5
Assess the logical validity of the critique
The critique is valid: if a division (e.g., 'alam) is defined in a way that only nouns can satisfy, then claiming that verbs or particles also fall under that division is false. The correction to restrict the scope eliminates the error. The reconciliation attempt shows that the original author's wording could be interpreted differently, but the need for such interpretation itself indicates the original was ambiguous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مباحثِ الألفاظ
الدسوقي
وكذلكَ الحرفُ؛ يكونُ مُشتركاً كَـ من بين الابتداء والتَّبعيض، ويكون حقيقةً «في» إذا استعمل في الظرفية، وقد يكون مجازاً كـ«في» إذا استعمل بمعنى، وأمَّا التَّواطُؤ والتَشكُّكُ؛ فَلَا يجريان إلا في الاسم، وكذلكَ العَلَم. وظاهرُ المصنف: أنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ مِن أقسام المفرد؛ سواء كان اسماً أو فعلاً أو أداة؛ ينقسم إلى هذهِ الأقسام السَّبعة، وليس كذلك، فكانَ الأَوْلَى لِلمصنِّفِ جعلُ المقسَّمِ الاسم خاصَّة كَمَا فعل الكاتبيُّ، وإن كان يمكنُ الجواب عن المصنِّف بأنَّ المراد بالمفردِ الَّذي جعلهُ مُقسَّماً لهذه الأقسام السبعة المفرد من حيثُ تحققه (۱) الاسم لا المفرد من حيثُ هو، فَتَأمَّل.
العطّار
في
عَبذب
جعل المقسم المفرد صدق تعريفِ العَلَمِ على الحرفِ بالنَّظر إلى ظَاهِرِ قَولِهِ: فَمَعَ تَشخُصِهِ وضَعاً علم؛ لأنَّ معنى الحرفِ جزئي مُشَخَصٌ فَمندفع بِمَا صَرَّحٍ بِهِ الحكيمِ مِنْ أنَّ هذا التَّقسيم مبني على رأي القائلين بأنَّ المضمرات وأسماء الإشارة والحروفَ مَوضوعةٌ للمعاني الكُلِّيَّةِ، إِلَّا أَنَّهُ شرط استعمالها في الجزئيات؛ فهي داخلةٌ في الكُلّيّ، وأمَّا على رأي مَنْ قالَ إنَّها مَوضُوعةٌ بِالوضع العام للمعاني الجزئية؛ فَخارجة عن أقسام القسمةِ الأولى؛ لِعدمِ كَونِ معناها واحداً، وَعَن أقسام القِسمةِ الثَّانية وهو ظاهرُ، ومَن قالَ إِنَّها موضوعةٌ لِمعانٍ مُشخّصةٍ؛ فَقَدْ سَهَا؛ لأنَّها موضوعةٌ لِمعانٍ جزئيّةٍ داخلةٍ تحتَ المفهوم الكُلّيّ الَّذي هُوَ آلَةٌ لِوَضْعِهَا؛ سواءٌ كَانَتْ مُشخّصةً أَوْ لَا .ا.هـ. على أنَّ البحث الَّذي في الحاشية أصلُهُ للدَّوَّاني، واقتصر
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ: المفرد من حيثُ تَحقّقُهُ ... إلخ) أي: فيراد به خصوص الاسم، وفي كلام المصنف استخدام حيث جعل المقسم أولاً المفرد من حيث هو ثم أعاد عليه الضمير في التقسيم الثاني باعتبار بعض أفراده وهو الاسم لأنه هو الذي يكون علماً ومتواطئاً ومشككاً دون قسيميه الكلمة والأداة، وإن اشترك الجميع في الباقي والذي دعا إلى هذا قول المصنف أيضاً وهذا الفهم غير متعين لجواز أن يكون المقسم الثاني هو الاسم بقرينة قوله فمع تشخص معناه والتعبير بأيضاً لا ينافيه فإن أقسام الجزئي وهو الاسم أقسام للكلّي وهو المفرد ضرورة تحقق الكلِّي في ضمن جزئياته كما لا يخفى، ولعلَّ هذا هو السر في أمر المحشّي . فتأمل .
Chapter: On the Investigations of Words. Dasuqi: And likewise the particle; it can be common (mushtarak) like 'min' between the meanings of commencement and partition, and it can be literal (haqiqa) like 'fi' when used for containment, and it can be metaphorical (majaz) like 'fi' when used to mean 'upon'. As for univocality (tawato') and gradation (tashkik), they do not apply except to the noun, and likewise the proper noun (alam). The apparent meaning of the author is that every one of the divisions of the single word (mufrad)—whether noun, verb, or particle—is divided into these seven divisions, but it is not so. Thus it would have been better for the author to make the divided term specifically the noun, as al-Katibi did, although a response for the author is possible: that what is meant by mufrad that he made subject to these seven divisions is the mufrad in terms of its realization (1) as a noun, not mufrad as such. So reflect. al-Attar: ... (the text regarding the definition of alam applying to particles is rebutted by what al-Hakim explicitly stated: that this division is based on the view that pronouns, demonstratives, and particles are placed for universal meanings, but their use is conditioned to particulars; thus they fall under the universal. As for the view that they are placed by a general putting for particular meanings, they fall outside the first division because their meaning is not one, and outside the second division, and that is apparent. Whoever says they are placed for particularized meanings has erred, because they are placed for particular meanings falling under the universal concept which is the instrument for their placement, whether they are particularized or not. Therefore, the discussion in the gloss originally belongs to al-Dawwani, and he limited it. Footnote (1): (His saying: 'mufrad in terms of its realization' etc.) i.e., it intends specifically the noun. In the author's statement, there is a usage of putting the divided term first as mufrad as such, then returning the pronoun in the second division with consideration of some of its individuals, which is the noun, because it is what is proper noun, univocal, and graded, not its two counterparts: the verb and the particle, even though all share in the rest. What prompted this is also the author's statement. And this understanding is not certain, because it is possible that the second divided term is the noun, indicated by his saying 'with the particularization of its meaning', and the expression 'also' does not negate it, for the divisions of the particular—which is the noun—are divisions of the universal—which is the mufrad—necessarily due to the realization of the universal within its particulars, as is not hidden. Perhaps this is the secret in the matter of the commentator. So reflect.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the division of the single word (mufrad) into seven categories: common (mushtarak), transferred (manqul), literal (haqiqa), metaphorical (majaz), univocal (mutawati'), graded (mushakkik), and proper noun (alam). The commentator, Dasuqi, clarifies that these divisions do not apply equally to nouns, verbs, and particles. He states that verbs and particles can be common, literal, and metaphorical, and can also be transferred (naql is rare for particles). However, univocality and gradation only apply to nouns, not to verbs or particles. Similarly, being a proper noun (alam) is exclusive to nouns. The commentator then critiques the author (the writer of the original text being commented upon) for appearing to claim that every mufrad—whether noun, verb, or particle—is divided into all seven. He says this is not the case, and it would have been better to make the divided term specifically the noun, as al-Katibi did. However, he offers a possible interpretation: that the author meant by 'mufrad' only the noun in the context of these seven divisions. The commentary then moves to al-Attar's remarks, which address a technical objection: the definition of 'alam (proper noun) might seem to apply to particles because their meanings are particularized. Al-Attar rebuts this by citing al-Hakim (likely Ibn Sina) that this division is built on the view that particles and demonstratives are placed for universal meanings, even if their usage is for particulars. If one holds that they are placed for particular meanings, they fall outside the initial division. The footnote further clarifies that the term 'mufrad from the perspective of its realization' refers specifically to the noun in the second division, explaining the apparent inconsistency. The core lesson is that logical classifications of words are sensitive to grammatical categories, and a one-size-fits-all approach fails.

اشتراك
نقل
حقيقة

Key Takeaway
