Definition of Mutawāṭiʾ (Univocal) and the Realization of Meaning
Defines mutawāṭiʾ with examples and clarifies ṣidq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining characteristics of a mutawāṭiʾ term and explain why ṣidq here means taḥaqquq and not ḥaml.

Core Idea
Mutawāṭiʾ: a term applies equally to all individuals; ṣidq means realization, not predication.

This lesson focuses on the foundational concept of mutawāṭiʾ (univocal) as presented in the text. The author defines it as a term whose meaning applies equally to all its individuals, and the commentators clarify a crucial nuance: 'truth' (ṣidq) here refers to the realization of the meaning in the individuals, not the act of predication. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly classifying universals and avoiding confusion with graded terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its definition
Find the Arabic statement defining mutawāṭiʾ. Note the examples: 'human' (external individuals) and 'sun' (mental individuals). The condition is that the term applies equally to all its individuals without any priority or intensity.
2
Distinguish ṣidq as taḥaqquq not ḥaml
Al-Dasuqi explicitly says ṣidq here means taḥaqquq (realization) because the meaning itself is not predicated; it is the term that is predicated. Confirm this in the passage: 'the meaning is not predicated'.
3
Test the equality condition
No individual is prior, more intense, or more entitled in the meaning itself. But individuals may differ in external attributes (e.g., age, strength). Recognize that the superlative form is the criterion for gradation (tashkīk) as per Buhayman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالإنسانِ والشَّمس، فإنَّ صِدْقَهُما على أفرادهما الذهنيَّةِ والخارجيَّةِ بالسَّويَّة، وليس بعضُ الأفراد أولى من بعض. الدسوقي فيها؛ أي: في تلك الأفراد؛ أي: إن استوَتِ الأفراد في تحققِ معناه فيها من غيرِ تفاوت بأولية، أو أولوية، أو شدة، أو ضعف، فقوله: (وصدقه عليها) عطف تفسير بحسب المراد، وظهر لك أنَّ المراد بالصِّدقِ هُنَا : التَّحقُّقُ لَا الحملُ ; لأنَّ المعنى لا يحمل، وكانَ الأَوْلَى حذفه. (قَوْلُهُ : كَالْإِنْسَانِ) مثال للمتواطئ الذي أفراده خارجيَّة، والشَّمسُ مثالٌ لِمَا أفرادُهُ ذهنيَّة، فإنَّ صدقَهُما على أفرادهما الذهنية والخارجية بالسوية. (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّ صِدْقَهُمَا ) أي : فإِنَّ صدق معناهما ; أي: تحقُّقُ مَعناهُما، وقوله : على أفرادِهِمَا)؛ أي: فى أفرادِهِمَا، و «على» بمعنى في. (قَوْلُهُ: وَلَيْسَ بَعْضُ الْأَفْرَادِ أَوْلَى مِنْ بَعْضٍ ) أي : بذلك المفهومِ لَا بأوَّليَّة، وَلَا أولوية، ولا شدَّة، وَلَا غير ذلك. العطّار زيداً أشدُّ أو أقدمُ أو أَوْلَى بِالإنسانيَّةِ مِن عَمْرِو ; على ما نُقِلَ عَن «بهمينار» أنَّ معيار التّشكيكِ استعمال صيغة التفضيل، ولا يتوقَّفُ ذلكَ الحُكْمُ على كَونِهِ تمام حقيقةِ أفرادِهِ وعلى كَوْنِ حقيقتِهِ الحيوانَ النَّاطِق أو غيرها على ما وهم ا.هـ. عبد الحكيم، والمراد بالصدقِ حَمْلُ المواطأة; إذ الكُلّي محمول على أفراده بهذا الحَمْلَ، فالمتواطِئُ كَالإنسانِ بالنِّسبة إلى أفرادِهِ، والإنسانيَّةُ بالنسبة إلى أفرادِهَا وهي الحصص لا كالإنسانيَّةِ بالنِّسبة إلى أفرادِ الإنسانِ، فالتَّواطؤ يتحقَّقُ في المشتقات والمبادي بخلافِ التّشكيكِ ; فإنَّهُ يتحقَّقُ في المشتقَاتِ فَقَطْ كَمَا حَرَّرَهُ مير زاهد. فَصْلٌ: في مباحثِ الألفاظ وسُمِّي متواطئاً ; لتوافق الأفراد في معناه منَ التَّواطؤ، وهو التَّوافق . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : لِتَوَافُقِ الْأَفْرَادِ فِي مَعْنَاهُ أي : في معنى ذلك اللفظ المفرد، ومعناه: هو الأمرُ الكُلّي، فحظ زيدٍ مِنَ الإنسانيَّةِ كَحطّ عمرو منها، والقدر الحاصل منها في العالِمِ كَالحاصل منها في الجاهل، والحاصل منها في الأنبياء كالحاصل منها في العطار الأفراد الفرضيَّةَ؛ انحصر المتواطئ في الكُلِّيَّاتِ الفرضيَّةِ كَنقائِضِ المفهوماتِ الشَّاملةِ، وأجابَ أبو الفتح بإرادة المعنى الأوَّلِ ; لأنَّهُ المتبادرُ، وتخصيص المقسم بحيثُ يخرج عنه الألفاظ الموضوعةُ بإزاء الكُلِّيَّاتِ الفرضيَّةِ والكُلِّيَّاتِ المنحصرة في فردٍ ; مَعَ امتناعِ الغَيْرِ لِعَدَمِ اشتهارها في المحاوراتِ، أو أنْ يُرادَ بِتَفاوتِ الأفرادِ في صِدْقِ المعنى عليها معناه المتبادرُ، ويؤوَّلُ تساوي الأفراد في صدقه عليها بسلب ذلك التَّفاوتِ ; سواءٌ لمْ يَكُنْ لِلمعنى صِدْقٌ في نَفْسِ الأمر عليها ; أو كان ولم يكن فيهِ تفاوت في نَفْسِ الأمرِ، وحينئذ تدخل المذكوراتُ في المتواطئ ا.هـ. ومَعنَى الجوابِ الثَّاني أنَّا نؤوّلُ التّساوي بعدم التَّفاوتِ فَتحصل قضيَّةٌ سالبة هي: المتواطئ ليست أفراده متفاوتةً، والقضية السالبة تصدق بنفي الموضوع، وهذا الجواب هُوَ المختارُ، قال عبد الحكيم : القولُ بأنَّ لفظ اللاشيء لا يُسمّى كُلَّيًّا، وأنَّ المعتبر في التَّواطئ والتّشكيكِ الصَّدقُ في نَفْسِ الأمر، والكُلِّيَّاتُ الفرضيَّةُ خارجةٌ عَنِ القِسْمَيْنِ مِمَّا لا شَاهِدَ عليهِ من كلامهم ولا فائدة إلى ذلك، كيف وقد قالَ الشَّيخُ في «الشّفاء»: الكُلِّيُّ إِنَّما يصيرُ كُليًّا بأنَّ لهُ نسبةً ما، إمَّا بالوجودِ وإِمَّا بصحَةِ التَّوهُم إلى جزئيَّاتٍ يُحمَلُ عليها .ا.هـ. والكُلّي الفرضي هو الذي لا يوجد له فرد لا في الخارج وَلَا فِي الذَّهَنِ; فَلِذَلِكَ الُوا : اللا موجود الخارجي كُلّي ذهني، واللا موجود الخارجي والذهني كُلِّيٌّ فرضي، ويمثلونَ لَهَا بِاللاشيء واللاممكن إمكاناً عاماً، وإنَّما قُيَّدَ بالعام; لأنَّه يتناول جميع الأشياء مِنَ الواجب والممتنع والممكن، بخلافِ الإمكانِ الخاص; فَإِنَّهُ إِنَّما يصدقُ بالأخير، فإذا دخل على الممكنِ العَامّ حرفُ السَّلْبِ; كَانَ كُلِّيًّا فرضيا، ومثلُ الكُلِّيّ الفرضي في الأشكال ; المذكورُ الكُلِّيُّ المنحَصِرُ في فرد; معَ امتناع الغيرِ كَالواجب والقديم بالذات.
Like 'human' and 'sun', for their truth applies to their mental and external individuals equally, and no individual is more entitled than another. al-Dasuqi: [the phrase 'in those individuals' means] that the individuals are equal in the realization of its meaning without any difference in priority, precedence, intensity, or weakness, so his statement 'and its truth upon them' is an explanatory apposition according to intent, and it became clear to you that what is meant by 'truth' here is actualization, not predication, because the meaning is not predicated, and it would have been better to omit it. (His statement: 'like human' is an example of the univocal whose individuals are external, and 'sun' is an example of that whose individuals are mental, for their truth applies to their mental and external individuals equally. (His statement: 'for their truth' means the truth of their meaning, i.e., the realization of their meaning, and his statement 'upon their individuals' means in their individuals, and 'upon' is in the sense of 'in'. (His statement: 'and no individual is more entitled than another' means by that concept, not by priority, precedence, intensity, or anything else. al-‘Attar: Zayd is more intense or older or more entitled to humanity than ‘Amr, according to what is reported from 'Buhayman' that the criterion of graded (mushakkik) is the use of the superlative form, and that judgment does not depend on it being the complete essence of its individuals or on its essence being 'rational animal' or something else, as was mistakenly thought. End quote. Abd al-Hakim: and what is meant by 'truth' is univocal predication, for the universal is predicated of its individuals by this predication, so the univocal is like 'human' in relation to its individuals, and 'humanity' in relation to its individuals which are the shares, not like 'humanity' in relation to the individuals of 'human', so univocity occurs in derivatives and principles, unlike gradation; it occurs only in derivatives, as Mīr Zāhid clarified. Section: on Discussions of Words. And it is called 'mutawāti'' (univocal) because of the agreement of individuals in its meaning, from 'tawātu'' which means agreement. al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'because of the agreement of individuals in its meaning' means in the meaning of that single word, and its meaning is the universal matter, so the share of Zayd from humanity is like the share of ‘Amr from it, and the amount obtained from it in the knowledgeable is like that obtained in the ignorant, and that obtained in the prophets is like that obtained in... al-‘Attar: [regarding] imaginary individuals: the univocal would be restricted to imaginary universals like negations of comprehensive concepts. Abu al-Fath answered: by intending the first meaning because it is the obvious one, and specifying the division so that words placed for imaginary universals and universals confined to one individual with impossibility of another are excluded, due to their lack of prevalence in discourse; or that what is intended by 'the individuals' difference in the truth of the meaning upon them' is its obvious meaning, and the equality of individuals in the truth upon them is interpreted as the negation of that difference, whether the meaning has no truth in itself upon them, or it does and there is no difference in itself, in which case the mentioned [universals] enter into the univocal. End quote. The meaning of the second answer is that we interpret equality as the absence of difference, so a negative proposition results: the univocal does not have its individuals differing, and the negative proposition is true by the negation of the subject. This answer is the chosen one. Abd al-Hakim said: The claim that the word 'nothing' is not called a universal, and that what is considered in univocity and gradation is truth in itself, and that imaginary universals are outside both divisions, has no evidence in their speech and no benefit to that, especially since the Shaykh said in al-Shifa': 'The universal only becomes universal by having a relation, either by existence or by the validity of imagination, to particulars upon which it is predicated.' End quote. The imaginary universal is that which has no individual in the external world nor in the mind; therefore they said: the non-existent externally is a mental universal, and the non-existent externally and mentally is an imaginary universal. They give as an example 'nothing' and 'the impossible with general possibility'. It was restricted to 'general' because it encompasses all things: the necessary, the impossible, and the possible, unlike specific possibility, for it applies only to the latter. So if the negative particle enters upon general possibility, it becomes an imaginary universal. An example of the imaginary universal in forms is the aforementioned universal confined to one individual with the impossibility of another, like the Necessary and the Eternal by essence.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is primarily a definitional expansion of the concept of 'mutawāti'' (univocal term), a key division of universals in logic. The author begins by providing examples: 'human' (with external individuals) and 'sun' (with mental individuals), stating that the term applies equally to all its individuals without one being more entitled. The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies that 'truth' (ṣidq) here means 'realization' (taḥaqquq) of the meaning in the individuals, not logical predication (ḥaml), because the meaning itself is not predicated; it is the term that is predicated. This is a subtle but important point: the focus is on the meaning's instantiation. Next, the commentator elaborates on the examples, explaining that 'upon' (ʿalā) in 'truth upon them' is used in the sense of 'in' (fī). The condition of equality is further specified: no individual is prior, more intense, or more entitled in the meaning itself, though they may differ in external attributes like intensity or age. Al-ʿAṭṭār then introduces the notion of mushakkik (graded terms), citing a source (Buhayman) that the criterion for gradation is the use of the superlative form, and notes that this judgment does not require that the common essence be fully realized in all individuals. ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm adds a clarification: what is meant by 'truth' here is 'univocal predication' (ḥaml al-muwaṭaʾa), and that univocity applies to both derivatives (like 'human') and principles (like 'humanity'), whereas gradation only occurs in derivatives. The discussion then shifts to a problem: if by 'individuals' we mean those in actual reality (fī nafs al-amr), then universals that have no actual individuals (like 'nothing') would fall outside both mutawāṭiʾ and mushakkik, yet they are still part of the universal division. The commentator al-ʿAṭṭār raises this issue, and a response attributed to Abū al-Fatḥ is presented. Two answers are given: first, restrict the division to terms used in common discourse, excluding such imaginary universals; second, reinterpret the equality condition negatively: mutawāṭiʾ means that its individuals do not differ in the truth of the meaning (i.e., the absence of difference), which allows imaginary universals to be included since they have no individuals to differ. The second answer is preferred. The passage concludes with a detailed explanation of the 'imaginary universal' (kullī farḍī): it is a universal that has no individuals in either the external world or the mind, exemplified by 'nothing' and 'impossible with general possibility'. It contrasts with 'mental universal' which has mental existence. The discussion ties back to logic by relating it to necessary and eternal entities. This is a deep exposition that guards against misunderstanding the scope of univocity and gradation, emphasizing that the logical division of universals must account for terms that denote non-existent concepts.

Key Terms
متواطئ (mutawāṭiʾ): A term that applies equally to all its individuals in the same sense, without any individual being more entitled or intense in the meaning; e.g., 'human' applies to Zayd and ʿAmr identically. [Note: Standard definition across logic works; no significant shift.]
صدق (ṣidq): Here means the 'realization' (taḥaqquq) of the meaning in the individuals, not logical predication (ḥaml). The commentator clarifies that it is the term that is predicated, not the meaning. [Note: In typical logic texts, ṣidq often means 'truth' as correspondence or predication; here it is glossed as taḥaqquq to avoid confusion.]
أفراد (afrād): The individuals of a universal, which can be external (concrete objects) or mental (concepts in the mind), and in the discussion also imaginary (with no existence at all). [Note: No significant shift.]
تشكيك (tashkīk): Gradation: a term that applies to its individuals in different degrees or priorities (e.g., 'existence' applied to God and creation). The criterion is the use of the superlative form. [Note: No significant shift.]
كلي فرضي (kullī farḍī): An imaginary universal that has no individuals at all, neither externally nor mentally, e.g., 'nothing' or 'impossible with general possibility'. [Note: This term appears in later logical works; earlier texts may not distinguish imaginary universals as a separate category.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator al-Dasuqi, what does 'ṣidq' (truth) mean in the definition of mutawāṭiʾ?
A) The act of predication (ḥaml) of the term
B) The realization (taḥaqquq) of the meaning in the individuals
C) The external existence of the individuals
D) The mental existence of the individuals

Key Takeaway
A univocal term (mutawāṭiʾ) applies equally to all its individuals, and its 'truth' refers to the realization of its meaning, not predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the term 'zakāh' in Islamic law. Is it mutawāṭiʾ or mushakkik? Provide a brief justification based on the condition of equality and the criterion of the superlative form.
?
Identify the term 'razīq' (provision) as used in the Qur'an. Does it apply equally to all recipients (rich and poor), or does it allow gradation? Use the definition from this lesson.

The Five Universals
advanced
10 min
Imaginary Individuals and the Scope of Univocals
Resolves objection about imaginary universals and defines kullī farḍī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the problem of imaginary universals (kullī farḍī) and evaluate the two proposed solutions for including them under mutawāṭiʾ.

Core Idea
Imaginary universals like 'nothing' have no individuals; they still fit mutawāṭiʾ via negative equality.

This lesson addresses a subtle challenge: how can a term like 'nothing' be a universal if it has no actual individuals? The commentators debate whether such imaginary universals fall under mutawāṭiʾ or are excluded. Two answers are given: one restricts the division to common discourse, another interprets univocity as the absence of difference among individuals (a negative condition). The second answer is preferred, ensuring that all universals are covered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection states that if we consider only actual individuals (external and mental), universals with no actual individuals (like 'nothing') would be neither mutawāṭiʾ nor mushakkik, yet they are universals. Find where al-ʿAṭṭār raises this point.
2
Examine the first answer
Abu al-Fath's first answer: restrict the division of al-mutawāṭiʾ to terms used in common discourse, thereby excluding unusual terms like 'nothing'. Evaluate if this is satisfactory.
3
Examine the second answer
The second answer: interpret the equality condition negatively. Instead of 'individuals are equal in the truth', we say 'individuals do not differ in the truth'. This negative proposition is true when the subject has no existence, so imaginary universals are included.
4
Understand the preferred answer and the definition of kullī farḍī
The second answer is chosen. Then the passage defines kullī farḍī as having no individual externally or mentally, with examples 'nothing' and 'impossible with general possibility'. Note the contrast with mental universal.

كالإنسانِ والشَّمس، فإنَّ صِدْقَهُما على أفرادهما الذهنيَّةِ والخارجيَّةِ بالسَّويَّة، وليس بعضُ الأفراد أولى من بعض. الدسوقي فيها؛ أي: في تلك الأفراد؛ أي: إن استوَتِ الأفراد في تحققِ معناه فيها من غيرِ تفاوت بأولية، أو أولوية، أو شدة، أو ضعف، فقوله: (وصدقه عليها) عطف تفسير بحسب المراد، وظهر لك أنَّ المراد بالصِّدقِ هُنَا : التَّحقُّقُ لَا الحملُ ; لأنَّ المعنى لا يحمل، وكانَ الأَوْلَى حذفه. (قَوْلُهُ : كَالْإِنْسَانِ) مثال للمتواطئ الذي أفراده خارجيَّة، والشَّمسُ مثالٌ لِمَا أفرادُهُ ذهنيَّة، فإنَّ صدقَهُما على أفرادهما الذهنية والخارجية بالسوية. (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّ صِدْقَهُمَا ) أي : فإِنَّ صدق معناهما ; أي: تحقُّقُ مَعناهُما، وقوله : على أفرادِهِمَا)؛ أي: فى أفرادِهِمَا، و «على» بمعنى في. على ما نُقِلَ عَن «بهمينار» أنَّ معيار التّشكيكِ استعمال صيغة التفضيل، ولا يتوقَّفُ ذلكَ الحُكْمُ على كَونِهِ تمام حقيقةِ أفرادِهِ وعلى كَوْنِ حقيقتِهِ الحيوانَ النَّاطِق أو غيرها على ما وهم ا.هـ. عبد الحكيم، والمراد بالصدقِ حَمْلُ المواطأة; إذ الكُلّي محمول على أفراده بهذا الحَمْلَ، فالمتواطِئُ كَالإنسانِ بالنِّسبة إلى أفرادِهِ، والإنسانيَّةُ بالنسبة إلى أفرادِهَا وهي الحصص لا كالإنسانيَّةِ بالنِّسبة إلى أفرادِ الإنسانِ، فالتَّواطؤ يتحقَّقُ في المشتقات والمبادي بخلافِ التّشكيكِ ; فإنَّهُ يتحقَّقُ في المشتقَاتِ فَقَطْ كَمَا حَرَّرَهُ مير زاهد. فَصْلٌ: في مباحثِ الألفاظ وسُمِّي متواطئاً ; لتوافق الأفراد في معناه منَ التَّواطؤ، وهو التَّوافق . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : لِتَوَافُقِ الْأَفْرَادِ فِي مَعْنَاهُ أي : في معنى ذلك اللفظ المفرد، ومعناه: هو الأمرُ الكُلّي، فحظ زيدٍ مِنَ الإنسانيَّةِ كَحطّ عمرو منها، والقدر الحاصل منها في العالِمِ كَالحاصل منها في الجاهل، والحاصل منها في الأنبياء كالحاصل منها في العطار الأفراد الفرضيَّةَ؛ انحصر المتواطئ في الكُلِّيَّاتِ الفرضيَّةِ كَنقائِضِ المفهوماتِ الشَّاملةِ، وأجابَ أبو الفتح بإرادة المعنى الأوَّلِ ; لأنَّهُ المتبادرُ، وتخصيص المقسم بحيثُ يخرج عنه الألفاظ الموضوعةُ بإزاء الكُلِّيَّاتِ الفرضيَّةِ والكُلِّيَّاتِ المنحصرة في فردٍ ; مَعَ امتناعِ الغَيْرِ لِعَدَمِ اشتهارها في المحاوراتِ، أو أنْ يُرادَ بِتَفاوتِ الأفرادِ في صِدْقِ المعنى عليها معناه المتبادرُ، ويؤوَّلُ تساوي الأفراد في صدقه عليها بسلب ذلك التَّفاوتِ ; سواءٌ لمْ يَكُنْ لِلمعنى صِدْقٌ في نَفْسِ الأمر عليها ; أو كان ولم يكن فيهِ تفاوت في نَفْسِ الأمرِ، وحينئذ تدخل المذكوراتُ في المتواطئ ا.هـ. ومَعنَى الجوابِ الثَّاني أنَّا نؤوّلُ التّساوي بعدم التَّفاوتِ فَتحصل قضيَّةٌ سالبة هي: المتواطئ ليست أفراده متفاوتةً، والقضية السالبة تصدق بنفي الموضوع، وهذا الجواب هُوَ المختارُ، قال عبد الحكيم : القولُ بأنَّ لفظ اللاشيء لا يُسمّى كُلَّيًّا، وأنَّ المعتبر في التَّواطئ والتّشكيكِ الصَّدقُ في نَفْسِ الأمر، والكُلِّيَّاتُ الفرضيَّةُ خارجةٌ عَنِ القِسْمَيْنِ مِمَّا لا شَاهِدَ عليهِ من كلامهم ولا فائدة إلى ذلك، كيف وقد قالَ الشَّيخُ في «الشّفاء»: الكُلِّيُّ إِنَّما يصيرُ كُليًّا بأنَّ لهُ نسبةً ما، إمَّا بالوجودِ وإِمَّا بصحَةِ التَّوهُم إلى جزئيَّاتٍ يُحمَلُ عليها .ا.هـ. والكُلّي الفرضي هو الذي لا يوجد له فرد لا في الخارج وَلَا فِي الذَّهَنِ; فَلِذَلِكَ الُوا : اللا موجود الخارجي كُلّي ذهني، واللا موجود الخارجي والذهني كُلِّيٌّ فرضي، ويمثلونَ لَهَا بِاللاشيء واللاممكن إمكاناً عاماً، وإنَّما قُيَّدَ بالعام; لأنَّه يتناول جميع الأشياء مِنَ الواجب والممتنع والممكن، بخلافِ الإمكانِ الخاص; Commentary
This passage is primarily a definitional expansion of the concept of 'mutawāti'' (univocal term), a key division of universals in logic. The author begins by providing examples: 'human' (with external individuals) and 'sun' (with mental individuals), stating that the term applies equally to all its individuals without one being more entitled. The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies that 'truth' (ṣidq) here means 'realization' (taḥaqquq) of the meaning in the individuals, not logical predication (ḥaml), because the meaning itself is not predicated; it is the term that is predicated. This is a subtle but important point: the focus is on the meaning's instantiation. Next, the commentator elaborates on the examples, explaining that 'upon' (ʿalā) in 'truth upon them' is used in the sense of 'in' (fī). The condition of equality is further specified: no individual is prior, more intense, or more entitled in the meaning itself, though they may differ in external attributes like intensity or age. Al-ʿAṭṭār then introduces the notion of mushakkik (graded terms), citing a source (Buhayman) that the criterion for gradation is the use of the superlative form, and notes that this judgment does not require that the common essence be fully realized in all individuals. ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm adds a clarification: what is meant by 'truth' here is 'univocal predication' (ḥaml al-muwaṭaʾa), and that univocity applies to both derivatives (like 'human') and principles (like 'humanity'), whereas gradation only occurs in derivatives. The discussion then shifts to a problem: if by 'individuals' we mean those in actual reality (fī nafs al-amr), then universals that have no actual individuals (like 'nothing') would fall outside both mutawāṭiʾ and mushakkik, yet they are still part of the universal division. The commentator al-ʿAṭṭār raises this issue, and a response attributed to Abū al-Fatḥ is presented. Two answers are given: first, restrict the division to terms used in common discourse, excluding such imaginary universals; second, reinterpret the equality condition negatively: mutawāṭiʾ means that its individuals do not differ in the truth of the meaning (i.e., the absence of difference), which allows imaginary universals to be included since they have no individuals to differ. The second answer is preferred. The passage concludes with a detailed explanation of the 'imaginary universal' (kullī farḍī): it is a universal that has no individuals in either the external world or the mind, exemplified by 'nothing' and 'impossible with general possibility'. It contrasts with 'mental universal' which has mental existence. The discussion ties back to logic by relating it to necessary and eternal entities. This is a deep exposition that guards against misunderstanding the scope of univocity and gradation, emphasizing that the logical division of universals must account for terms that denote non-existent concepts.

Key Terms
متواطئ (mutawāṭiʾ): A term that applies equally to all its individuals in the same sense, without any individual being more entitled or intense in the meaning; e.g., 'human' applies to Zayd and ʿAmr identically. [Note: Standard definition across logic works; no significant shift.]
صدق (ṣidq): Here means the 'realization' (taḥaqquq) of the meaning in the individuals, not logical predication (ḥaml). The commentator clarifies that it is the term that is predicated, not the meaning. [Note: In typical logic texts, ṣidq often means 'truth' as correspondence or predication; here it is glossed as taḥaqquq to avoid confusion.]
أفراد (afrād): The individuals of a universal, which can be external (concrete objects) or mental (concepts in the mind), and in the discussion also imaginary (with no existence at all). [Note: No significant shift.]
تشكيك (tashkīk): Gradation: a term that applies to its individuals in different degrees or priorities (e.g., 'existence' applied to God and creation). The criterion is the use of the superlative form. [Note: No significant shift.]
كلي فرضي (kullī farḍī): An imaginary universal that has no individuals at all, neither externally nor mentally, e.g., 'nothing' or 'impossible with general possibility'. [Note: This term appears in later logical works; earlier texts may not distinguish imaginary universals as a separate category.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which answer is chosen to include imaginary universals under mutawāṭiʾ?
A) Restrict the division to common discourse
B) Interpret equality as absence of difference
C) Exclude imaginary universals
D) Redefine individuals to include imaginary ones

Key Takeaway
Imaginary universals (kullī farḍī) are included in mutawāṭiʾ by interpreting the condition negatively: univocity means no difference among individuals.

The Four Categories of Names (al-Asma')
Al-Sharnubi enumerates the four categories of names with subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given Arabic word into one of the four main categories and identify its subcategory based on the relationship between word and meaning.

Core Idea
Names are classified by word and meaning unity/multiplicity, yielding four categories and nine total types.

Al-Sharnubi fills a gap in the main text by providing a complete taxonomy of names based on whether the word (lafz) and its meaning (ma'na) are singular or multiple. This classification is foundational for precise definition in logic and usul al-fiqh, as it allows jurists and theologians to identify whether a term is unambiguous, ambiguous, metaphorical, or transferred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two criteria
Recognize that the classification uses two criteria: the word (lafz) and the meaning (ma'na). Each can be either singular (muttaḥid) or multiple (muta‘addid).
2
Combine criteria to form four categories
Combine the two binary options: (1) word singular, meaning singular; (2) word singular, meaning multiple; (3) word multiple, meaning singular; (4) word multiple, meaning multiple. These are the four main categories.
3
Memorize first category subcategories
First category (word=1, meaning=1) includes: 'alam (proper noun), mutawati' (univocal, e.g., 'insan'), and mushakkik (ambiguous, e.g., 'wujud'). Understand each definition.
Mushakkik applies unequally (priority vs. posteriority).
4
Memorize second category subcategories
Second category (word=1, meaning>1) includes: mushtarak (homonym), manqul (transferred, e.g., 'salat' from supplication to prayer), haqiqah (literal), and majaz (metaphorical).
Mushtarak here means homonym, not synonym.
5
Recognize third and fourth categories
Third category (word>1, meaning=1): synonyms (mushtarak in the sense of multiple words for one meaning, e.g., ghadanfar, hizabr). Fourth category (word>1, meaning>1): divergents (mutabayin, e.g., 'insan' and 'faras').
Be careful: 'mushtarak' has two meanings depending on context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُ المصنّف : وإلا فَحَقِيقَةٌ اعلم أن المصنف لم يستوعب أقسام الاسم، وإليك بيانها : هي أربعة إجمالاً وتسعة تفصيلاً، الأوَّل: ما اتَّحد لفظه ومعناه وتحته ثلاثة؛ العلم والمتواطئ والمشكك . الثَّاني : ما اتَّحد لفظه وتعدّد معناه وتحته أربعة؛ المشترك والمنقول والحقيقة والمجاز . الثَّالث : عكس الثَّاني، أي ما تعدّد لفظه واتَّحد معناه؛ وهو المشترك كغضنفر وهِزَبْر وقشورة للحيوان المفترس. الرَّابع عكس الأوّل ؛ أي: ما تعدّد لفظه ومعناه وهو المتباين كإنسان وفرس. ا.هـ. الشَّرْنوبي.
The author's statement: 'Otherwise it is haqiqah'. Know that the author did not exhaust the divisions of the name. Here is its explanation: They are four in summary and nine in detail. First: what unifies in word and meaning, and under it are three: proper noun (alam), univocal (mutawati'), and ambiguous (mushakkik). Second: what unifies in word and multiplies in meaning, and under it are four: homonym (mushtarak), transferred (manqul), literal (haqiqah), and metaphorical (majaz). Third: the opposite of the second, i.e., what multiplies in word and unifies in meaning, and it is synonymous (mushtarak) like ghadanfar, hizabr, and qashura for the predatory animal. Fourth: the opposite of the first, i.e., what multiplies in word and meaning, and it is divergent (mutabayin) like 'insan' and 'faras'. End. By al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a footnote by al-Sharnubi that provides a complete classification of names (asma'). He begins by noting that the author's text did not exhaust all divisions, so he presents them. The divisions are based on two criteria: unity or multiplicity of the word (lafz) and unity or multiplicity of its meaning (ma'na). This yields four main categories. The first category is words that are one in both word and meaning. Under this are three sub-types: alam (proper noun, which designates a specific individual exclusively), mutawati' (univocal, like 'insan' meaning human applied to many equally), and mushakkik (ambiguous, like 'wujud' meaning existence, applied to different things with priority). The second category is words that are one in word but have multiple meanings. Under this are four sub-types: mushtarak (homonym, like 'ayn meaning eye, spring, etc.), manqul (transferred, like 'salat' originally meaning supplication but transferred to the ritual prayer), haqiqah (literal, the original meaning), and majaz (metaphorical, a secondary meaning). The third category is the opposite of the second: multiple words with one meaning, which is synonymy (mushtarak in the sense of synonyms, not to be confused with homonym). Examples given are ghadanfar, hizabr, and qashura, all meaning lion or predatory animal. The fourth category is the opposite of the first: multiple words with multiple meanings, which is mutabayin (divergent), like insan (human) and faras (horse), which have no relation. This enumeration provides a framework for understanding how words relate to meanings, essential for logic and usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
المتواطئ
المشكك
المشترك
المنقول
المتباين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under which main category does a proper noun like 'Arḍ' (a specific name) fall?
A) First category (word=1, meaning=1)
B) Second category (word=1, meaning>1)
C) Third category (word>1, meaning=1)
D) Fourth category (word>1, meaning>1)

Key Takeaway
The classification of names into four categories based on word-meaning relationship is essential for avoiding ambiguity in Islamic legal and theological discourse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the word 'wujud' (existence) used for both God and creation: which of the four categories and which subcategory does it belong to? Explain your reasoning.
?
Provide an example from the Qur'an or Hadith of a word that is manqul (transferred) and state its original and transferred meanings.

Defining the Particular: Conceptual Perspective vs. External Evidence
Defines the particular and resolves a critical objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition of perspective (qayd al-haythiyya) to correctly define the particular (al-juz'i) and refute the objection concerning the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud).

Core Idea
The particular is defined solely by its concept, not by external evidence.

This lesson refines the definition of the particular by addressing a key objection: if external evidence is considered, the Necessary Existent would be incorrectly classified as particular. The solution lies in the precise concept of 'haythiyya' (perspective).

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of the particular
Find the phrase in the Arabic: 'الجزئي ما يمتنع أن يحكم العقل بصدقه على كثيرين'. This is the core definition to be refined.
2
Identify the two interpretations of 'impossibility'
The objection states that 'impossibility' could be either from the mere concept or from external evidence. Note both possibilities in the text.
3
Understand the counterexample (Necessary Existent)
If external evidence is considered, the Necessary Existent would be considered particular because the mind, after evidence, cannot predicate Him of many. Recognize why this is problematic.
4
Apply the condition of perspective (haythiyya)
The answer restricts the definition: 'qayd al-haythiyya' means only the conceptual perspective is considered, excluding external evidence. This preserves the definition.
See the response: 'والجواب : أن قيد الحيثية مراعى'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصله : أن الجزئي ما يمتنع؛ أي: يستحيل أن يحكم العقل بصدقه؛ أي: حمله على كثيرين. وفيه : أن هذا صادق بأن يكون امتناع العقل من ذلك بالنظر لمجرد تصور مفهومه، بقطع النظر عن غيره، وصادق بأن يكون امتناع العقل من ذلك بالنظر لغيره أيضاً، كالنظر في الدليل. وهذا يوجب الخلل في تعريف الجزئي والكلي؛ لأن تعريف الجزئي يكون غير مانع من دخول واجب الوجود ونحوه فيه، ويصير تعريف الكلي غير جامع لذلك. والجواب : أن قيد الحيثية مراعى؛ أي: من حيث تصوره فقط؛ أي: لا من حيث تصوره مع ملاحظة الدليل.

العطار: أن يكون زيد كليا، لأنَّا نقول : إنَّما يلزم هذا لو كانت هذه الصورة من زيد معنى واحدا ذهنيا مطابقا لكثيرين في خارج الذهن، وظاهر أنها ليست كذلك؛ قاله الخلخالي، وفي الحاشية أن الصورة الحاصلة منه في كل ذهن إن أخذت مع قطع النظر عن الإضافة إلى المحل فمتحدة بالذات والمفهوم ولا تعدد فيها حتى تتحقق المطابقة، وإن أخذت مع الإضافة إلى المحل فلا نسلم التطابق والتصادق بينها؛ بل التباين. والجواب المذكور مسطور في شرح المصنف على الأصل.

قوله : على كثيرين قال أبو الفتح: للكثرة معنيان؛ أحدهما : ما يقابل الوحدة، وثانيهما : ما يقابل العلة، وكلاهما صحيح ههنا، إنما اختاروا جمع الكثرة بالياء والنون تنبيها على أن جميع الكليات متساوية باعتبار نفس التصور، حتى إنه ما من كلي إلا وهو صادق على ذوي عقول متكثرة بهذا الاعتبار، وإن كان مباينا لها بحسب نفس الأمر. ا.هـ.
And its summary is: The particular is that which is impossible—i.e., the mind cannot judge its truth—i.e., its predication of many. And in it [the definition] is: That this is true in the sense that the mind's inability to do so is by considering only the conception of its concept, disregarding other things, and it is also true in the sense that the mind's inability is by considering other things as well, such as considering evidence. And this causes defect in the definition of the particular and the universal; because the definition of the particular would not be exclusive enough to prevent the entry of the Necessary Existent and similar things into it, and the definition of the universal would not be comprehensive enough to include it. And the answer: That the condition of perspective is observed; i.e., only from the perspective of its conception; i.e., not from the perspective of its conception along with noting the evidence.

Al-Atar: That Zayd would be universal, because we say: This only follows if this form of Zayd were a single mental meaning corresponding to many outside the mind, and it is apparent that it is not like that; said by al-Khalikhali, and in the marginal commentary: The form obtained from him in each mind, if taken disregarding the relation to the subject, is identical in essence and concept and there is no multiplicity in it such that correspondence is realized; and if taken with the relation to the subject, we do not concede correspondence and convergence between them; rather, they are disparate. And the mentioned answer is written in the commentary of the author on the original.

His statement: 'to many'—Abu al-Fath said: For 'multitude' there are two meanings: one: what is opposite to unity, and the second: what is opposite to cause, and both are valid here. They chose the plural of 'multitude' with ya and nun to indicate that all universals are equal with respect to the conception itself, such that there is no universal except that it is true of many minds with this consideration, even if it is disparate with them according to the essential reality. End.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of the particular (al-juz'i) as given in the matn. It opens with al-Dasuqi's summary: 'the particular is that which it is impossible—i.e., the mind cannot judge its truth—i.e., its predication of many.' This is a concise, technically precise definition that hinges on the mind's inability to predicate the concept of multiple individuals.

An objection is then raised: This definition can be understood in two ways—either the mind's inability is based solely on the concept itself (disregarding any external evidence) or based on external evidence as well (like a rational proof). If the latter is allowed, then the definition fails: the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud) would be wrongly classified as particular, because the mind, after considering the proof of His oneness, cannot predicate Him of many. Conversely, the universal would not include Him. This poses a serious challenge.

The answer is that the condition of perspective (qayd al-haythiyya) is observed: only the conceptual perspective is considered, not the perspective that includes external evidence. Thus, the definition stands: the particular is that whose concept alone prevents predication of many.

Al-Atar then addresses a further objection: if many people conceive of Zayd, their mental images correspond to the same external Zayd, which might imply Zayd is universal. The response is that those mental forms, when considered apart from their relation to the subject, are essentially the same and not multiple; when considered with the relation, they are disparate and do not correspond to many external individuals. So Zayd remains particular.

Finally, the meaning of 'many' (kathira) is clarified: it can mean 'many in number' (opposite of one) or 'many in the sense of not being a cause' (opposite of 'illa). Both are acceptable here. The plural form 'kathirin' is used to emphasize that all universals are equal in being predicable of many minds, even if they differ in reality.

Throughout, the passage demonstrates the rigorous precision of the hashiya tradition, refining definitions to avoid logical pitfalls.

Key Terms
الجزئي
فرض الصدق
الحيثية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the objection that the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud) appears particular not refute the definition?
A) Because the Necessary Existent is not a concept but a real entity.
B) Because the definition considers only the conceptual perspective, not external evidence.
C) Because the Necessary Existent is truly a universal.
D) Because the objection misinterprets 'impossible'.

Key Takeaway
The definition of the particular must be understood exclusively from the conceptual perspective to avoid misclassifying the Necessary Existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of 'haram' (forbidden) as 'what the Lawgiver prohibits in a binding manner'. How would you apply the condition of perspective to prevent including acts that are simply harmful? Write one sentence explaining the refinement.

The Two Meanings of Kathra and the Equality of Universals
Clarifies the meaning of 'multitude' in universal definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of 'kathra' (multitude) and explain why universals are equal in being predicable of many minds.

Core Idea
Kathra can mean numerical multiplicity or the opposite of a cause; both support the universality of concepts.

This lesson clarifies the term 'kathra' (multitude) in the definition of the universal. Abu al-Fath highlights that 'kathra' has two meanings: opposite of unity and opposite of cause. Both are valid, and the plural form emphasizes that all universals are equally predicable of many minds, regardless of their external reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'kathra' and its two meanings
Find the phrase 'للکثرة معنیان' in the Arabic. Note that the two meanings are: (1) what is opposite of unity, (2) what is opposite of cause.
2
Understand why both meanings are acceptable
Both meanings are valid here because universality can be understood as predication of many individuals (numerical) or as not being a cause (i.e., the concept does not necessitate its instances).
3
Reflect on the use of the plural form 'kathirin'
The plural form is chosen to indicate that all universals are equal in being true of many minds, even if they differ in external reality. This emphasizes that universality is a property of the concept itself.
See: 'تنبيها على أن جميع الكليات متساوية باعتبار نفس التصور'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

beginner
6 min
True Particular and Additive Particular: Two Kinds of Particular
Author distinguishes true particular from additive particular and defines universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a true particular and an additive particular, and identify the universal in a given example.

Core Idea
A true particular cannot be predicated of many; an additive particular can be subsumed under a higher universal.

In this passage, the author sharpens our understanding of 'particular' by dividing it into two types: true particular and additive particular. The distinction is crucial because when we use the word 'particular' in logic, we usually mean the true particular — that which cannot be applied to more than one individual. But some terms, like 'man', are particular only in relation to a higher genus; they are additive particulars. Learning this distinction will prevent confusion when analyzing definitions and classifications in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines 'particular' (juz'i) and distinguishes its two types. Locate where the author introduces 'true particular' and 'additive particular'.
2
Extract the defining property of true particular
The true particular is defined as that which cannot be predicated of many — its essence, when conceived, prevents the mind from supposing it true of multiple individuals. Example: the essence of Zayd.
The impossibility is logical, not merely contingent.
3
Extract the defining property of additive particular
The additive particular is that which can be predicated of many, but is itself subsumed under something else. Example: 'man' is subsumed under 'animal'.
Every true particular is also additive, but not vice versa.
4
Connect to the definition of universal
The universal (kulli) is defined as that which does not prevent predication of many when conceived abstractly. Note the contrast: the universal can be true of many; the true particular cannot.
The universal is related to the whole (kull) as its parts are under it, but the universal itself is a part of its particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فجزئي حقيقي، كذات زيد فإنه إذا حصل عند العقل؛ استحال فرض صدقه على كثيرين. (والكلي) وإلا: أي وإن لم يمتنع بمجرد الحصول فرض صدقه على كثيرين. (قوله: فجزئي) نسبة للجزء، وهو كلية، كما أن الكلي نسبة للكل، وهو جزئية، فزيد مثلاً جزئي نسبة لجزئيه، وهو إنسان الذي هو كلية، وإنسان كلي نسبة للكل، وهو زيد مثلاً الذي هو جزئي من جزئيات الإنسان. (قوله: حقيقي) يخرج الجزئي الإضافي، فالجزئي الحقيقي: ما لم يندرج تحته شيء، واندرج هو تحت غيره كزيد. والجزئي الإضافي: ما اندرج تحت غيره كالإنسان، فإنه جزئي إضافي؛ لاندراجه تحت الحيوان، فكل جزئي حقيقي جزئي إضافي، ولا عكس. واعلم أن الجزئي متى أطلق انصرف للحقيقي، وتعريف المصنف للجزئي المراد عند الإطلاق، وحينئذ فلا يرد عليه الإضافي. (قوله: فإنه) أي ذات زيد، وذكر ضميرها؛ إما باعتبار أنها شيء، أو أنها مفهوم، وقوله: (استحال فرض صدقه؛ أي: امتنع حكم العقل بصدقه على كثيرين). (قوله: أي: وإن لم يمتنع) أي: وإن لم يمتنع حكم العقل بصدقه على كثيرين بالنظر لحصوله في العقل المجرد عن ملاحظة الدليل. (قوله: نسبة للجزء) توضيح ذلك أنهم يقولون: كل كلي جزء لجزئيه، وكل جزئي كل لكليه، فالإنسان مثلاً كلي تحته جزئيات كثيرة كزيد هذا الجزئي وهو زيد مركب من الإنسان ومن شيء آخر وهو التشخص الخارجي، فأنت ترى الإنسان الكلي جزءاً من حقيقة زيد الذي هو جزئية، وترى أيضاً زيداً الجزئي كلاً للإنسان الذي هو كلية لتركبه منه ومن التشخص، ومعلوم أن الكل ما تحته أجزاء كالحصير، والكلي ما تحته جزئيات كالإنسان. الشنوبي.
So it is a true particular, like Zayd's essence, for when it occurs in the mind, it is impossible to suppose its truth about many. (And the universal) otherwise: i.e., if it is not impossible by mere occurrence to suppose its truth about many. (His statement: 'so a particular') is a relation to the part, and it is a universality, just as the universal is a relation to the whole, and it is a particularity. So Zayd, for example, is a particular in relation to his part, which is 'man' that is a universality, and 'man' is a universal in relation to the whole, which is Zayd for example, who is a particular among the particulars of man. (His statement: 'true') excludes the additive particular. So the true particular is that under which nothing is subsumed, and it is subsumed under something else, like Zayd. The additive particular is that which is subsumed under something else, like 'man', for it is an additive particular because it is subsumed under 'animal'. So every true particular is an additive particular, and not vice versa. Know that when 'particular' is mentioned absolutely, it refers to the true particular, and the author's definition of the particular is intended when used absolutely, and thus the additive one does not pose a problem. (His statement: 'for it') i.e., Zayd's essence, and the pronoun for it is mentioned either considering it as a thing or as a concept. His statement: 'it is impossible to suppose its truth' i.e., the mind's judgment of its truth about many is prevented. (His statement: 'i.e., if it is not impossible') i.e., if the mind's judgment of its truth about many is not prevented by considering its occurrence in the mind abstracted from observing a proof. (His statement: 'is a relation to the part') explanation of that: they say: every universal is a part of its particulars, and every particular is a whole for its two parts? [check translation] Actually: 'every universal is a part of its particulars, and every particular is a whole for its two parts'? The text says 'كل كلي جزء لجزئيه، وكل جزئي كل لكليه' – 'every universal is a part of its particulars, and every particular is a whole for its two things'? The example clarifies: man is a universal under which are many particulars like Zayd, and Zayd is composed of man and something else which is external individuation. So you see the universal 'man' is a part of the reality of Zayd who is a particularity, and you also see Zayd the particular as a whole for 'man' which is a universality because Zayd is composed of man and individuation. And it is known that the whole is that under which are parts, like a mat, and the universal is that under which are particulars, like man. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage defines the true particular (juz'i haqiqi) and the additive particular (juz'i idafi), and then defines the universal (kulli). Starting with the definition: a true particular is something that, when conceived in the mind, cannot be supposed to be true of many (e.g., Zayd's essence). The universal is the opposite: that which does not prevent such predication. The commentator then explains the terminology: 'juz'i' (particular) is a relational term meaning 'related to a part' (juz'), and 'kulli' (universal) means 'related to a whole' (kull). Examples: Zayd is particular in relation to his part 'man', and 'man' is universal in relation to Zayd. The word 'haqiqi' (true) excludes additive particulars. A true particular is that under which nothing is subsumed, but it is itself subsumed under something else (e.g., Zayd under 'man'). An additive particular is that which is subsumed under something else (e.g., 'man' under 'animal'). Importantly, every true particular is also additive, but not vice versa. When 'particular' is used without qualification, it means the true particular, so the definition given earlier applies only to that. The passage also clarifies that 'impossibility of predication' means the mind cannot judge it to be true of many, considering the concept alone. The final note by al-Sharnubi explains the part-whole relationship: the universal is part of its particular, and the particular is a whole composed of the universal and individuation; thus the universal is a part, not a whole in the sense of having parts under it. This distinguishes 'kull' (whole) from 'kulli' (universal).

الجزئي الحقيقي
الجزئي الإضافي
الكلي
الكل
التشخص

D) 'Jism' (body)

Key Takeaway
The key distinction: a true particular cannot be true of many individuals, while an additive particular can be subsumed under a higher universal; the universal, by contrast, can be predicated of many.

Real Particular and Universal: The Criterion of Sharing
Definitions of juz'i haqiqi and kulli

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a real particular from a universal using the criterion of possibility of supposing sharing (imkan fard al-ishtirak).

Core Idea
Universality is the possibility of being shared; particularity is the impossibility.

In this passage, al-Dasuqi defines two fundamental categories: the real particular and the universal. The key is not actual sharing but the mental possibility. Let's break down the definitions and see how this criterion works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiens
Find the definitions of real particular and universal in the Arabic passage. Note the condition: 'when it occurs in the mind, it is impossible to suppose its truth about many' for particular, and the negation for universal.
2
Contrast impossibility vs possibility
Real particular makes supposition of truth about many impossible; universal makes it not impossible. This is the core distinction.
3
Understand 'imkan fard al-ishtirak'
Universality is defined as the possibility of supposing sharing, not actual predication. This is a mental possibility.
4
Apply to examples
Zayd is a real particular because you cannot suppose 'Zayd' true of anyone else; 'human' is universal because you can suppose it true of many.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَجُزْئِي حقيقي، كذاتِ زيدٍ فإنَّه إذا حصل عند العقل؛ استحال فرضُ صدقه على كثيرين. (وَإِلَّا) : أي : وإن لم يمتنع بمجرد الحصول فرضُ صدقه على كثيرين فَكُلِّي. فالكلِّيَّةُ إمكانُ فرضِ الاشتراك، والجزئية استحالته.
ثم أورد عليه: أنَّ كلَّ جزئي إذا تصوَّرَهُ طائفةٌ فالصُّورة الحاصلة في ذهن زيدٍ مثلاً مُطابقةٌ لِلصُّورة الحاصلة في ذهن الآخرين، فيجبُ أنْ يكونَ كُلِّيًّا. والجواب: أنَّ معنى شركة الكثير: أن يكونَ الكثيرون أفراده، ويعتبر هو مطابقاً لها وصادقاً عليها، والصُّورتان الحاصلتان في ذهن زيدٍ وعمرو إنْ أُخِذَنَا معَ قطعِ النَّظر عن الإضافة إلى المحلّين فهما مُتَّحدانِ بالذَّاتِ والمفهوم ولا اثنينيَّة؛ أي: لا تعدُّدَ بينهما حتَّى يتحقَّق المطابقة، وإن أخذنا مع اعتبار الإضافة إلى المحلّين فَلَا تتم المطابقةُ والتَّصادِقُ بينهما لِمَا بينَ تلكَ الصُّورِ مِن التَّباين.
So a real particular, like the essence of Zayd, for when it occurs in the mind, it is impossible to suppose its truth about many. (Otherwise) meaning: if it is not impossible by mere occurrence to suppose its truth about many, then it is universal. So universality is the possibility of supposing sharing, and particularity is its impossibility. Then an objection was made against it: that every particular, if a group conceives it, the form occurring in the mind of Zayd, for example, corresponds to the form occurring in the minds of others, so it must be universal. The answer: that the meaning of sharing by many is that the many are its individuals, and it is considered corresponding to them and true of them; the two forms occurring in the minds of Zayd and 'Amr, if taken disregarding relation to the two loci, are identical in essence and concept with no duality; meaning there is no multiplicity between them so that correspondence is realized; and if taken considering relation to the two loci, then correspondence and truth-relation are not complete between them because of the difference between those forms.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage defines the real particular and the universal, and addresses an objection. The author (al-Dasuqi, commenting on the matn) begins by giving the definition of a real particular (juz'i haqiqi): it is a concept such that when it occurs in the mind, it is impossible for the intellect to suppose it true of multiple individuals. The example is the essence of Zayd; Zayd as an individual cannot be predicated of many. Then the opposite is defined: if it is not impossible to suppose its truth of many, then it is universal. The author then clarifies that universality (kulliyya) is the possibility of supposing sharing (imkan fard al-ishtirak) — not actual sharing, but the mental possibility. Particularity is the impossibility of that. Next, an objection is raised: if a particular like Zayd is conceived by multiple people, the mental form in each mind corresponds to the form in the others, so that form would seem to be universal. The answer distinguishes two ways of considering the mental forms. If we disregard their relation to the individual minds, the two forms are identical in essence and concept — there is no multiplicity, so there is no 'correspondence' between distinct things that could make one true of many. If we consider them as related to their respective minds, they are distinct due to the relation, so correspondence fails. Therefore, the mental form of a particular remains particular, not universal. This reinforces the definition of universality as a property of the concept itself, not of individual mental representations.

Key Terms
جزئي حقيقي
كلي
إمكان فرض الاشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes a concept a real particular (juz'i haqiqi)?
A) It refers to a specific physical object.
B) When the mind obtains it, it is impossible to suppose it true of many.
C) It cannot be conceived by more than one person.
D) It is a part of a larger category.

Key Takeaway
A real particular is a concept that cannot be supposed true of many individuals; a universal is one for which such supposition is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the concept 'the first man, Adam'. Is it a real particular or a universal? Explain using the criterion from this passage.

The Objection of Multiple Minds: Why Mental Forms Don't Make Particulars Universal
Objection and response about mental forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze an objection against the definition of particularity and evaluate the response using the distinction between the form considered in itself and as related to a mind.

Core Idea
Mental forms of a particular are identical in essence, so they cannot correspond as universal to many.

An objector claims that if multiple people conceive a particular like Zayd, the mental form in each mind corresponds to the others, so it seems that form could be shared—making it universal. Al-Dasuqi's answer reveals a subtle logical distinction.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objector says: when many conceive a particular, the mental form in each mind is identical or matches, so it should be universal.
2
Identify the assumption
The objector assumes that identity of forms implies universality.
3
Analyze the answer's two cases
The answer considers forms (a) disregarding relation to minds → they are identical, so no multiplicity; (b) considering relation → they are different, so no correspondence.
4
Conclude
Therefore, the mental form of a particular remains particular, not universal.

Source Text

Distinguishing the Concept of 'Particular' from Its Instances
Author refutes the objection using semantic distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the concept of 'particular' and its instances in a dialectical argument, and identify the equivocation fallacy.

Core Idea
The term 'particular' is a universal concept; its instances are not.

One of the trickiest objections in the five universals is the claim that the particular itself is universal. This passage shows how the logician resolves the apparent contradiction by carefully distinguishing between the word or concept 'particular' and the actual individuals it describes. Understanding this distinction is essential for precise definition and debate in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector argues: the particular does not prevent being predicated of many (Zayd, Bakr are all called 'particular'), so it is universal. But that leads to a contradiction because the particular would be both particular and universal. Trace the objector's reasoning: minor premise 'the particular can be predicated of many' leads to conclusion 'the particular is universal'.
The objector assumes a single meaning of 'particular'.
2
Locate the key distinction
The responder distinguishes two meanings of 'particular': (a) the instances (ma sadaqa 'alayhi) like Zayd and Amr, and (b) the word or concept (lafz al-juz'i). Note that al-Dasuqi clarifies that if 'particular' means the instances, the minor premise is false (instances are not predicable of many). If it means the concept, the conclusion is not impossible because the concept is universal.
This is a semantic distinction—equivocation is resolved.
3
Apply the distinction
Test both interpretations: Under interpretation (a), the argument fails because the minor premise is false. Under interpretation (b), the argument is valid but not problematic—the concept 'particular' is indeed universal. No contradiction remains.
The distinction saves the classification of the particular as a type of universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قُلْتَ: الجزئي لا يمتنع بمجرَّدِ حصوله في العقل فرض صدقه على كثيرين، وكلُّ مَا كانَ كذلك ; فهو كلّي، فالجزئي كلّي، وهو محالٌ. قُلْتُ : المراد من الجزئي إن كانَ ما صَدَقَ عليه لفظ الجزئي من نحو زيد وغيره، فلا نسلّمُ الصُّغرى. وإن كانَ المراد لفظ الجزئي ; الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنْ قُلْتَ : ... إلخ) هذا مُعارضةٌ واردةٌ على جعل الجزئي قسيماً لِلكُلّيّ . وحاصلها : لا نسلّمُ أَنَّ الجزئي قسيم لِلكُلّي، بل الجزئي كُلِّيِّ، فهو فردّ مِن أفراده؛ لا قسيم له. (قَوْلُهُ : لَا يَمْتَنِعُ بِمُجَرَّدِ حُصُوْلِهِ فِي الْعَقْلِ ... إِلَخ) أي : لأَنَّهُ يصدقُ على زيد أنَّهُ جزئي، وعلى بكرٍ أنَّه جزئي، وهكذا. (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ) أي : لأَنَّهُ يلزمُ عليه اتِّصافُ الشَّيء بنقيضه، وهو جَمْعُ بينَ النقيضين . (قَوْلُهُ: قُلْتُ : الْمُرَادُ . . . إِلَخ) حاصلُهُ : إِن أردتَ بالجزئي الذي لا يمتنع فرضُ صدقِهِ على كثيرين زيداً وعَمْراً ; فَلَا نسلّمُ الصُّغرى، وإنْ كانَ المراد لفظ الجزئي ; فَلَا نسلّم استحالةَ النَّتيجة؛ إذ يصدقُ على زيدٍ أَنَّهُ جزئي ، وعلى بكرٍ أنَّه جزئي، وهكذا. (قَوْلُهُ : مَا صَدَقَ عَلَيْهِ ... إلخ) أي: وهي أفراده، والحاصلُ : أَنَّ المصدَّق غيرُ المفهوم كإنسان؛ فإنَّ ما صدقه : زيد وعمرو، ومفهومه: حيوان ناطق . (قَوْلُهُ : لَفْظُ الْجُزْنِيّ) أي: من حيثُ معناه، ولو قال : وإِنْ كانَ المراد مفهوم لفظ الجزئي ; كانَ أَوْلَى؛ لأنَّهُ أنسبُ بِما قبله؛ ولأنَّ الكُلِّيَّ إِنَّما يلزم مفهوم هذا اللَّفظِ لَا نفْسَه ويمكنُ أنْ يُقالَ : إِنَّهُ على حذف مضاف؛ أي : وإِنْ كانَ المراد مفهوم لفظ الجزئي. العطار هذا القسم مِنَ الفردِ المنتشر ; هُوَ الاشتراك على سبيل البدلية دون الاجتماع; لأنَّ الوحدةَ مُعتبرة فيه ا.هـ. وأجابَ الدَّوَانيُّ بجواب آخَرَ نقلَهُ المحشّي وقدحَ فيه، وهوَ مِنَ التَّعفيرِ في وجوهِ الحِسَانِ ومَنشَؤُهُ عدم تدبُّر كلامِهِ .
If you say: The particular does not prevent, by its mere occurrence in the mind, the supposition of its truth concerning many things, and everything that is like that is universal, so the particular is universal, which is impossible. I say: The intended meaning of 'particular' if it is that which the word 'particular' applies to, such as Zayd and others, then we do not accept the minor premise. And if the intended meaning is the word 'particular'... al-Dasuqi: His saying 'If you say...' etc.: This is an objection directed against making the particular a counterpart of the universal. Its summary: We do not accept that the particular is a counterpart of the universal; rather the particular is universal, so it is an individual of it, not a counterpart. His saying 'It does not prevent by its mere occurrence in the mind...' etc.: That is because it is true of Zayd that he is particular, and of Bakr that he is particular, and so on. His saying 'which is impossible': That is because it entails the description of a thing by its opposite, which is combining two opposites. His saying 'I say: The intended meaning...' etc.: Its summary: If you mean by 'particular' that which does not prevent the supposition of its truth concerning many things, like Zayd and Amr, then we do not accept the minor premise; and if the intended meaning is the word 'particular', then we do not accept the impossibility of the result, because it is true of Zayd that he is particular, and of Bakr that he is particular, and so on. His saying 'that which the word applies to': That is, its individuals. The summary: That which is applied to is other than the concept, like 'human'; what it applies to is Zayd and Amr, and its concept is 'rational animal'. His saying 'the word “particular”': That is, in terms of its meaning. If he had said 'if the intended meaning is the concept of the word “particular”', it would have been better, because it is more appropriate to what came before, and because the universal only applies to the concept of this word, not to itself. It can be said that there is a deleted genitive construct; i.e., if the intended meaning is the concept of the word “particular”. al-Attar: This type is the scattered individual; it is common in the mode of substitution, not conjunction, because unity is considered in it. He said. And al-Dawani answered with another answer that the annotator quoted and criticized, and it is like prostrating on beautiful faces, and its source is lack of reflection on his words.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (objection and response) concerning the classification of the particular (الجزئي) and the universal (الكلي). The objector argues that the particular is universal because the term 'particular' can be predicated of many individuals (Zayd, Bakr, etc.), so it does not prevent sharing, which is a characteristic of universals. This leads to a contradiction: the particular would be both particular and universal. The response distinguishes between the particular as an instance (ma sadaqa 'alayhi) and the particular as a concept or word (lafz al-juz'i). If by 'particular' you mean the individuals to which the word applies, then we do not accept the minor premise that 'the particular does not prevent sharing' because those individuals are not predicable of many. If you mean the word itself (or its concept), then the conclusion that 'the particular is universal' is not impossible because the word 'particular' as a concept is indeed universal. Al-Dasuqi elaborates: the objection tries to make the particular a counterpart of the universal, but in reality the particular (as a concept) is a universal and is an individual of the universal. He clarifies that the impossibility lies in describing a thing by its opposite, but here the predication is not problematic because we are not saying an individual Zayd is universal; we are saying the concept of particular is universal. Al-Attar adds a note about the 'scattered individual' (al-fard al-muntashir), which is a type of universal that applies to many in a substitutive manner (badaliyya), not conjunctively (ijtima'), because unity is preserved in each instance. He then criticizes al-Dawani's alternative answer, calling it an ugly face-prostration, indicating it lacks careful reflection.

Key Terms
جزئي
كلي
ما صدق عليه
الفرد المنتشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the objection-response, what is the key distinction that resolves the claim that the particular is universal?
A) Distinguishing between essential and accidental predicates
B) Distinguishing between the concept of 'particular' and its instances
C) Distinguishing between universal and particular in the mind
D) Distinguishing between logical and grammatical terms

Key Takeaway
The key to resolving apparent contradictions in the classification of universals is to carefully separate the concept from its instances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the term 'wajib' (obligatory). Its instances include salat, zakat, etc. The concept 'wajib' is universal. Explain why it is correct to say 'wajib is a universal' but not 'salat is universal'.
?
Identify the equivocation in the following argument: 'The word "kulli" is universal, so everything universal is called "kulli".'

The Second Division of Universal: Possible but Non-Existent
Defining the second division of universal with the example of al-'Anqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second division of universal and distinguish it from the first division based on the existence of its individuals.

Core Idea
Not all universals need existing individuals; possibility alone suffices.

In the classification of universals, we have seen that some have existing individuals. But what about universals like the mythical 'Anqa? In this passage, the author defines the second division: a universal whose individuals are possible in existence but have never been actualized. Let's explore this category with the help of the commentators.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the exact Arabic phrase: 'وَ) لكن (لَمْ تُوْجَدْ في الخارج، وهو القسمُ الثَّاني، كالعنقاء، فإنَّه كلّي ممكن الأفراد، لكنَّها لم تُوجَد في الخارج.' This defines the second division.
The condition is that the individuals are possible (mumkin) but not actualized externally.
2
Recognize the key condition
Note the term 'mumkin al-afrad' (possible individuals) – possibility is necessary, not impossibility or necessity.
This distinguishes from the first division where individuals exist, and from impossible universals (third division).
3
Examine the example
Understand why the 'Anqa (a mythical bird with forty heads) is used: it is a universal concept with possible individuals, yet none are found in external existence.
The example is purely hypothetical to illustrate the logical category.
4
Note al-Dusuqi's grammatical clarification
Al-Dusuqi suggests rephrasing 'wa huwa al-qism al-thani' to 'fa-huwa al-qism al-thani' to make it the answer to an implied condition.
This highlights the importance of precise wording in logical definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ) لكن (لَمْ تُوْجَدْ في الخارج، وهو القسمُ الثَّاني، كالعنقاء، فإنَّه كلّي ممكن الأفراد، لكنَّها لم تُوجَد في الخارج. الدسوقي: قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ الْقِسْمُ الثَّانِي الأَوْلَى : فهوَ القسمُ الثَّاني؛ لأنَّ الشَّرِطَ مُقدَّرُ، فيكون هذا جوابَ الشَّرط، وهكذا يُقالُ فيما سيأتي . قَوْلُهُ : كَالْعَنْقَاءِ هي طائرُ له أربعونَ رأساً ، يخطفُ الصغار، وَلَا شَكٍّ أنَّ هذا كُليّ، قِيلَ: إنَّ عدم وجودِهَا إِنَّما هو في آخرِ الزَّمان، وإنَّها كانَتْ موجودةً في زمن سيّدنَا سُليمان، وإنَّها كانَتْ تُكذِّبُ بالقضاء والقدَرِ، فدعا عليها سليمان، فقطعَ الله نسلها. العطار: قَوْلُهُ : كَالْعَنْقَاءِ وبحرٍ مِن زئبق، وجبل من ياقوت، وكانَ التَّمثيل بهذه الأمورِ لمجرَّدِ الفرض، وإلا ؛ فكيفَ يعلمُ أنَّ مثل هذه الأمورِ ممكنه الوجود ولم توجد أبداً؟! قالَهُ المحشّي، وتعقب بأنَّ إمكانَ مَا ذكر قطعي؛ إذ لا يلزم على تقديرِ وجودِهِ مُحال، وليس يلزم من عدم وجودِ الشَّيء عدم إمكانه ا.هـ. ، والمتعقِّبُ نظر لمجرّدِ قولِهِ : ممكنه الوجودِ؛ فحلَّلَ المركَّبَ، واعترض، وإلا ؛ فَالاستفهامُ عَن مجموع الأمرين إمكان وجودِهَا ؛ معَ كونها معدومةٌ، إذ يجوز أن تكون موجودةً ولم نَطَّلع عليها.
(And) but (they are not found in external existence), and it is the second division, like the Anqa, for it is a universal whose individuals are possible, but they are not found in external existence. al-Dusuqi: His saying: 'And it is the second division' - the better is 'so it is the second division' because the condition is implied, so this is the answer to the condition, and likewise it is said in what follows. His saying: 'like the Anqa' - it is a bird with forty heads, snatching children, and there is no doubt that this is a universal. It is said that its non-existence is only in the later times, and that it existed in the time of our master Solomon, and that it used to deny predestination and decree, so Solomon prayed against it, and Allah cut off its progeny. al-'Attar: His saying: 'like the Anqa' and a sea of mercury, a mountain of ruby, and these examples were merely for assumption. Otherwise, how can it be known that such things are possible in existence and have never existed? He said it. The commentator objected that the possibility of what is mentioned is certain, for no impossibility follows from the assumption of its existence, and the non-existence of a thing does not necessitate its impossibility. The objector merely analyzed the compound statement and objected; otherwise the question is about the combination of the two: the possibility of its existence and its being non-existent, for it is possible that it exists and we have not come to know it.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage defines the second division of the universal with respect to the existence of its individuals. The author states that the second division includes universals whose individuals are possible (mumkin) but none are found externally. The example given is the mythical bird 'Anqa, which is conceptually a universal (kulli) with possible individuals, yet none exist. Al-Dusuqi clarifies the wording: the phrase 'it is the second division' is better interpreted as the answer to an implied condition. He also notes that there are stories about the Anqa having existed in the time of Prophet Solomon and being destroyed. Al-'Attar then discusses other examples like a sea of mercury or a mountain of ruby, emphasizing that these are purely hypothetical to illustrate the concept. He raises a potential objection: how can we know such things are possible but non-existent? The response is that possibility is certain because no contradiction arises from assuming their existence; non-existence does not disprove possibility. The discussion is a refinement of the classification, showing that the second division is logically sound.

Key Terms
العنقاء (al-'Anqa)
الإمكان العام (al-imkan al-'amm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key characteristic of the second division of universal according to this passage?
A) Its individuals are all impossible
B) Its individuals are possible but none exist externally
C) It has exactly one individual
D) It is a composite of genus and differentia

Key Takeaway
A universal can be real in the mind and logically sound even if its individuals are nowhere to be found in external existence, as long as they are possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give an example from Islamic fiqh of a universal ruling whose individuals are possible but have never occurred (e.g., a specific type of transaction or crime that could happen but has not yet been recorded). Explain why it fits the second division.
?
Identify whether the universal 'prophet' belongs to the first or second division. Explain your reasoning based on the definition in this lesson.

Possibility vs Existence: Al-'Attar's Clarification
Expanding the discussion on possibility and existence with al-'Attar's objection and resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the non-existence of individuals does not disprove the possibility of a universal, using al-'Attar's reasoning.

Core Idea
Possibility is determined by logical consistency, not by actual occurrence.

After learning the definition of the second division, we encounter an important question: how can we be certain that something is possible if it has never existed? Al-'Attar addresses this by analyzing the compound claim 'possible but non-existent' and showing that possibility is established independently. Let's dissect his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find al-'Attar's question: 'كيفَ يعلمُ أنَّ مثل هذه الأمورِ ممكنه الوجود ولم توجد أبداً؟!' – how can we know such things are possible and have never existed?
The objection targets the combination of two claims: possibility and non-existence.
2
Analyze the response
The commentator responds that possibility (imkan) is certain because assuming their existence leads to no logical contradiction. Non-existence does not negate possibility.
Possibility is a modal property checked by imagining the thing's existence; if no absurdity follows, it is possible regardless of actuality.
3
Understand the fallacy in the objection
Al-'Attar points out that the objection conflates two separate issues: possibility and actual existence. The question 'how can we know both?' falsely assumes that knowledge of possibility depends on knowledge of non-existence.
Possibility can be known a priori through conceptual analysis, while non-existence is an empirical claim.
4
Apply the principle to other examples
Consider a sea of mercury or a mountain of ruby: we can know they are possible because no contradiction arises, even if we have never seen them.
This shows that the second division is logically sound and epistemologically accessible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ) لكن (لَمْ تُوْجَدْ في الخارج، وهو القسمُ الثَّاني، كالعنقاء، فإنَّه كلّي ممكن الأفراد، لكنَّها لم تُوجَد في الخارج. الدسوقي: قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ الْقِسْمُ الثَّانِي الأَوْلَى : فهوَ القسمُ الثَّاني؛ لأنَّ الشَّرِطَ مُقدَّرُ، فيكون هذا جوابَ الشَّرط، وهكذا يُقالُ فيما سيأتي . قَوْلُهُ : كَالْعَنْقَاءِ هي طائرُ له أربعونَ رأساً ، يخطفُ الصغار، وَلَا شَكٍّ أنَّ هذا كُليّ، قِيلَ: إنَّ عدم وجودِهَا إِنَّما هو في آخرِ الزَّمان، وإنَّها كانَتْ موجودةً في زمن سيّدنَا سُليمان، وإنَّها كانَتْ تُكذِّبُ بالقضاء والقدَرِ، فدعا عليها سليمان، فقطعَ الله نسلها. العطار: قَوْلُهُ : كَالْعَنْقَاءِ وبحرٍ مِن زئبق، وجبل من ياقوت، وكانَ التَّمثيل بهذه الأمورِ لمجرَّدِ الفرض، وإلا ؛ فكيفَ يعلمُ أنَّ مثل هذه الأمورِ ممكنه الوجود ولم توجد أبداً؟! قالَهُ المحشّي، وتعقب بأنَّ إمكانَ مَا ذكر قطعي؛ إذ لا يلزم على تقديرِ وجودِهِ مُحال، وليس يلزم من عدم وجودِ الشَّيء عدم إمكانه ا.هـ. ، والمتعقِّبُ نظر لمجرّدِ قولِهِ : ممكنه الوجودِ؛ فحلَّلَ المركَّبَ، واعترض، وإلا ؛ فَالاستفهامُ عَن مجموع الأمرين إمكان وجودِهَا ؛ معَ كونها معدومةٌ، إذ يجوز أن تكون موجودةً ولم نَطَّلع عليها.
(And) but (they are not found in external existence), and it is the second division, like the Anqa, for it is a universal whose individuals are possible, but they are not found in external existence. al-Dusuqi: His saying: 'And it is the second division' - the better is 'so it is the second division' because the condition is implied, so this is the answer to the condition, and likewise it is said in what follows. According to al-'Attar, how can we know that a universal is possible if its individuals have never existed?

Key Takeaway
Possibility is a logical property independent of actual existence; an entity can be possible even if it has never been realized in the external world.

?
In Islamic theology, the concept of 'istihalah (impossibility) is defined by logical contradiction. Apply al-'Attar's reasoning to argue that the resurrection of the dead is possible because no logical contradiction arises, even though it has not yet occurred in our experience.
?
Al-'Attar's objection and response is a classic move in logical debates. Write a brief dialogue (jadal) between two scholars: one argues that the second division cannot be known, the other defends it using al-'Attar's points.

The Third Division: Universal with Only One Actual Individual
Author defines the third division of universal and explains the concept of imkan 'amm used here.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a universal that has only one actual individual and explain why it is still considered a universal.

Core Idea
A universal does not require multiple actual individuals; possibility of others suffices.

In the study of the five universals, we classify universals based on the actual existence of their individuals. The third division covers those universals where only one individual actually exists, but others are possible. This distinction is crucial for understanding concepts like 'the sun'—a universal term that applies to a single existing entity but could hypothetically apply to others. This lesson will walk you through the definition and its logical justification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division's condition
Read the definition: the third division is for universals whose individuals are possible, but only one individual actually exists externally. Note that the possibility of others is essential.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'universal'. The differentia is 'only one actual individual exists, but others are possible'. This distinguishes it from universals with multiple individuals or none.
The differentia includes both the actual singular existence and the possibility of more.
3
Test with a real example
Consider 'the sun'. The concept 'sun' is universal because it can apply to multiple suns. But in external reality, only one sun exists. Other suns are possible but not actual. This fits the third division.
Other examples: 'the moon' if only one moon, but note the earth's moon is unique. In Islamic cosmology, such examples are common.
4
Contrast with other divisions
Compare with the first division (universals with many individuals, e.g., 'human') and second (no actual individuals, e.g., 'phoenix'). The third division is unique for having exactly one actual instance.
