معنى الأخص ، فتفسيرُ الجزئي بِهِ تفسيرُ بِلفظ أشهر، فيكون تعريفاً لفظيا، وليس تعريفاً للشَّيء بِنَفْسِهِ ; لأنَّهُ إِنَّما يقدح في التَّعاريف الحقيقية، ولا تعريفاً بالمجهول، فإِنْ قلْتَ: الَّذي عُلِمَ في بحثِ النِّسبةِ الأخصُّ المختص بالكُلّيّ، والأخص هنا شامل له وللحقيقيّ، فَمَا أريد به هَهُنا ليس عين ما علم به; بل أعم منه، وأيضاً تعريف الجزئي الإضافي بالأخص بعد بيان العموم والخصوص في الكُلِّيَّاتِ ليسَ على ما ينبغي ; لإيهامه كونَ المرادِ هَهُنا ما يخصُّ الكُلَّيَّ، والجواب أنَّ قولَه : (وهو أعم)؛ قرينة واضحة على أن المرادَ بِهِ مَا يشملُ الجزئي الحقيقي; تَأَمَّلْ، ثمَّ إِنَّه اشتهر في موضوع القضيَّةِ الموجبةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ عَدُّ أحد المتساويين جزئيا إضافيًا لِلآخَرِ، فإذا قُلْنَا : كُلَّ إنسان ناطق ; فإنَّ الضاحِكَ والكاتِبَ وكَذَا في هذه القضيَّةِ موضوعاً حقيقيًّا لِلنَّاطِقِ الَّذي هو المحمولُ الكُلّي، فيكونُ كُلّ منها جزئيا مندرجاً تحتَهُ لِمَا تقرَّرَ أنَّ الموضوع والمحكوم عليه في القضيَّة المتعارفةِ ; الأفراد على ما ذهب إليه المتأخّرونَ ، ومِنَ المعلوم أنَّ كُلَّ فردٍ مندرج تحتَ المحمول المساوي لعنوان الموضوع، فالتَّعريفُ المذكور للجزئي الإضافي لا يشملُهُ، فَالأَوْلَى أَنْ يُقال في تفسيره : هو المندرج تحت الموضوعِ الكُلّيّ; ليكونَ شاملاً للجميع، وقد يعتذر عن ذلكَ بِمَا قالَهُ السَّيِّدُ في حاشية المطالع; أنَّ المتبادر مِنْ كَوْنِ الشَّيء مُندرجاً تحتَ آخَرَ ; أنْ يكونَ أخصَّ منه، وقد ظهرَ لَّكَ مِمَّا فَرَّرْنَاهُ أنَّ قولَ المحشّي : والأولى : أو شخصيَّة .ا.هـ. يعني : زيادةً على القضيَّةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ بأنْ يُقال : ما صلح أنْ يكونَ موضوعاً لِلكُلِّي في قضيَّةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ أو شخصيَّةٍ; ليس على ما ينبغي; إذْ موضوعُ الشَّخصيَّةِ لا يندرج فيه شيءٌ لتشخُصِهِ، فَإِنْ أَرادَ أَنَّها تكونُ في حكم الكُلّيَّةِ إذا وقعت كُبرى الشَّكلِ الأَوَّل ; فذاك شيءٌ آخرُ ليسَ مِمَّا نحنُ بِصددِهِ. (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ كَمَا يُقَالُ ... إلخ) الكاف لمجرَّدِ القران بينَ الفَعَلَيْنِ كَمَا سَبقَ ، ثمَّ إِنَّ قضيَّةَ ذكرٍ أنَّ للجزئي معنيين والشكوتُ على الكُلّي ; يدلُّ على أنَّ لِلكُلّى معنى واحداً
And it may be said: the particular; al-Dasuqi: and there is distinctiveness of 'no human in horse' and distinctiveness of 'no horse in human', and in both cases partial contrast is realized. al-Attar: the essence of their two negations in what they are free from, and the separation of the negation of each from the negation of the other; by the separation of each from the other, and every two contrasting terms cannot be free from both; like human and non-rational; so between their negations also there is total contrast; due to the realization of separation without conjunction. He said: and let us conclude the speech by explaining the relationship between the essence of one of the two extremes and the negation of the other, and we say: the relationship between one of the two equivalents and the negation of the other, and between the negation of the more general and the essence of the more specific absolutely, is total contrast; and between the more general and the negation of the more specific absolutely, is generality from one aspect; and one of the two equivalents is more specific than the negation of the other absolutely; and the more general from one aspect is separated from the negation of its counterpart; where it meets it, so either it is absolutely more general, which is when it is impossible to be free from both essences, like animal with the negation of human; or from one aspect, which is when it is possible to be free from both, like animal with the negation of white; all that appears by reflection. End quote. If you say: human contrasts with laughing, even though human and laughing are equivalent; and similarly 'no partner of the evening' for an individual, even though 'partner' and 'individual' are contrasting; the answer to the first is by specifying the claim to when negation does not enter into one of them; and to the second by denying that 'no partner' does not apply to other than the individual; because it applies to individuals of animal, for example; and it is not hidden that specification in such matters does not suit the rules of the art; and as for the second answer, it is clearly false. (His saying: 'and it may be said the particular for the more specific') means: it is said by homonymy for every more specific under the more general, absolutely or from one aspect, according to the statement of the author of al-Kashf; and the author means al-Katibi, like human in relation to animal, and animal in relation to white; and the verifiers hold that the intended meaning is absolute generality and specificity; said by the author in Sharh al-Asl; and al-Jalal indicated this verification by his saying: it is the more specific than the thing; i.e., absolutely. End quote. So what is meant is the absolute more specific; not the absolute more specific that includes it and the more specific from one aspect; otherwise, it would necessitate that each of the more general and the more specific from one aspect is a particular for its counterpart, and it is not so; then it is not hidden that it has been known previously from the explanation of the relationship of absolute generality and specificity in the chapter on the principles of conceptions. i.e., as it is said: the particular for the true particular mentioned, which prevents itself; al-Dasuqi: as for the second form: because contrast is only in some forms; so it is partial; and as for the first: because total contrast entails partial contrast; that is because partial contrast (al-Attar: all individuals of human; occurred) means the more specific; so interpreting the particular by it is an interpretation by a more famous term, so it is a verbal definition, not a definition of the thing itself; because that only detracts from real definitions, nor is it a definition by the unknown. If you say: that which was known in the discussion of relationship is the more specific specific to the universal, but the more specific here includes it and the true particular; so what is meant here is not the same as what was known; rather it is more general; and also defining the relative particular by the more specific after explaining generality and specificity in universals is not appropriate; because it suggests that what is meant here is what is specific to the universal. The answer: his saying 'and it is more general' is a clear indication that what is meant includes the true particular; reflect. Then it is well-known in the subject of the affirmative universal proposition to count one of the two equivalents as a relative particular for the other; so if we say: every human is rational; then the laughing and the writer and so on in this proposition are real subjects for 'rational' which is the universal predicate; so each of them is a particular subsumed under it due to the established fact that the subject and the thing predicated in the conventional proposition are individuals, according to the later scholars; and it is known that every individual is subsumed under the predicate that is equal to the description of the subject; so the aforementioned definition of the relative particular does not include it; so it is more appropriate to say in its interpretation: it is that which is subsumed under the universal subject; to be inclusive of all. And an excuse may be given for that by what al-Sayyid said in Hashiyat al-Mutala': that the immediate understanding from a thing being subsumed under another is that it is more specific. And it has become clear to you from what we have set forth that the statement of the commentator: 'and the better: or individual' i.e., in addition to the universal proposition, by saying: what is suitable to be a subject for the universal in a universal or individual proposition; is not appropriate; because the subject of the individual proposition does not subsume anything due to its particularity; so if he meant that it is in the ruling of the universal if it occurs as the major premise of the first figure, that is something else not relevant to our discussion. (His saying: 'i.e., as it is said... etc.') the kaf is merely for conjunction between the two verbs as previously; then the statement 'that for the particular there are two meanings and the doubt upon the universal' indicates that for the universal there is one meaning.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the definitions of 'particular' (juz'i) and 'contrast' (tabayun) in logic. The author begins by discussing partial contrast (tabayun juz'i), exemplified by the relation between 'human' and 'horse': each is distinct from the other, but they share some common aspect. Then it moves to total contrast (tabayun kulli), where the negations of two terms are also in total contrast because they cannot be free from both, as in 'human' and 'non-rational'. The passage then explains the relationships between terms and their negations, distinguishing between absolute generality and generality from one aspect. The commentator critiques a proposed definition of 'relative particular' (al-juz'i al-idafi) as 'the more specific' (al-akhass), showing that it fails to cover cases where two equivalent terms (like 'human' and 'laughing') are involved. He offers a better definition: the relative particular is 'that which is subsumed under a universal subject' (al-mundarij taht al-mawdu' al-kulli). The passage also rejects a suggestion to extend the definition to individual propositions, arguing that the subject of an individual proposition is not generalizable. Throughout, the commentary uses examples from the Islamic logical tradition, such as 'animal' and 'white', to illustrate the relationships. The discussion is dense and assumes familiarity with logical terminology and prior discussions of universals and propositions.

العموم والخصوص المطلق
المندرج تحت الموضوع الكلي

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the definition of the relative particular as 'the more specific' insufficient?
A) It does not apply to true particulars.
B) It fails to include equivalent terms like 'human' and 'laughing' in 'Every human is rational'.

Key Takeaway
The relative particular is best defined as that which is subsumed under a universal subject, not merely as the more specific.

Real Particular vs. Relative Particular
Defines two types of particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real particular and relative particular using the criteria of essential individuality vs relational specificity.

Core Idea
Particular has two meanings: truly individual (Zayd) or relatively specific (human vs animal).

In logic, the term 'particular' is not always about individuals. This passage introduces two meanings: real particular, which is truly individual and cannot be predicated of many, and relative particular, which is more specific than something else but still a universal in itself. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct definition and argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of 'particular'
In the passage, the author says 'the particular has two meanings; one real, and the other relative.' Locate this statement and note the Arabic terms.
2
Define real particular
Real particular is something like Zayd, which cannot be predicated of many. Its conception prevents participation. Find the description in the passage: 'تصوَّره من وقوع الشركة فيه' meaning its conception prevents the occurrence of participation.
3
Define relative particular
Relative particular is something more specific than something else, like human relative to animal. It is called 'relative' because its particularity is only in relation to what is above it, not in reality. Find where it says 'يُسمَّى جزئيًا إضافيًا'.
4
Apply with examples
Given examples: Zayd is real particular, human is relative particular (compared to animal), animal is relative particular (compared to body). Verify that human is not a real particular because it can be predicated of Zayd, Amr, etc.
5
Test understanding
Check the relationship: every real particular is relative particular (because it is more specific than something), but not vice versa. The passage states this at the end: 'كل جزئي حقيقي أخص من شيء' and 'ولا عكس'.

تصوَّره من وقوع الشركة فيه، كذلك يقال: الجزئي (للأخص) من شيءٍ كـ «الإنسان الأخصّ من الحيوان والحيوان الأخصّ من الجسم» ويُسمَّى جزئيًا إضافيًا؛ لأنَّ جزئيَّته بالإضافة إلى ما فوقه لا بالحقيقة.
الدسوقي: النامي، يرجع لسالبتين جزئيتين، والتباين الكلّي راجع لسالبتين كلّيتين، ولا شك أنَّ السلب الكلّي مستلزم للسلب الجزئي، فقولنا: كل إنسان ليس بجماد؛ مستلزم لبعض الإنسان ليس بجماد، هذا محصِّل كلامه.
وفيه: أنَّ لا وجود ولا عدم ليس بينهما تباين كلّي، بل عموم وخصوص وجهي مثل: لا فرس ولا إنسان، وذلك لصدقهما على أفراد الحيوان؛ لأنها ذات متحققة عند نفي الصفتين، وانفراد لا وجود بصدقه على العدم وانفراد لا عدم بصدقه على الوجود، فكان الأولى التمثيل بلا موجود ولا معدوم، فإنهما متباينان تباينًا كليًا؛ بناءً على التحقيق من نفي الأحوال. وأما على القول بثبوت الأحوال؛ فيتحقق لا موجود ولا معدوم في الحال، فيكون بينهما العموم والخصوص الوجهي.
(قوله: للأخص) كان ذلك الأخص يمنع نفس تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه أو لا (قوله: لا بالحقيقة) أي: لا بالنظر لحقيقته؛ لأنه يكون بالنظر لحقيقته كليًا لا جزئيًا.
العطّار: وهو الكلّي الحقيقي، وللجزئي معنيين؛ أحدهما حقيقي، والآخر إضافي، كما يُستفاد من ظاهر كلام المحقق الرازي في شرح المطالع، وقال السيد في حاشيته عليه: المشهور أنَّ الكلّي له مفهوم واحد يقابل الجزئي الحقيقي تقابل العدم والملكة، ويقابل الجزئي الإضافي تقابل التضايف، واعترضه مير زاهد بأنَّ المفهوم الواحد لا يمكن أن يقابل بمفهوم تقابل العدم والملكة، وهو بعينه مقابل لمفهوم آخر تقابل التضايف؛ ضرورة أنه على الأول: معنى غير إضافي، وعلى الثاني معنى إضافي، فالحق أنَّ للكلّي معنى واحدًا يقابل الجزئي الحقيقي تقابل العدم والملكة، وللجزئي معنيين؛ أحدهما مقابل للأعم من شيء تقابل التضايف، والثاني يقابل الكلّي تقابل العدم والملكة.
فصل: في مبادئ التصورات (وهو) أي: الجزئي بالمعنى الثاني (أعم) من الجزئي بالمعنى الأول مطلقًا؛ لأنَّ كلَّ جزئي حقيقي أخصُّ من شيء، ولا عكس.
(قوله: لأنَّ كلَّ جزئي حقيقي أخص من شيء ألا ترى أن زيدًا وغيره من أفراد الإنسان جزئي حقيقي؛ وهو أخص من الإنسان؟
(قوله: ولا عكس أي: وليس كل أخص من شيء جزئيًا حقيقيًا، ألا ترى أن الإنسان أخص من الحيوان، وليس جزئيًا حقيقيًا؟
His conception from the occurrence of participation in it. Likewise it is said: the particular (is for the more specific) of a thing, like 'human is more specific than animal and animal is more specific than body' and it is called relative particular; because its particularity is relative to what is above it, not in reality. 
Al-Dasūqī: an-Nāmī, it returns to two particular negatives, and universal opposition returns to two universal negatives, and there is no doubt that universal negation entails particular negation, so our statement: every human is not an inanimate object entails some human is not an inanimate object, this is the summary of his words. 
And in it: that 'non-existence' and 'non-nonexistence' are not universally opposed between them, but rather generality and particularity in one respect like: 'not a horse and not a human', and that is because they are true of the individuals of animal; because they are existing essences when the two attributes are negated, and 'non-existence' alone is true of nonexistence and 'non-nonexistence' alone is true of existence, so it was better to exemplify with 'neither existent nor nonexistent', for they are universally opposed; based on verification of the negation of states. But on the assertion of states, 'neither existent nor nonexistent' is realized in the state, so between them there is generality and particularity in one respect. 
(His statement: 'for the more specific') that more specific either prevents the very conception of it from the occurrence of participation in it or not. (His statement: 'not in reality') i.e., not with respect to its reality; because it may be, with respect to its reality, universal not particular. 
Al-'Attār: And it is the real universal, and the particular has two meanings; one real, and the other relative, as is understood from the apparent statement of al-Muḥaqqiq al-Rāzī in Sharḥ al-Maṭāli'. And al-Sayyid said in his gloss on it: the well-known is that the universal has one concept which is opposed to the real particular by the opposition of privation and possession, and is opposed to the relative particular by the opposition of correlation. And Mīr Zāhid objected to him that the one concept cannot be opposed to one concept by the opposition of privation and possession, and the same is opposed to another concept by the opposition of correlation; necessarily because in the first, it is a non-relative meaning, and in the second, a relative meaning. So the truth is that the universal has one concept which is opposed to the real particular by the opposition of privation and possession, and the particular has two meanings; one is opposed to what is more general than a thing by the opposition of correlation, and the second is opposed to the universal by the opposition of privation and possession. 
Chapter: On the foundations of conceptualizations. (And it) i.e., the particular in the second meaning (is more general) than the particular in the first meaning absolutely; because every real particular is more specific than something, and not vice versa. 
(His statement: 'because every real particular is more specific than something, do you not see that Zayd and others from the individuals of human are real particular, and he is more specific than human?') 
(His statement: 'and not vice versa' i.e., and not every more specific than something is a real particular, do you not see that human is more specific than animal, and is not a real particular?)
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of particular (juz'ī): real (ḥaqīqī) and relative (iḍāfī). The author begins by explaining that 'particular' can refer to something more specific than another thing, like 'human' relative to 'animal' or 'animal' relative to 'body'. This is called relative particular because its particularity is only in relation to what is above it, not in essence. The commentary by al-Dasūqī then discusses logical relationships between universal and particular negative propositions, using the example 'every human is not inanimate' to show that universal negation entails particular negation. He then critiques the use of 'non-existence' and 'non-nonexistence' as examples, showing they are not universally opposed but have a face-to-face relationship. Al-'Attār next clarifies that the real universal is opposed to the real particular by privation, while the relative particular is opposed to the universal by correlation. A disagreement between al-Sayyid and Mīr Zāhid is presented: al-Sayyid claims the universal has one concept that relates differently to the two particulars, but Mīr Zāhid objects that one concept cannot have two different oppositions. The resolution is that the universal has one meaning (opposed to real particular), while particular has two meanings (relative and real). The chapter heading 'On the foundations of conceptualizations' introduces a new section where it is stated that the second meaning of particular (relative) is more general than the first (real) because every real particular is more specific than something, but not every more specific thing is a real particular. This is illustrated with Zayd as a real particular (more specific than human) and human as not a real particular (despite being more specific than animal).

جزئي حقيقي
جزئي إضافي
كلّي حقيقي
تقابل العدم والملكة
تقابل التضايف

The term 'particular' has two meanings: real (individual) and relative (more specific), and every real particular is relative but not vice versa.

Opposition and Entailment between Universal and Particular
Discusses logical opposition and entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of opposition between universal and particular (privation and correlation) and apply the rule that universal negation entails particular negation.

Core Idea
Universal and particular relate by two oppositions, and universal negation logically entails particular negation.

This passage discusses how the concepts of universal and particular are opposed in different ways depending on whether we consider real or relative particular. It also establishes an important logical rule: a universal negative statement implies a particular negative. Understanding these relationships is essential for valid reasoning in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of opposition
The passage mentions two types: 'تقابل العدم والملكة' (privative opposition) and 'تقابل التضايف' (correlative opposition). Locate these in the Arabic.
2
See how universal relates to real particular
The universal is opposed to the real particular by privation, just as sight is opposed to blindness. The universal has a positive attribute (predicability) that the real particular lacks.
3
See how universal relates to relative particular
The relative particular is opposed to the universal by correlation, like father and son. The universal and relative particular are relative to each other: human is more specific than animal, animal is more general.
4
Understand Mīr Zāhid's objection
How can the same universal concept have two different oppositions? The truth is that the universal has one meaning (opposed to real particular), while particular has two meanings (real and relative).
5
Apply the entailment rule
The passage states that universal negation entails particular negation. For example, 'Every human is not stone' entails 'Some human is not stone'. Verify this with the text: 'كل إنسان ليس بجماد مستلزم لبعض الإنسان ليس بجماد'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصوَّره من وقوع الشركة فيه، كذلك يقال: الجزئي (للأخص) من شيءٍ كـ «الإنسان الأخصّ من الحيوان والحيوان الأخصّ من الجسم» ويُسمَّى جزئيًا إضافيًا؛ لأنَّ جزئيَّته بالإضافة إلى ما فوقه لا بالحقيقة.
الدسوقي: النامي، يرجع لسالبتين جزئيتين، والتباين الكلّي راجع لسالبتين كلّيتين، ولا شك أنَّ السلب الكلّي مستلزم للسلب الجزئي، فقولنا: كل إنسان ليس بجماد؛ مستلزم لبعض الإنسان ليس بجماد، هذا محصِّل كلامه.
وفيه: أنَّ لا وجود ولا عدم ليس بينهما تباين كلّي، بل عموم وخصوص وجهي مثل: لا فرس ولا إنسان، وذلك لصدقهما على أفراد الحيوان؛ لأنها ذات متحققة عند نفي الصفتين، وانفراد لا وجود بصدقه على العدم وانفراد لا عدم بصدقه على الوجود، فكان الأولى التمثيل بلا موجود ولا معدوم، فإنهما متباينان تباينًا كليًا؛ بناءً على التحقيق من نفي الأحوال. وأما على القول بثبوت الأحوال؛ فيتحقق لا موجود ولا معدوم في الحال، فيكون بينهما العموم والخصوص الوجهي.
(قوله: للأخص) كان ذلك الأخص يمنع نفس تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه أو لا (قوله: لا بالحقيقة) أي: لا بالنظر لحقيقته؛ لأنه يكون بالنظر لحقيقته كليًا لا جزئيًا.
العطّار: وهو الكلّي الحقيقي، وللجزئي معنيين؛ أحدهما حقيقي، والآخر إضافي، كما يُستفاد من ظاهر كلام المحقق الرازي في شرح المطالع، وقال السيد في حاشيته عليه: المشهور أنَّ الكلّي له مفهوم واحد يقابل الجزئي الحقيقي تقابل العدم والملكة، ويقابل الجزئي الإضافي تقابل التضايف، واعترضه مير زاهد بأنَّ المفهوم الواحد لا يمكن أن يقابل بمفهوم تقابل العدم والملكة، وهو بعينه مقابل لمفهوم آخر تقابل التضايف؛ ضرورة أنه على الأول: معنى غير إضافي، وعلى الثاني معنى إضافي، فالحق أنَّ للكلّي معنى واحدًا يقابل الجزئي الحقيقي تقابل العدم والملكة، وللجزئي معنيين؛ أحدهما مقابل للأعم من شيء تقابل التضايف، والثاني يقابل الكلّي تقابل العدم والملكة.
فصل: في مبادئ التصورات (وهو) أي: الجزئي بالمعنى الثاني (أعم) من الجزئي بالمعنى الأول مطلقًا؛ لأنَّ كلَّ جزئي حقيقي أخصُّ من شيء، ولا عكس.
(قوله: لأنَّ كلَّ جزئي حقيقي أخص من شيء ألا ترى أن زيدًا وغيره من أفراد الإنسان جزئي حقيقي؛ وهو أخص من الإنسان؟
(قوله: ولا عكس أي: وليس كل أخص من شيء جزئيًا حقيقيًا، ألا ترى أن الإنسان أخص من الحيوان، وليس جزئيًا حقيقيًا؟
His conception from the occurrence of participation in it. Likewise it is said: the particular (is for the more specific) of a thing, like 'human is more specific than animal and animal is more specific than body' and it is called relative particular; because its particularity is relative to what is above it, not in reality. 
Al-Dasūqī: an-Nāmī, it returns to two particular negatives, and universal opposition returns to two universal negatives, and there is no doubt that universal negation entails particular negation, so our statement: every human is not an inanimate object entails some human is not an inanimate object, this is the summary of his words. 
And in it: that 'non-existence' and 'non-nonexistence' are not universally opposed between them, but rather generality and particularity in one respect like: 'not a horse and not a human', and that is because they are true of the individuals of animal; because they are existing essences when the two attributes are negated, and 'non-existence' alone is true of nonexistence and 'non-nonexistence' alone is true of existence, so it was better to exemplify with 'neither existent nor nonexistent', for they are universally opposed; based on verification of the negation of states. But on the assertion of states, 'neither existent nor nonexistent' is realized in the state, so between them there is generality and particularity in one respect. 
(His statement: 'for the more specific') that more specific either prevents the very conception of it from the occurrence of participation in it or not. (His statement: 'not in reality') i.e., not with respect to its reality; because it may be, with respect to its reality, universal not particular. 
Al-'Attār: And it is the real universal, and the particular has two meanings; one real, and the other relative, as is understood from the apparent statement of al-Muḥaqqiq al-Rāzī in Sharḥ al-Maṭāli'. And al-Sayyid said in his gloss on it: the well-known is that the universal has one concept which is opposed to the real particular by the opposition of privation and possession, and is opposed to the relative particular by the opposition of correlation. And Mīr Zāhid objected to him that the one concept cannot be opposed to one concept by the opposition of privation and possession, and the same is opposed to another concept by the opposition of correlation; necessarily because in the first, it is a non-relative meaning, and in the second, a relative meaning. So the truth is that the universal has one concept which is opposed to the real particular by the opposition of privation and possession, and the particular has two meanings; one is opposed to what is more general than a thing by the opposition of correlation, and the second is opposed to the universal by the opposition of privation and possession. 
Chapter: On the foundations of conceptualizations. (And it) i.e., the particular in the second meaning (is more general) than the particular in the first meaning absolutely; because every real particular is more specific than something, and not vice versa. 
(His statement: 'because every real particular is more specific than something, do you not see that Zayd and others from the individuals of human are real particular, and he is more specific than human?') 
(His statement: 'and not vice versa' i.e., and not every more specific than something is a real particular, do you not see that human is more specific than animal, and is not a real particular?)
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of particular (juz'ī): real (ḥaqīqī) and relative (iḍāfī). The author begins by explaining that 'particular' can refer to something more specific than another thing, like 'human' relative to 'animal' or 'animal' relative to 'body'. This is called relative particular because its particularity is only in relation to what is above it, not in essence. The commentary by al-Dasūqī then discusses logical relationships between universal and particular negative propositions, using the example 'every human is not inanimate' to show that universal negation entails particular negation. He then critiques the use of 'non-existence' and 'non-nonexistence' as examples, showing they are not universally opposed but have a face-to-face relationship. Al-'Attār next clarifies that the real universal is opposed to the real particular by privation, while the relative particular is opposed to the universal by correlation. A disagreement between al-Sayyid and Mīr Zāhid is presented: al-Sayyid claims the universal has one concept that relates differently to the two particulars, but Mīr Zāhid objects that one concept cannot have two different oppositions. The resolution is that the universal has one meaning (opposed to real particular), while particular has two meanings (relative and real). The chapter heading 'On the foundations of conceptualizations' introduces a new section where it is stated that the second meaning of particular (relative) is more general than the first (real) because every real particular is more specific than something, but not every more specific thing is a real particular. This is illustrated with Zayd as a real particular (more specific than human) and human as not a real particular (despite being more specific than animal).

Key Terms
تقابل العدم والملكة
تقابل التضايف
سلب كلي
سلب جزئي
استلزام

Key Takeaway
Universal negation entails particular negation, and the universal is opposed to real particular by privation and to relative particular by correlation.

Distinguishing the Two Meanings of 'Particular'
Author defines the two meanings of 'particular' and shows the first is more general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the real particular and the relative particular and explain why the relative particular is more general.

Core Idea
Real particular: individual; relative particular: specific universal. The relative is more general.

In this passage, the author clarifies two distinct meanings of the term 'particular' (جزئي). One refers to concrete individuals like Zaid that cannot be predicated of many. The other refers to concepts that are more specific than others, even though they are universals themselves. Understanding this distinction is crucial for working with definitions and the five universals in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
The passage defines two senses: the real particular (جزئي حقيقي) and the relative particular (جزئي إضافي). Notice the examples given: Zaid is a real particular; man is a relative particular when compared to animal.
2
Recognize the real particular
A real particular is an individual entity that cannot be predicated of many. For example, Zaid is a real particular because he is a single person, not a concept shared by multiple individuals.
3
Recognize the relative particular
A relative particular is something that is more specific (أخص) than something else, though it may itself be a universal. For instance, man is more specific than animal, but man is a universal (it can be predicated of many individuals).
4
Understand the relationship
Every real particular is more specific than something, so every real particular is also a relative particular. However, not every relative particular is a real particular; e.g., man is a relative particular but not a real particular.
This is summarized by the phrase 'ولا عكس' (not conversely).
5
Apply to theological discussion
The passage notes that Allah's essence is not a real particular because it cannot be mentally presented in a way that admits of universal and particular. This illustrates the limits of the concept.

(قَوْلُهُ : لِأَنَّ كُلَّ جُزْنِيٌّ حَقِيقِيٌّ أَخَصُّ مِنْ شَيْءٍ) أي : مُندرج تحتَ عام، وأقلُهُ الشَّيء والممكن العام ، بل مُندرج تحت مفهوم الجزئي، وقيل: لأنَّ كُلَّ جزئي حقيقي مندرج تحت ما هيَّتِهِ المعرَّاةِ عَنِ النَّشْخُص ، وليس بشيء؛ لانتقاضه بذاتِ الواجب تعالى وتقدَّسَ؛ كذا قيلَ، وفيهِ تَصريح بأنَّ الذَّات المقدَّسَ مِمَّا يوصفُ بالجزئية، وفي حاشية السَّيِّدِ؛ أنَّ مناط الكُلّيَّةِ والجزئية هو الوجود الذهني، وليسَ مِنْ شأن الموجودِ المعيَّن الذي هو واجب الوجودِ لِذاتِهِ أنْ تحصلَ ذاتُهُ في الذهن حتَّى تَتَّصِفَ بالجزئية، بَلْ لا يعقل إلا بوجوهِ كلَّيَّةٍ مُنحصرةٍ في الشَّخْص ا.هـ. ؛ أي: فهوَ واسطةٌ بين الجزئي والكُلِّي كَمَا قالَهُ عبد الحكيم والأدب هو هذا ؛ لا ما رجع إليهِ السَّيِّدُ آخَرَ كَلامِهِ مِن صدقِ الجزئي الحقيقي على الذَّاتِ المقدَّس ؛ بناءً على أَنَّ مَعنى الجزئي هو مَا كَانَ بحيث لو حصل في الذِّهْنِ يمنعُ ، إذ لم يريدُوا بِهِ كونَهُ مَفهوماً بالفعل، وذلك لا يتوقف على الحصولِ بالفعل، وأمَّا جواب المصنفِ في شرح الأصلِ؛ بَأَنَّ تَشخُصَهُ تعالى، غيرُ ذاتِهِ في الخارج، ولا يُنافي ذلك تحليله إلى ماهيَّةٍ وتَشخُص في الذهنِ؛ فيكون داخلاً تحت الماهية المعرَّاةِ، فقد شنّعَ عليه عبد الحكيم قائِلاً : وَلَعَمْرِي إِنَّ هذا مصداقُ مَا قِيلَ: إِنَّ لِكُلِّ عالِم هَفوةٌ ؛ لأنَّه مُصرَّح في الكتب الحكميَّةِ بأنَّ تَسْخُصَهُ عين ذاتِهِ ؛ بحيثُ لا يتصوَّرُ الانفكاكُ ، وهذا غاية مرتبة التَّوحيدِ ا . هـ. لا يُقالُ : سبق مفهوم الواجب مَعدود في تقسيم الكُلّيّ ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : الكلام هنا في خصوص تعالى؛ لا في ذلك المفهوم الصَّادقِ عليها ، فَلَا التباس. (His saying: because every real particular is more specific than something) meaning it falls under a general thing, the least of which is 'thing' and 'possible existing thing', rather it falls under the concept of the particular. And it was said: because every real particular falls under its quiddity stripped of individuation, and it is nothing because it is contradicted by the essence of the Necessary Exalted and Glorified; thus it was said, and in it is an explicit statement that the sacred essence is described as particular. And in the gloss of al-Sayyid: the criterion of universality and particularity is mental existence, and it is not the nature of the specific existent that is necessary of existence by itself that its essence should occur in the mind so as to be described as particularity; rather it is only conceived through universal aspects confined to the person. End quote. That is, it is an intermediary between the particular and the universal as said by Abd al-Hakim, and the proper judgment is this, not what al-Sayyid returned to at the end of his speech concerning the truth of the real particular applying to the sacred essence, based on the meaning of particular being that which if it occurs in the mind prevents [participation?], for they do not intend by it its being actually a concept, and that does not depend on actual occurrence. As for the author's response in the commentary on the original: that His individuation, may He be exalted, is other than His essence externally, and that does not prevent its analysis into quiddity and individuation in the mind, so it falls under the stripped quiddity; Abd al-Hakim strongly criticized him saying: By my life, this is an instance of what was said: every scholar has a lapse, because it is explicitly stated in the philosophical books that His individuation is identical to His essence, such that separation is inconceivable, and this is the ultimate degree of monotheism. End quote. It is not said: the concept of the Necessary has preceded, counted in the division of the universal; because we say: the speech here is about the Exalted Himself, not about that concept that is true of Him, so there is no confusion. (His saying: and not conversely) The author said in the commentary on the original: If one objects that the real particular may not have its relation to what is above it considered, so it would not be a relative particular, then it would be something that its essence...
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage defines two senses of 'particular' (جزئي). The first sense is the real particular (جزئي حقيقي), like Zaid, an individual that cannot be predicated of many. The second sense is the particular relative to something else (جزئي إضافي), like 'man' relative to 'animal'—man is more specific (أخص) than animal but is still a universal in itself. The author states that the particular in the second sense is absolutely more general than the first sense because every real particular is more specific than something (e.g., Zaid is more specific than man), but not every more specific thing is a real particular (e.g., man is more specific than animal but is a universal). The commentary by al-Dusooqi and al-Attar explains this further. Al-Attar also discusses a theological objection: the essence of Allah is not a real particular because it cannot be mentally presented in a way that admits of universal and particular. The discussion involves philosophical and theological nuance, distinguishing between mental existence and external existence. The passage ends with an objection: if a real particular does not consider its relation to what is above it, it would not be a relative particular. The author's response (cut off) probably asserts that the relation is inherent (bi al-quwwa).

Key Terms
جزئي حقيقي (juz'i haqiqi): A real particular: an individual that cannot be predicated of many, like Zaid.
جزئي إضافي (juz'i idafi): A relative particular: something that is more specific than another thing, though it may itself be a universal (e.g., man is more specific than animal).
أعم (a'am): More general: used here to indicate the broader applicability of the relative particular compared to the real particular.
أخص (akhass): More specific: the relationship of a lesser universal to a greater one, or of an individual to a universal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is always more general than something else.
B) It is more specific than something else, and the converse holds for every specific thing.
C) It is more specific than something else, but not every specific thing is a real particular.
D) It is neither more specific nor more general.

Key Takeaway
The relative particular is more general than the real particular because every real particular is more specific than something, but not every more specific thing is a real particular.

The Five Universals

The Five Universals: Their Nature and Function
Author states the universals are five by induction, with commentary on their function.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five universals and explain their role in constructing definitions and descriptions.

Core Idea
The five universals are the essential building blocks of every definition.

This lesson introduces the five universals – genus, species, differentia, property, and accident general – and explains how they function as the principles of concepts and the components of definitions and descriptions. The passage presents them as enumerated by induction, but the commentary reveals a deeper rational basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author states that the universals are five according to induction. Notice that he does not give a rational proof here, only an inductive enumeration.
2
Understand their role
The commentary explains that the universals are the 'principles of concepts' – the fundamental building blocks that lead to concepts. Definitions and descriptions are composites of these universals.
3
Distinguish the five
The five are: genus (jins), species (naw'), differentia (fasl), property (khassa), and accident general (arad 'aam). Genus and differentia are parts of the quiddity; species is the whole quiddity; property and accident are external to the quiddity.
Accident general and property are not used in composite definitions but are still counted among the five.
4
Connect to Islamic sciences
In usul al-fiqh, defining a legal concept like 'riba' requires identifying its genus and differentia. For example, riba is an increase (genus) in a specific exchange (differentia).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْكُلّيَّاتُ) بحسب الاستقراء (خَمْسٌ)؛ (قَوْلُهُ : وَالْكُلِّيَّاتُ ... إلخ) اعلَمُ أنَّ الكُلِّيَّاتِ مبادنُ التَصوراتِ ؛ أي: مبادئ الموصل لِلتَّصوراتِ ؛ أي : وسائل الموصل للتصورات ، والمراد بكونها وسائل له : أنَّهُ يتركَبُ منها ؛ أي: من مجموعِهَا ؛ إذ العرضُ العام والخاصة لا يتركَّب منها ، وقد يُقَالُ : إِنَّ الموصل قد يكونُ مُفرداً كَالخاصَّة فقط، إلا أنْ يُقَالَ : الكلام في الغالبِ، وهذه الرُّسومُ النَّاقصةُ خلافُ الغالب، وأمَّا مَقاصد النَّصوراتِ ؛ أي: المقاصد لِلتَّصورات ; فهو المركَّبُ مِن مجموع هذهِ الكُلِّيَّات، وهو المعرفُ والرَّسم . (قَوْلُهُ: بِحَسَبِ الِاسْتِقْرَاء) أي : التَّبع ، فليس حصرها في الخمس عقليًا، إلا أنَّ قولَه : لأنَّ الكُلِّيَّ ... إلخ) يقتضي أنَّ حصرها في الخمس عقلي، ففيه تنافٍ، وَيُجابُ : بأنَّ المراد الاستقراء المقوّى بالدليل، فتأمل(۱) ا.هـ. والجوابُ أنَّ الإضافة إلى ما فوقَهُ مُتحقِّقَةٌ في نَفْسِ الأمر، واعتبارُ الإضافة بالفعل غير معتبر ; بل المدار على الصلوحية، وهي لازمةٌ له غيرُ مُنفَكَةٍ عنهُ؛ تَأَمَّلْ. (قَوْلُهُ: بِحَسبِ الاِسْتِقْراء) أي : استقراء العقل؛ فالحصر عقلي، ولا يرد الصنف كالرامي مثلاً ، فإنَّهُ بالنسبةِ لجميع الأفرادِ عَرَضُ عام، ولِمَجموعِهَا خَاصَّةٌ. (قَوْلُهُ : خَمْسٌ) أي : خمسة أنواع كما في الجلال، قالَ الزَّاهديُّ : وفيه إيراد، وهو أنَّ كَوْنَ الجنسِ نَوعاً مِنَ الكُلّيّ ; يقتضي أن يكون أخصَّ منهُ مُطلقاً ، وَكَوْنَ الكُلّى جنساً له خاصا يقتضي أن يكون أعم منهُ مُطلقاً ، والجوابُ أنَّ العُموم والخصوص هَهُنا (ثم footnote) (۱) (قَوْلُهُ : فتأمل) تأملناه فوجدناه خلاف ما في الحواشي من أنَّ الحصر عقلي، وقد أجابوا عن الصنف برجوعه إلى الخاصة أو العرض العام فلا نقض وأيضاً ضابطه وهو التردد بين النفي والإثبات متحقق هنا كما بيَّنه الشارح بالقوة، وحاصله أن تقول: الكلي بالنسبة إلى أفراده المندرجة تحته إمَّا جزء من ماهيتها أو لا ؟ الأوَّل : إما جنس إن كانت أفراده حقائق متباينة كحيوان، وإما فصل إن كانت أفراداً لحقيقة واحدة كناطق، والثاني : إمام تمام ماهية أفراده أو لا ; الأوَّل النوع ، والثَّاني وهو الخارج عن ماهية أفراده إما مقول على ما تحت حقيقة واحدة أو لا، الأوّل الخاصة والثاني العرض العام. ا.هـ.
And the universals, according to induction, are five. (His saying: 'And the universals... etc.') Know that the universals are the principles of concepts; i.e., principles that lead to concepts; i.e., means that lead to concepts. The intent in their being means for it is that a composite is made from them; i.e., from their totality; since the accident general and the property are not composed from them. It may be said that the leader may be singular, like the property alone, except that it is said: the speech is about the majority, and these incomplete definitions are contrary to the majority. As for the ends of concepts; i.e., the purposes for concepts, it is the composite from the totality of these universals, and it is the definition and the description. (His saying: 'according to induction') i.e., following, so its limitation to five is not rational, except that his saying: 'because the universal... etc.' implies that its limitation to five is rational, so there is a contradiction, and it is answered: that what is meant is induction strengthened by evidence, so reflect. End quote. And the answer is that the relation to what is above it is realized in itself, and considering the relation actually is not considered; rather the criterion is potentiality, and it is necessary for it, not separate from it; reflect. (His saying: 'according to induction') i.e., induction of the mind; so the limitation is rational, and the category like 'the shooter' for example is not an objection, because it is relative to all individuals an accident general, and relative to their totality a property. (His saying: 'five') i.e., five types, as in al-Jalal. al-Zahidi said: There is an objection in it, which is that the genus being a type of the universal requires that it be absolutely more specific than it, and the universal being a genus for it specifically requires that it be absolutely more general than it, and the answer is that generality and specificity here (then footnote) (1) (His saying: 'reflect') We reflected on it and found it contrary to what is in the glosses: that the limitation is rational, and they answered concerning the category by returning it to property or accident general, so there is no objection. Also, its criterion, which is oscillation between negation and affirmation, is realized here as the commentator explained in potentiality. Its summary is: the universal with respect to its individuals falling under it is either a part of their quiddity or not. The first: either a genus if its individuals are distinct realities like animal, or a differentia if its individuals are of one reality like rational. The second: either the entirety of the quiddity of its individuals or not. The first is species. The second, which is external to the quiddity of its individuals, is either predicated of what is under one reality or not: the first is property, the second is accident general. End quote.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage states that the universals (الكليات) are five, based on induction (الاستقراء). The commentator explains that the universals are the means that lead to concepts, and from them definitions and descriptions are composed, though some universals (accident general and property) are not used in compositions. There is a discussion about whether the division into five is purely empirical (by induction) or rational. The matn says 'by induction', but the commentator notes that the subsequent reasoning implies it is rational. The resolution is that 'induction' here means induction strengthened by rational proof, so it is not a purely empirical count. Further, an objection about the category (الصنف) is addressed: it reduces to either property or accident general. The footnote provides a rational criterion for the division: considering whether the universal is a part of the quiddity of its individuals or not, and whether it completely or partially describes them. This yields the five: genus, species, differentia, property, accident general. The classification is thus ultimately rational, even if initially presented as inductive.

Key Terms
الاستقراء
الكليات الخمس
المعرف والرسم
الصنف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the role of the five universals?
A) They are the only objects of knowledge.
B) They are the principles that lead to concepts, and from them definitions and descriptions are composed.
C) They are the names of the categories in Aristotle's logic.
D) They are the five senses of induction.

Key Takeaway
The five universals are the foundational components of all definitions and descriptions, and their enumeration, though presented by induction, is ultimately rational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a definition of 'wine' (khamr) using the proper genus and differentia. What genus does it belong to? What distinguishes it from other intoxicants?
?
Identify which of the five universals is the term 'mujtahid' (one who performs ijtihad) in the context of usul al-fiqh. Is it a genus, species, differentia, property, or accident general? Justify your answer.

The Five Universals
intermediate
5 min
Induction or Reason: The Basis for the Five Universals
Author states enumeration by induction, commentary reconciles with rational basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the division of universals into five is both inductive and rational, and how the apparent contradiction is resolved.

Core Idea
The division of universals is rational, even though it is presented as inductive.

The matn states that the universals are five 'according to induction', but the subsequent reasoning implies a rational basis. This lesson examines the apparent contradiction and its resolution, showing that 'induction' here is strengthened by rational evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradiction
The matn says 'according to induction', which suggests empirical enumeration. But the author's own words (as noted in the commentary) imply that the limitation is rational. Spot this tension.
2
Find the resolution in the commentary
The commentator says: 'what is meant is induction strengthened by evidence'. So the enumeration is not purely empirical; it is guided by reason.
3
Examine the rational criterion in the footnote
The footnote provides a complete rational derivation: a universal is either a part of the quiddity or not, and then subdivides. This shows that the five emerge from logical analysis, not mere observation.
4
Apply to an Islamic concept
Consider the term 'mutakallim' (theologian). Using the rational criterion, determine which of the five universals it is. Is it a part of quiddity? Is it a species?

The Objection of 'الصنف' (Category) and Its Resolution
Author addresses the objection of the category, showing it reduces to property or accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the category (الصنف) is not a sixth universal and how it reduces to property or accident general.

Core Idea
The category is not a separate universal; it is always reducible to property or accident general.

An objection arises: what about terms like 'shooter' (رامي) that seem to be a universal but do not fit neatly into the five? The passage addresses this objection and shows that such categories are actually either property or accident general, depending on the perspective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection states that the category (الصنف) like 'shooter' appears to be a universal that is not one of the five. Recognize this as a potential counterexample.
2
Understand the response
The commentary explains that 'shooter' relative to all individuals (shooters) is an accident general, but relative to the totality of shooters as a group it is a property. So it is not a new universal.
The same term can be accident or property depending on the perspective.
3
Grasp the criterion
The footnote mentions the 'criterion of oscillation between negation and affirmation'. This criterion helps in determining whether a universal is a property or accident. Apply it to other examples.
4
Practice with an Islamic example
Consider the term 'mufassir' (exegete). Is it a sixth universal? Determine if it can be reduced to property or accident general. Use the criterion of oscillation: can it be affirmed and negated of the same subject?

(قَوْلُهُ: بِحَسَبِ الِاسْتِقْرَاء) أي : التَّبع ، فليس حصرها في الخمس عقليًا، إلا أنَّ قولَه : لأنَّ الكُلِّيَّ ... إلخ) يقتضي أنَّ حصرها في الخمس عقلي، ففيه تنافٍ، وَيُجابُ : بأنَّ المراد الاستقراء المقوّى بالدليل، فتأمل(۱) ا.هـ. والجوابُ أنَّ الإضافة إلى ما فوقَهُ مُتحقِّقَةٌ في نَفْسِ الأمر، واعتبارُ الإضافة بالفعل غير معتبر ; بل المدار على الصلوحية، وهي لازمةٌ له غيرُ مُنفَكَةٍ عنهُ؛ تَأَمَّلْ. (قَوْلُهُ: بِحَسبِ الاِسْتِقْراء) أي : استقراء العقل؛ فالحصر عقلي، ولا يرد الصنف كالرامي مثلاً ، فإنَّهُ بالنسبةِ لجميع الأفرادِ عَرَضُ عام، ولِمَجموعِهَا خَاصَّةٌ. (قَوْلُهُ : خَمْسٌ) أي : خمسة أنواع كما في الجلال، قالَ الزَّاهديُّ : وفيه إيراد، وهو أنَّ كَوْنَ الجنسِ نَوعاً مِنَ الكُلّيّ ; يقتضي أن يكون أخصَّ منهُ مُطلقاً ، وَكَوْنَ الكُلّى جنساً له خاصا يقتضي أن يكون أعم منهُ مُطلقاً ، والجوابُ أنَّ العُموم والخصوص هَهُنا (ثم footnote) (۱) (قَوْلُهُ : فتأمل) تأملناه فوجدناه خلاف ما في الحواشي من أنَّ الحصر عقلي، وقد أجابوا عن الصنف برجوعه إلى الخاصة أو العرض العام فلا نقض وأيضاً ضابطه وهو التردد بين النفي والإثبات متحقق هنا كما بيَّنه الشارح بالقوة، وحاصله أن تقول: الكلي بالنسبة إلى أفراده المندرجة تحته إمَّا جزء من ماهيتها أو لا ؟ الأوَّل : إما جنس إن كانت أفراده حقائق متباينة كحيوان، وإما فصل إن كانت أفراداً لحقيقة واحدة كناطق، والثاني : إمام تمام ماهية أفراده أو لا ; الأوَّل النوع ، والثَّاني وهو الخارج عن ماهية أفراده إما مقول على ما تحت حقيقة واحدة أو لا، الأوّل الخاصة والثاني العرض العام. ا.هـ.
And the universals, according to induction, are five. (His saying: 'And the universals... etc.') Know that the universals are the principles of concepts; i.e., principles that lead to concepts; i.e., means that lead to concepts. The intent in their being means for it is that a composite is made from them; i.e., from their totality; since the accident general and the property are not composed from them. It may be said that the leader may be singular, like the property alone, except that it is said: the speech is about the majority, and these incomplete definitions are contrary to the majority. As for the ends of concepts; i.e., the purposes for concepts, it is the composite from the totality of these universals, and it is the definition and the description. (His saying: 'according to induction') i.e., following, so its limitation to five is not rational, except that his saying: 'because the universal... etc.' implies that its limitation to five is rational, so there is a contradiction, and it is answered: that what is meant is induction strengthened by evidence, so reflect. End quote. And the answer is that the relation to what is above it is realized in itself, and considering the relation actually is not considered; rather the criterion is potentiality, and it is necessary for it, not separate from it; reflect. (His saying: 'according to induction') i.e., induction of the mind; so the limitation is rational, and the category like 'the shooter' for example is not an objection, because it is relative to all individuals an accident general, and relative to their totality a property. (His saying: 'five') i.e., five types, as in al-Jalal. al-Zahidi said: There is an objection in it, which is that the genus being a type of the universal requires that it be absolutely more specific than it, and the universal being a genus for it specifically requires that it be absolutely more general than it, and the answer is that generality and specificity here (then footnote) (1) (His saying: 'reflect') We reflected on it and found it contrary to what is in the glosses: that the limitation is rational, and they answered concerning the category by returning it to property or accident general, so there is no objection. Also, its criterion, which is oscillation between negation and affirmation, is realized here as the commentator explained in potentiality. Its summary is: the universal with respect to its individuals falling under it is either a part of their quiddity or not. The first: either a genus if its individuals are distinct realities like animal, or a differentia if its individuals are of one reality like rational. The second: either the entirety of the quiddity of its individuals or not. The first is species. The second, which is external to the quiddity of its individuals, is either predicated of what is under one reality or not: the first is property, the second is accident general. End quote.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage states that the universals (الكليات) are five, based on induction (الاستقراء). The commentator explains that the universals are the means that lead to concepts, and from them definitions and descriptions are composed, though some universals (accident general and property) are not used in compositions. There is a discussion about whether the division into five is purely empirical (by induction) or rational. The matn says 'by induction', but the commentator notes that the subsequent reasoning implies it is rational. The resolution is that 'induction' here means induction strengthened by rational proof, so it is not a purely empirical count. Further, an objection about the category (الصنف) is addressed: it reduces to either property or accident general. The footnote provides a rational criterion for the division: considering whether the universal is a part of the quiddity of its individuals or not, and whether it completely or partially describes them. This yields the five: genus, species, differentia, property, accident general. The classification is thus ultimately rational, even if initially presented as inductive.

الصنف
العرض العام
الخاصة

D) It is a differentia.

Key Takeaway
The category is not a sixth universal; it is a relational term that can be analyzed as property or accident general.

The Five Universals
beginner
5 min
Classifying the Five Universals by Essence Relationship
Author classifies the five universals based on their relation to the essence of individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize any simple universal into one of the three essence relationships: part (genus/differentia), whole (species), or external (property/accident).

Core Idea
The five universals are defined by how they relate to the essence: part, whole, or external.

In classical Islamic logic, every universal (kullī) that applies to individuals has a specific relation to the essence (māhiyya) of those individuals. The author of this passage gives a clear tripartite classification: a universal can be a part of the essence, the whole essence, or external to it. This classification yields the five universals: genus, differentia, species, property, and general accident. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for constructing correct definitions (ḥadd) and descriptions (rasm) in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The passage states: 'the universal... is either a part of the essence, or the whole of it, or external to it.' Note these three categories.
2
Link each category to the five universals
Part yields genus and differentia; whole yields species; external yields property and general accident. Map each universal to its category.
Genus and differentia are both parts but differ in the nature of the individuals under them.
3
Distinguish genus from differentia within the part category
Both are parts of the essence, but genus applies when the individuals are diverse realities (different species), e.g., 'animal' for humans and horses. Differentia applies when the individuals are of a single reality (same species), e.g., 'rational' for humans.
The commentary addresses a potential objection about compound terms like 'rational animal'.
4
Distinguish property from general accident within the external category
Property (khāṣṣa) is exclusive to one species, e.g., 'the one who laughs' for humans. General accident (‘araḍ ‘āmm) applies to multiple species, e.g., 'the one who walks' for humans and animals.
5
Apply the classification to a new example
Consider the universal 'living' (ḥayy). Determine which category it falls into: is it a part, whole, or external? (Answer: It is a genus because it is a part of the essence of many species like animals and plants.)
Use the criteria from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات
لأنَّ الكلّيَّ بالنّسبة إلى ما تحته من الأفرادِ إمَّا جزء من ماهية الأفرادِ وهو الجنسُ والفضلُ، أو تمامها وهو النَّوعُ، أو خارج عنها وهو الخاصة والعَرَضُ العام، فالكليات خمس:
الديوتي
(قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ الْجِنْسُ) إِنْ قُلْتَ: يُرادُ الجوهرُ النَّاطِقُ أو الحسّاس؛ فإِنَّهُ جزءٌ وليس جنساً ولا فصلاً، وأجيب بأنَّ كلامنا في المفردِ لا المركَّب .
وقوله : (وهو الجنس)؛ أي: إنْ كانَتِ الأفرادُ الَّتي تحته ـ وهو جزءٌ منها - حقائق كالحيوان.
وقولُهُ: (والفصل)؛ أي: إنْ كانَتِ الأفرادُ الَّتى تحته ـ وهو جزء منها ـ أفراداً حقيقية كالنَّاطِقِ، وكلٌّ مِن هَذَيْنِ الكُلِّيَّيْن يُقال له: كُلّيّ ذاتي؛ لدخولِهِ في ماهية ما تحتَهُ مِنَ الذَّاتِ، ووقوعه جزءاً منها .
(قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ تَمَامِهَا) عطف على قوله : ((جزء)؛ أي: أو تمام ماهية ما تحتَهُ مِنَ الأفرادِ كَالإنسان. (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ خَارِجٌ عَنْهَا أي: عن ماهية ما تحتَهُ مِنَ الأفراد، وقوله: (وهو الخاصَّة؛ أي : كَالصَّاحِكِ، والعَرَضُ العام : كَالماشي، ويقال لهما : كُلِّيَّانِ عَرَضيَّان؛ لعروضِهِمَا لِمَاهِيَّةِ ما تحتَهما وعدم دخولهما فيها، وعلى هذا فالنَّوع ليس ذاتيا ولا عَرَضيَّا؛ لأنَّهُ تمام الماهيَّة، وتمامُ الشَّيء ليس داخلاً فيهِ وَلَا خارجاً عنه
Chapter: On the Principles of Concepts. Because the universal, in relation to the individuals under it, is either a part of the essence of the individuals—and that is genus and differentia; or the whole of it—and that is species; or external to it—and that is property and general accident. Thus the universals are five. Dyuti said: His statement: 'and that is genus'—if you say: the term 'rational animal' or 'sentient animal' is a part and is not genus or differentia, it is answered that our discussion is about the simple term, not the composite. His statement: 'and that is genus' means if the individuals under it—while it is a part of them—are diverse realities, like 'animal'. His statement: 'and differentia' means if the individuals under it—while it is a part of them—are individuals of a single reality, like 'rational'. Both of these universals are called essential universals because they enter into the essence of what is under them from the substance and occur as a part of it. His statement: 'or the whole of it' is coordinated with his statement 'a part'; that is, or the whole of the essence of what is under it from the individuals, like 'human'. His statement: 'or external to it' means external to the essence of what is under it from the individuals, and his statement: 'and that is property' like 'the one who laughs', and 'general accident' like 'the one who walks'. Both of these are called accidental universals because they are accidental to the essence of what is under them and do not enter into it. On this basis, species is neither essential nor accidental because it is the whole essence, and the whole of a thing is neither inside it nor outside it.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of the five universals (al-kulliyāt al-khams) in logic. It begins by establishing a principle: a universal, when considered in relation to the individuals that fall under it, can have one of three relationships to the essence (māhiyya) of those individuals. First, it may be a part of the essence—in which case, if the individuals are diverse realities (different species), the universal is genus (jins), and if the individuals are instances of a single reality, the universal is differentia (fasl). For example, 'animal' is a genus for humans, horses, etc.; 'rational' is a differentia for human individuals. Second, the universal may be the whole essence—this is species (naw‘), as 'human' is the entire essence of each human individual. Third, the universal may be external to the essence—this yields either property (khāṣṣa), like 'the one who laughs' for humans, or general accident (‘araḍ ‘āmm), like 'the one who walks'. The commentary by Dyuti elaborates on each type. It addresses a potential objection: a compound term like 'rational animal' is partly a part, but it is not a simple universal; the discussion is restricted to simple universals. The commentary further explains that genus and differentia are called essential (dhātī) universals because they enter into the essence of their individuals as parts. Property and general accident are called accidental (‘araḍī) universals because they are external to the essence. Species is neither essential nor accidental because it is the whole essence—a whole is neither inside nor outside itself. This classification is foundational for understanding definition (ḥadd) and description (rasm) in logic.

جنس
فصل
نوع
خاصة
عرض عام
ذاتي
عرضي

A) Genus and differentia
B) Species and property
C) Property and general accident
D) Genus and species

Key Takeaway
The five universals are classified according to whether they are part of, the whole of, or external to the essence of their individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which category the universal 'alim (knowing) falls into when applied to humans: is it genus, differentia, species, property, or general accident? Explain using the passage's criteria.
?

The Five Universals

Essential vs Accidental Universals and the Status of Species
Author classifies the five universals based on their relation to the essence of individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a universal is essential (dhātī), accidental (‘araḍī), or neither, based on its relationship to the essence.

Core Idea
Species is the whole essence; it is neither essential nor accidental in the strict sense.

The passage not only classifies the five universals by their relation to essence, but also labels some as essential (dhātī) and others as accidental (‘araḍī). Genus and differentia are essential because they are parts of the essence. Property and general accident are accidental because they are external. However, species stands in a unique position: it is the entire essence, and so it is neither inside nor outside. Understanding this nuance prevents confusion when constructing definitions and descriptions in logic.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three categories
From the previous lesson, recall the three categories: part (genus/differentia), whole (species), external (property/accident). List them.
2
Identify essential universals
The passage says genus and differentia are essential because they enter into the essence as parts. Note: they are inside the essence.
Essential universals are also called dhātī.
3
Identify accidental universals
Property and general accident are accidental because they are external to the essence. They do not enter.
Accidental universals are also called ‘araḍī.
4
Analyze the unique case of species
Species is the whole essence. The passage states: 'the whole of a thing is neither inside it nor outside it.' Therefore, species is neither essential nor accidental. This is important because species cannot serve as a part in a definition; it is the definiendum itself.
The commentary by Dyuti reinforces this point.

The Five Universals
intermediate
7 min
The Five Universals: Part, Whole, or External to Essence
Author divides universals by their relation to particulars' essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify each of the five universals (genus, differentia, species, property, common accident) according to whether it is a part of the essence, the whole essence, or external to the essence.

Core Idea
The five universals are divided by their relation to essence: part, whole, or external.

In this passage, the author establishes the foundational classification of the five universals. Understanding which universals enter the essence and which are accidental is essential for constructing precise definitions in Islamic logic and for applying them in fields like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three relational categories
Notice that the universal in relation to its particulars can be either a part of the essence, the whole essence, or external to the essence.
2
Match each category to the universals
Part of the essence includes genus and differentia; whole essence is species; external includes property and common accident.
3
Understand the essential vs. accidental distinction
Universals that are part of the essence are called essential (dhati); those that are external are called accidental ('aradi).
4
Recognize the unique status of species
Species is the whole essence, so it is neither inside nor outside, and thus neither essential nor accidental.

فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات. لأنَّ الكلّيَّ بالنّسبة إلى ما تحته من الأفرادِ إمَّا جزء من ماهية الأفرادِ وهو الجنس والفضلُ، أو تمامها وهو النَّوعُ ، أو خارج عنها وهو الخاصة والعَرَضُ العام، فالكليات خمس : (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ الْجِنْسُ) إِنْ قُلْتَ: يُرادُ الجوهرُ النَّاطِقُ أو الحسّاس؛ فإِنَّهُ جزءٌ وليس جنساً ولا فصلاً، وأجيب بأنَّ كلامنا في المفردِ لا المركَّب . وقوله : (وهو الجنس)؛ أي: إنْ كانَتِ الأفرادُ الَّتي تحته ـ وهو جزءٌ منها - حقائق كالحيوان. وقولُهُ: (والفصل)؛ أي: إنْ كانَتِ الأفرادُ الَّتى تحته ـ وهو جزء منها ـ أفراداً حقيقية كالنَّاطِقِ، وكلٌّ مِن هَذَيْنِ الكُلِّيَّيْن يُقال له: كُلّيّ ذاتي؛ لدخولِهِ في ماهية ما تحتَهُ مِنَ الذَّاتِ، ووقوعه جزءاً منها . (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ تَمَامِهَا) عطف على قوله : ((جزء) ؛ أي: أو تمام ماهية ما تحتَهُ مِنَ الأفرادِ كَالإنسان. (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ خَارِجٌ عَنْهَا أي: عن ماهية ما تحتَهُ مِنَ الأفراد، وقوله: (وهو الخاصَّة ؛ أي : كَالصَّاحِكِ ، والعَرَضُ العام : كَالماشي ، ويقال لهما : كُلِّيَّانِ عَرَضيَّان؛ لعروضِهِمَا لِمَاهِيَّةِ ما تحتَهما وعدم دخولهما فيها، وعلى هذا؛ فالنَّوع ليس ذاتيا ولا عَرَضيَّا ؛ لأنَّهُ تمام الماهيَّة، وتمامُ الشَّيء ليس داخلاً فيهِ وَلَا خارجاً عنه. (قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّ الكُلّيّ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِمَا تَحْتَهُ ... إِلَخ) بيان لوجهِ الحصر؛ أي: إلى ما يحملُ هو عليه؛ لأنَّ نِسبتَهُ إلى المباين غيرُ مُعتبرةٍ ، فإِنَّهُ بالنِّسبة إليهِ ليس شيئاً مِنَ الأقسامِ الثَّلاثةِ، ثمَّ قيّد بكونِهِ مِنَ الجزئياتِ ؛ سواء كانت مِنْ تبعيضيَّةً أو ابتدائية؛ أي : حال كونِهِ بَعضاً منها أو ناشئاً منها ؛ للإشارة إلى أنَّ المعتبر النسبة إلى جزئي واحدٍ ؛ أي جُزئي كانَ؛ لَا إلى مجموع الجزئيَّاتِ ؛ لأنَّه يبطل الحصرُ، إِذْ هنا أقسام أربعة أُخرَى؛ هِيَ أن تجتمع في الكُلّي تلك الأقسامُ الثَّلاثة ثناء أو ثلاث، ولا إلى جزئي واحدٍ مُعيَّن ؛ لأنَّهُ حينئذٍ تصيرُ الأقسام مُتباينة، وقد اعتبر تصادقها، حيث ذكر الجنس في تمام الماهية وجزئيتها، بل هو مُعتبر على إطلاقه، فتكونُ الأقسام متخالفة بالاعتبارِ على مَا صرَّحُوا بِهِ مِن جواز اجتماع الخمسة في كُلّيّ واحد، ثمَّ الجزئي الواحد لا جوز أن يُراد به الحقيقي، وإلا ، أَخَرَجَ الأجناس والفصول العالية والمتوسطة وخواصها وأعراضُها مقيسة إلى الماهِيَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ أَجناس متوسطة أو سافلةٌ، الإضافي، وللإشارة إلى ذلكَ ؛ عبّر عنه :بقولِهِ : ما تَحتَهُ هذا ؛ لكن يردُ النَّاطِقُ مَقيساً إلى الحيوان؛ فإنَّهُ خاصَّةٌ لهُ معَ عدم دخولِهِ في الكُلّيّ المنسوب إلى ما تحتَهُ مِن جزئيّاتِهِ، إِلَّا أَنْ يُقال : ما يحملُ عليهِ شيءٌ فهو جزئي إضافي له، ثمَّ الظَّاهِرُ أَنَّ الكُلِّيَّاتِ الفرضيَّةَ داخلة في هذه الأقسام الثَّلاثةِ، وذلك لأنَّ إمكانَ فرضِ صدقها على كثيرين ؛ نظراً إلى مجرَّدِ مفهوم يستدعي إمكانَ فرضِ الأقسام الثَّلاثةِ فيها، وإنْ لم يكن شيءٌ منها في نَفْسِ الأمرِ ، فاندفع ما قيلَ : إِنَّ فرضَ صدقها في نفس الأمر محال، فيجوز أن يستلزم المحالُ بأن لا يكون شيئاً مِنَ الأقسامِ الثَّلاثةِ، وأَنَّهُ يَجوزُ فرضُ صدقِهَا نَفْساً وجزءاً وخارجاً بالنسبة إلى أمرٍ واحدٍ فيلزم صدقُ الكُلِّيَّاتِ الخمسِ عليها بالنّسبة إلى ذلكَ الأمرِ ؛ لأنَّ الفرضَ والمفروض كلاهُمَا مُمْتَنِعَانِ، إِذْ لا يمكنُ للعقل تجويز كونها نَفْساً وجزءاً وخارجاً بالنسبة إلى أمرٍ واحد، ويجوز أن تخرج الكُلِّيَّاتُ الفرضيَّةُ، وتعتبرُ النِّسبةُ إلى ما يُحمَلُ عليهِ في نفس الأمرِ؛ بناءً على عدمِ تعلقِ الفرض الحكمي بأحوالِ الكُلِّيَّاتِ الفرضيَّةِ، ويكونُ إدخالها في التَّعريف تبعَ إدخال مفهوم الواجب فيه، وهذا على طبق ما قالُوا في النّسب بينَ الكُلِّيَّاتِ.
Section: On the foundations of concepts. Because the universal, in relation to its particulars, is either a part of the essence of the particulars — and that is genus and differentia, or the whole of it — and that is species, or external to it — and that is property and common accident. So the universals are five: (His saying: 'and it is the genus') If you say: What is meant is the rational or sensitive substance; for it is a part and is not genus or differentia, and it is answered that our discourse is about the simple, not the compound. And his saying: 'and it is the genus'; meaning: if the particulars beneath it — while it is a part of them — are real essences like animal. And his saying: 'and the differentia'; meaning: if the particulars beneath it — while it is a part of them — are real individuals like rational. And each of these two universals is called essential universal, because it enters into the essence of what is beneath it from the essence, and it occurs as a part of it. (His saying: 'or the whole of it') is conjoined to his saying 'part'; meaning: or the whole of the essence of what is beneath it from the particulars, like human. (His saying: 'or external to it') meaning: external to the essence of what is beneath it from the particulars, and his saying: 'and it is the property'; meaning: like laughing, and the common accident: like walking, and they are called accidental universals; because they attach to the essence of what is beneath them and do not enter into it. And on this; the species is neither essential nor accidental; because it is the whole of the essence, and the whole of a thing is neither inside it nor outside it. (His saying: 'Because the universal in relation to what is beneath it... etc.') is an explanation of the basis of the enumeration; meaning: to what it is predicated; because its relation to what is disparate is not considered; for it is not one of the three divisions in relation to it. Then he restricted it to being from the particulars; whether they are partial or primary; meaning: in the state of its being a part of them or arising from them; to indicate that the considered relation is to a single particular; i.e., any particular; not to the totality of particulars; because that would invalidate the enumeration, since there would be four other divisions: that the three divisions combine in the universal two or three times, nor to a single specific particular; because then the divisions would become mutually exclusive, and their compatibility is considered, since he mentioned genus in the whole of the essence and its partiality. Rather, it is considered absolutely, so the divisions become different by consideration, as they explicitly stated concerning the permissibility of the five universals combining in one universal. Then the single particular cannot be meant as the real particular; otherwise it would exclude the higher and intermediate genera and differentia, and their properties and accidents measured to the essence that is intermediate or lower genera, the relative. And to indicate that, he expressed it by his saying: 'what is beneath it'. But there remains the rational measured to animal; for it is a property for it while it does not enter into the universal attributed to what is beneath it from its particulars, unless it is said: whatever something is predicated of it is a relative particular for it. Then it is apparent that the hypothetical universals are included in these three divisions. That is because the possibility of supposing their truth of many, considering the mere concept, necessitates the possibility of supposing the three divisions in them, even if none of them exists in actual fact. So what was said is repelled: that supposing their truth in actual fact is impossible, so it would necessitate the impossible, that there not be any of the three divisions. And that it is permissible to suppose their truth as the essence itself, or a part, or external in relation to one thing, so the truth of the five universals on them in relation to that thing would follow; because both the supposition and the supposed are impossible, since the intellect cannot allow them to be the essence itself, a part, and external in relation to one thing. And it is permissible that the hypothetical universals are excluded, and the relation is considered to what they are predicated of in actual fact, based on the non-attachment of the judgmental supposition to the states of hypothetical universals. And their inclusion in the definition follows the inclusion of the concept of the necessary (wajib) in it. And this is in accordance with what they said concerning the relations among the universals.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of the five universals (al-kulliyat al-khams). The author begins by stating that a universal, in relation to its particulars (the individuals under it), can be one of three things: (1) a part of the essence of those particulars — this includes genus (jins) and differentia (fasl); (2) the whole essence — this is species (naw'); (3) external to the essence — this includes property (khassa) and common accident ('arad 'amm). Thus, there are five universals. The commentary then clarifies each: if someone objects that 'rational substance' or 'sensitive substance' is a part but not genus or differentia, the reply is that we are dealing with simple terms, not compounds. The genus is like 'animal' when it is a part of the essence of its individuals; differentia is like 'rational' when it is a part of real individuals. Both are called 'essential universals' (kulli dhati) because they enter into the essence of what is beneath them as a part. Species is the whole essence, like 'human'. Property is like 'laughing', and common accident is like 'walking'; these are 'accidental universals' (kulli 'aradi) because they attach to the essence externally. The author then notes that species is neither essential nor accidental because it is the whole, which is neither inside nor outside the thing — it is the thing itself. Next, the explanation of the enumeration (al-hasr) is given: the relation is only to what the universal is predicated of, not to what is disparate. The restriction to 'particulars' means any particular, not the sum, else there would be additional divisions. Also, it is not a specific particular, else divisions would become mutually exclusive. The commentary addresses imaginary universals (al-kulliyat al-fardiyya) and their inclusion: they can be considered under the three divisions by supposition, even if not actual. The point about 'natiq' (rational) relative to 'hayawan' (animal) is raised: 'natiq' is a property of animal? Actually, it's a differentia for man but a property for animal? The commentary resolves by saying that what is predicated is a relative particular. Overall, this passage gives a thorough definition of the five universals and clarifies their relations to essence.

Checkpoint
According to the text, which two universals are part of the essence?
A) Genus and differentia
B) Species and property
C) Property and common accident
D) Genus and species

Key Takeaway
The five universals are classified as part, whole, or external to essence; this determines their essential or accidental nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the five universals the term 'rational' is in relation to the species 'human'. Justify your answer using the criteria from this lesson.
?
Classify 'walking' according to the five universals and explain why it is accidental.

The Five Universals
intermediate
6 min
Essential vs Accidental Universals and the Status of Species
Author explains essential/accidental distinction and the unique status of species

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential universals (dhati) from accidental universals ('aradi) and explain why species is neither.

Core Idea
Essential universals enter the essence; accidental universals attach from outside; species is the whole essence.

This lesson deepens the distinction between essential and accidental universals. The author emphasizes that genus and differentia are essential because they are part of the essence, while property and common accident are accidental. Species, as the whole essence, transcends this dichotomy.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three relational categories
Part (genus/differentia), whole (species), external (property/common accident).
2
Define essential universals
Any universal that enters the essence as a part is essential (kulli dhati). Examples: 'animal' for man (genus), 'rational' for man (differentia).
3
Define accidental universals
Any universal that is external to the essence is accidental (kulli 'aradi). Examples: 'laughing' (property), 'walking' (common accident).
4
Explain why species is neither
Species is the whole essence, so it cannot be a part (essential) nor external (accidental). It is the essence itself.

فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات. لأنَّ الكلّيَّ بالنّسبة إلى ما تحته من الأفرادِ إمَّا جزء من ماهية الأفرادِ وهو الجنس والفضلُ، أو تمامها وهو النَّوعُ ، أو خارج عنها وهو الخاصة والعَرَضُ العام، فالكليات خمس : (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ الْجِنْسُ) إِنْ قُلْتَ: يُرادُ الجوهرُ النَّاطِقُ أو الحسّاس؛ فإِنَّهُ جزءٌ وليس جنساً ولا فصلاً، وأجيب بأنَّ كلامنا في المفردِ لا المركَّب . وقوله : (وهو الجنس)؛ أي: إنْ كانَتِ الأفرادُ الَّتي تحته ـ وهو جزءٌ منها - حقائق كالحيوان. وقولُهُ: (والفصل)؛ أي: إنْ كانَتِ الأفرادُ الَّتى تحته ـ وهو جزء منها ـ أفراداً حقيقية كالنَّاطِقِ، وكلٌّ مِن هَذَيْنِ الكُلِّيَّيْن يُقال له: كُلّيّ ذاتي؛ لدخولِهِ في ماهية ما تحتَهُ مِنَ الذَّاتِ، ووقوعه جزءاً منها . (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ تَمَامِهَا) عطف على قوله : ((جزء) ؛ أي: أو تمام ماهية ما تحتَهُ مِنَ الأفرادِ كَالإنسان.
