(قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّ الكُلّيّ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِمَا تَحْتَهُ ... إِلَخ) بيان لوجهِ الحصر؛ أي: إلى ما يحملُ هو عليه؛ لأنَّ نِسبتَهُ إلى المباين غيرُ مُعتبرةٍ ، فإِنَّهُ بالنِّسبة إليهِ ليس شيئاً مِنَ عليهِ شيءٌ فهو جزئي إضافي له، ثمَّ الظَّاهِرُ أَنَّ الكُلِّيَّاتِ الفرضيَّةَ داخلة في هذه الأقسام الثَّلاثةِ، وذلك لأنَّ إمكانَ فرضِ صدقها على كثيرين ؛ نظراً إلى مجرَّدِ مفهوم يستدعي إمكانَ فرضِ الأقسام الثَّلاثةِ فيها، وإنْ لم يكن شيءٌ منها في نَفْسِ الأمرِ ، فاندفع ما قيلَ : إِنَّ فرضَ صدقها في نفس الأمر محال، فيجوز أن يستلزم المحالُ بأن لا يكون شيئاً مِنَ الأقسامِ الثَّلاثةِ، وأَنَّهُ يَجوزُ فرضُ صدقِهَا نَفْساً وجزءاً وخارجاً بالنسبة إلى أمرٍ واحدٍ فيلزم صدقُ الكُلِّيَّاتِ الخمسِ عليها بالنّسبة إلى ذلكَ الأمرِ ؛ لأنَّ الفرضَ والمفروض كلاهُمَا مُمْتَنِعَانِ، إِذْ لا يمكنُ للعقل تجويز كونها نَفْساً وجزءاً وخارجاً بالنسبة إلى أمرٍ واحد، ويجوز أن تخرج الكُلِّيَّاتُ الفرضيَّةُ، وتعتبرُ النِّسبةُ إلى ما يُحمَلُ عليهِ في نفس الأمرِ؛ بناءً على عدمِ تعلقِ الفرض الحكمي بأحوالِ الكُلِّيَّاتِ الفرضيَّةِ، ويكونُ إدخالها في التَّعريف تبعَ إدخال مفهوم الواجب فيه، وهذا على طبق ما قالُوا في النّسب بينَ الكُلِّيَّاتِ.
Section: On the foundations of concepts. Because the universal, in relation to its particulars, is either a part of the essence of the particulars — and that is genus and differentia, or the whole of it — and that is species, or external to it — and that is property and common accident. So the universals are five: (His saying: 'and it is the genus') If you say: What is meant is the rational or sensitive substance; for it is a part and is not genus or differentia, and it is answered that our discourse is about the simple, not the compound. And his saying: 'and it is the genus'; meaning: if the particulars beneath it — while it is a part of them — are real essences like animal. And his saying: 'and the differentia'; meaning: if the particulars beneath it — while it is a part of them — are real individuals like rational. And each of these two universals is called essential universal, because it enters into the essence of what is beneath it from the essence, and it occurs as a part of it. (His saying: 'or the whole of it') is conjoined to his saying 'part'; meaning: or the whole of the essence of what is beneath it from the particulars, like human. (His saying: 'or external to it') meaning: external to the essence of what is beneath it from the particulars, and his saying: 'and it is the property'; meaning: like laughing, and the common accident: like walking, and they are called accidental universals; because they attach to the essence of what is beneath them and do not enter into it. And on this; the species is neither essential nor accidental; because it is the whole of the essence, and the whole of a thing is neither inside it nor outside it. (His saying: 'Because the universal in relation to what is beneath it... etc.') is an explanation of the basis of the enumeration; meaning: to what it is predicated; because its relation to what is disparate is not considered; for it is not one of the three divisions in relation to it. Then he restricted it to being from the particulars; whether they are partial or primary; meaning: in the state of its being a part of them or arising from them; to indicate that the considered relation is to a single particular; i.e., any particular; not to the totality of particulars; because that would invalidate the enumeration, since there would be four other divisions: that the three divisions combine in the universal two or three times, nor to a single specific particular; because then the divisions would become mutually exclusive, and their compatibility is considered, since he mentioned genus in the whole of the essence and its partiality. Rather, it is considered absolutely, so the divisions become different by consideration, as they explicitly stated concerning the permissibility of the five universals combining in one universal. Then the single particular cannot be meant as the real particular; otherwise it would exclude the higher and intermediate genera and differentia, and their properties and accidents measured to the essence that is intermediate or lower genera, the relative. And to indicate that, he expressed it by his saying: 'what is beneath it'. But there remains the rational measured to animal; for it is a property for it while it does not enter into the universal attributed to what is beneath it from its particulars, unless it is said: whatever something is predicated of it is a relative particular for it. Then it is apparent that the hypothetical universals are included in these three divisions. That is because the possibility of supposing their truth of many, considering the mere concept, necessitates the possibility of supposing the three divisions in them, even if none of them exists in actual fact. So what was said is repelled: that supposing their truth in actual fact is impossible, so it would necessitate the impossible, that there not be any of the three divisions. And that it is permissible to suppose their truth as the essence itself, or a part, or external in relation to one thing, so the truth of the five universals on them in relation to that thing would follow; because both the supposition and the supposed are impossible, since the intellect cannot allow them to be the essence itself, a part, and external in relation to one thing. And it is permissible that the hypothetical universals are excluded, and the relation is considered to what they are predicated of in actual fact, based on the non-attachment of the judgmental supposition to the states of hypothetical universals. And their inclusion in the definition follows the inclusion of the concept of the necessary (wajib) in it. And this is in accordance with what they said concerning the relations among the universals.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of the five universals (al-kulliyat al-khams). The author begins by stating that a universal, in relation to its particulars (the individuals under it), can be one of three things: (1) a part of the essence of those particulars — this includes genus (jins) and differentia (fasl); (2) the whole essence — this is species (naw'); (3) external to the essence — this includes property (khassa) and common accident ('arad 'amm). Thus, there are five universals. The commentary then clarifies each: if someone objects that 'rational substance' or 'sensitive substance' is a part but not genus or differentia, the reply is that we are dealing with simple terms, not compounds. The genus is like 'animal' when it is a part of the essence of its individuals; differentia is like 'rational' when it is a part of real individuals. Both are called 'essential universals' (kulli dhati) because they enter into the essence of what is beneath them as a part. Species is the whole essence, like 'human'. Property is like 'laughing', and common accident is like 'walking'; these are 'accidental universals' (kulli 'aradi) because they attach to the essence externally. The author then notes that species is neither essential nor accidental because it is the whole, which is neither inside nor outside the thing — it is the thing itself. Next, the explanation of the enumeration (al-hasr) is given: the relation is only to what the universal is predicated of, not to what is disparate. The restriction to 'particulars' means any particular, not the sum, else there would be additional divisions. Also, it is not a specific particular, else divisions would become mutually exclusive. The commentary addresses imaginary universals (al-kulliyat al-fardiyya) and their inclusion: they can be considered under the three divisions by supposition, even if not actual. The point about 'natiq' (rational) relative to 'hayawan' (animal) is raised: 'natiq' is a property of animal? Actually, it's a differentia for man but a property for animal? The commentary resolves by saying that what is predicated is a relative particular. Overall, this passage gives a thorough definition of the five universals and clarifies their relations to essence.

The Five Universals
intermediate
8 min
The Threefold Division and Relative Particularity
Expansion on the condition of the threefold division and relative particularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition of the threefold division (relation to a single particular) and distinguish relative particulars (juz'i idafi) from real particulars.

Core Idea
The three divisions are relative to one particular, not to a set.

The commentator clarifies that the threefold division (essential, part, external) is defined in relation to a single particular—any particular—not to a set of particulars or a specific one. This prevents invalidating the division and ensures the divisions coincide. The concept of relative particularity is introduced: something predicated on another thing is a relative particular to that higher universal, e.g., 'rational' relative to 'animal'. Understanding this condition is crucial for correctly applying the five universals to definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the condition of 'being from the particulars'
In the passage, the commentator says 'then he restricted it to being from the particulars'. Find this phrase and understand that the threefold division is only considered when the universal is related to particulars.
The Arabic phrase is 'ثم قيد بكونه من الجزئيات'.
2
Understand why relation is not to a set
The commentator argues that if the relation were to a set of particulars, there would be four additional divisions (where the three divisions combine in the universal two or three times), which would invalidate the original division. Why would that happen? Because the set could include subgroups with different combinations.
This shows the logical need for a single particular.
3
Understand why not to a specific particular
If the relation were to a single particular specifically, the divisions would be disparate (they would not coincide), but the author intended them to coincide. So the relation must be to 'any particular'.
The commentator emphasizes that coincidence was considered.
4
Grasp the concept of relative particular (juz'i idafi)
The commentator explains that if something is predicated on something else, it becomes a relative particular to that higher universal. For example, 'rational' is predicated on 'animal', so 'rational' is a relative particular to 'animal'.
This is distinct from the real particular (juz'i haqiqi).
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
In usul al-fiqh, when defining 'qiyas' (analogical reasoning), the genus 'reasoning' and differentia 'from a specific source' would be relative particulars to the definition. This shows how the tool works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل الأقسام الثلاثة، ثم قيد بكونه من الجزئيات؛ سواء كانت من تبعيضية أو ابتدائية؛ أي: حال كونه بعضا منها أو ناشئا منها؛ للإشارة إلى أن المعتبر النسبة إلى جزئي واحد؛ أي جزئي كان؛ لا إلى مجموع الجزئيات؛ لأنه يبطل الحصر، إذ هنا أقسام أربعة أخر؛ هي أن تجتمع في الكلي تلك الأقسام الثلاثة ثناء أو ثلاث، ولا إلى جزئي واحد معين؛ لأنه حينئذ تصير الأقسام متباينة، وقد اعتبر تصادقها، حيث ذكر الجنس في تمام الماهية وجزئيتها، بل هو معتبر على إطلاقه، فتكون الأقسام متخالفة بالاعتبار على ما صرحوا به من جواز اجتماع الخمسة في كلي واحد، ثم الجزئي الواحد لا يجوز أن يراد به الحقيقي، وإلا أخرج الأجناس والفصول العالية والمتوسطة وخواصها وأعراضها مقيسة إلى الماهية التي هي أجناس متوسطة أو سافلة، الإضافي، وللإشارة إلى ذلك عبر عنه بقوله: ما تحته هذا؛ لكن يرد الناطق مقيسا إلى الحيوان؛ فإنه خاصة له مع عدم دخوله في الكلي المنسوب إلى ما تحته من جزئياته، إلا أن يقال: ما يحمل عليه شيء فهو جزئي إضافي له، ثم الظاهر أن الكليات الفرضية داخلة في هذه الأقسام الثلاثة، وذلك لأن إمكان فرض صدقها على كثيرين؛ نظرا إلى مجرد مفهوم يستدعي إمكان فرض الأقسام الثلاثة فيها، وإن لم يكن شيء منها في نفس الأمر، فاندفع ما قيل: إن فرض صدقها في نفس الأمر محال، فيجوز أن يستلزم المحال بأن لا يكون شيء من الأقسام الثلاثة، وأنه يجوز فرض صدقها نفسا وجزءا وخارجا بالنسبة إلى أمر واحد فيلزم صدق الكليات الخمس عليها بالنسبة إلى ذلك الأمر؛ لأن الفرض والمفروض كلاهما ممتنعان، إذ لا يمكن للعقل تجويز كونها نفسا وجزءا وخارجا بالنسبة إلى أمر واحد، ويجوز أن تخرج الكليات الفرضية، وتعتبر النسبة إلى ما يحمل عليه في نفس الأمر؛ بناء على عدم تعلق الفرض الحكمي بأحوال الكليات الفرضية، ويكون إدخالها في التعريف تبع إدخال مفهوم الواجب فيه، وهذا على طبق ما قالوا في النسب بين الكليات؛ فإن بعضهم
Rather, the three divisions, then he restricted it to being from the particulars; whether they are from partitioning or original; i.e., being a part of them or arising from them; to indicate that what is considered is the relation to a single particular, any particular whatsoever; not to the set of particulars, because then the division would be invalid, for here there are four other divisions; that those three divisions combine in the universal two or three times, nor to a single particular specifically, because then the divisions would be disparate, whereas their coincidence was considered, where genus was mentioned in the entirety of the quiddity and its partiality, but rather it is considered absolutely, so the divisions differ by consideration as they explicitly stated about the permissibility of the five coming together in one universal. And to indicate that he expressed it by his statement: 'what is under this'; but the objection arises concerning 'rational' measured to 'animal', for it is a property of it without being included in the universal related to what is under it of its particulars, unless it is said: whatever something is predicated on is a relative particular to it. Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the definition of the threefold division of universals (genus, species, differentia, property, accident) and the condition of being 'from the particulars'. The commentator explains that the three divisions (being essential, part of the quiddity, and external) are considered relative to a single particular, not to a set of particulars or a specific particular. He defends this by showing that if it were to a set, there would be four additional divisions (combinations), and if to a specific particular, the divisions would be separate whereas they are supposed to coincide. He then addresses the issue of relative particulars (juz'i idafi) and hypothetical universals (kulliyyat fardiyya). He argues that hypothetical universals, even if they cannot actually be true of many, can be assumed to be true, and thus they are included in the three divisions. He refutes an objection that assuming them true in reality is impossible, explaining that the assumption itself is impossible, but that does not prevent them from being considered under the three divisions. He concludes by noting that the inclusion of hypothetical universals in the definition follows the pattern of including the concept of the necessary (wajib) in definitions. This passage uses examples like 'animal' as a genus and 'rational' as a property relative to 'animal' to illustrate relative particularity.

كلي فرضي

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is the relation not to a set of particulars when defining the threefold division?
A) Because it would invalidate the division, producing four additional divisions.
B) Because the set of particulars is infinite.
C) Because the three divisions require a single particular.
D) Because the definition must be simple.

Key Takeaway
The threefold division of universals is always relative to a single particular, not to a set or a specific one, and relative particulars are terms predicated on higher universals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
